A LARGE BONUS.
The Company has recently forwarded to the holder of policy No, 278,127 a cheque for
£1,099 13s. 6d. in payment of the cash va ue of the Bonus for 1891, the
Policy being for 10,000, and issued in 1886. on the five year distribution plan. This
return is equal to an annual cash bonus of £2 4s. for each £lOO insured. Many of
these policies are reaching the bonus period with results very gratifying to the Insured.
The total payments to Polioy-lrolders to December, 1890, amounted to £63,469,822,
of which upwards of £17,400,000 were bonus .payments—more than twice the amount of
Bonuses paid by any other Company.

Dr. Perry’s Pamphlet on Nervous Diseases s
Their ause, Symptoms, and Treatment.
This pamphlet is specially written to show a method of
counteracting the ill effects of indiscretions in early life,
over-work, mental worry, &c., and contains a fund of use-
ful information for every sufferer from the following com-
plaints, viz Loss of Energy and Will Power, NERVOUS
DEBILITY, Pains in the Back, Defective Memory, Specks
before the Eyes, Excessive Nervousness, Premature
Decay, Bashfulness, Excessive Heart Palpitation, -Des-
pondency, Impure Blood, Liver and Kidney Complaints,
Contagious Diseases, and various other symptoms experi-
enced by Nervous Sufferers.
This Pamphlet is sent Free on Application,
and all in any way suffering from the above would derive
benefit from the perusal of this work.
If the patient’s case is of long standing, and, therefore
in need of treatment, he should lose no time in writing
me a letter detailing in full his symptoms, on receipt of
which I will at once reply, explaining the nature of his
complaint, and if I can effect a cure, the time it will take,
and the cost of doing so. My long experience in the
treatment of Nervous Diseases has made me thoroughly
conversant with all their peculiarities. I am prepared,
therefore, to
Guarantee a Permanent and Complete tare ia every case 1 undertake.
I invite the fullest investigation into my qualifications. For some time I was House Surgeon to the Great
Northern Hospital, London, where I had special opportunities of investigating the causes of Nervous Diseases, and
bee une perfectly acquainted with the most approved scientific and successful methods of affecting their cure.

This eminent Specialist, Founder, and Chief

PRIVATE, NERVOUS, AND SKIN
DISEASES.
He has by profound research thoroughly investi-
gated their origin, traced their deadly sources and
insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest

cated, with these widely recognised qualifications
combined with unswerving faithfulness to hit
patients, he has during his practice, extending
over FORTY YEARS, attained a well-deserved
pre-eminence in their treatment. His practice
embraces diseases which probably cause more un-
happiness than any other class of troubles that
afflict mankind.

SATURDAY, FEBRUARY 27, 1892.
An Incurable Constitution.
True to its inherited instincts and the spirit
of its allotments and its bank-balance, the
Legislative Council of New South Wales has
rejected the measure for the abolition of
plural voting, and the great democratic
scheme has got back practically to the point
at which it started. There was a large—a
wonderfully large—gathering of the vene-
rable nominees to assert the privileges of
Property on this occasion, and out of 59
members who either voted or paired on the
second reading, only 27 could be found to
support the cause of progress, leaving the
moneybag element in a majority of five.
Most of the 27, too, only supported the
second reading with a view to abolishing
the One-man-one-vote principle in Com-
mittee ; at least twenty of them had drivelled
off and on for months about the necessity of
granting a special vote for “ Thrift”— which,
under the circumstances, practically meant
“Theft”—and the democratic element in
the Upper House of New South Wales may
be estimated as consisting of seven members,
five of whom are doubtful, and gone either
in the head or the knees. With such a
Legislature the future prospects of the
democratic programme are exceedingly un-
certain, and yet, by the insane muddlement
of the New South Wales Constitution, it is
practically master of the situation. There
is no lawful or constitutional mode of over-
riding its decision. It may possibly be scared
into submission, seeing that it is very old
and senile and toothless ; but the country
has no available weapon to employ against
it, and can only treat it after the manner of
the Chinaman who bangs his empty drum to
frighten away devils—if the devil sees fit to
stay in spite of the drum then there is
nothing more to be said. New South Wales
sold its soul in the days gone by to the 70
potentates of the Upper House ; it is
weighed down by a bald and spectacled
nightmare, and if that Upper House chooses
to remain firm there is no remedy for it
short of insurrection or homicide. In this
respect the province does not stand alone ;
Aqstralasia is burdened with several more
Chambers of the same kind, all equally
useless, bull-headed, and impracticable ;
and therefore the situation is an interesting
one. The Councils of Victoria, New South
Wales, Queensland, and Maoriland have all
systematically blocked progressive legisla-
tion, and in ail these provinces ominous
threats have arisen over and over again that
the Upper House must either be mended or
ended. So far, however, nobody has dis-
covered any method of carrying out the
threat. Premier Dibbs now proposes, as
many other Premiers have done, to start a
war against the nominees who stand in the
road, and he will probably find that the con-
tract is a large one.
* *
The first proceeding on the Government
programme is to call a new session of Parlia-
ment, to pass the One-man-one-vote Bill
again through the Assembly, and to present
it once more to the stagnant old obstruc-
tionists who block the way. There is no
probability that these obstructionists will be
any less old or any less stagnant then than
they are now, but it is necessary to allow
them an opportunity of reconsidering the
position. When they have rejected the
measure a second time, a wild, sad cry
will go up to Heaven from the Foreign-trade
press demanding an immediate dissolution
and an appeal to the country. The Council,
for want of a better excuse, professes to
object to the scheme “because the people
have not been consulted,” and therefore the
Foreign-trade papers will call for a general
election by way of consulting the people and
letting “ the traitor ” Parkes have another
chance to get into office. The Council’s
alleged reason, however, as both it and the
shoddy editors are well aware, is lying—it
would really be a lie to use any other word—
pure and unadulterated. The last general
election was fought on this question above
all others, and every single member of the
Lower House, whether Foreign-trade, Pro-
tectionist, Labour, Independent, or non-
descript, was returned to support the
principle of manhood suffrage without any
reservation whatever. And not only that,
but among the defeated candidates only one,
ex-Treasurer Burns, ventured to declare
himself a supporter of the present system ;
and despite the powerful support of Parkes,
and the fact that he stood in a district where
he had spent the country’s money like water
under circumstances the memory of which is
still green, and likely to remain so, Burns
was defeated ignominiously. Never since it
first existed has New South Wales been
so unanimous on any subject, and if
the Council and the Foreign-trade press
are not content with the result, and still
assert that the country “has not been
consulted,” the only answer is that the
country cannot possibly be consulted any
more or any harder than it already has been.
The nation returned a whole Assembly all
pledged to this issue in the most uncom-
promising terms, and if forty general elec-
tions were held in one year, it could do
nothing more definite than that—unless,
indeed, it hanged a Legislative Councillor on
a lamp-post as an expression of opinion.
» # #
Under these conditions there are only
three possible alternatives. One is to flood
the Upper House with a sufficient num-
ber of new men to make a democratic
majority ; another, to utterly abolish
that gathering of cracked emperors as a
curse and a hopeless‘anomaly ; and the
third, as already mentioned, to trade on the
age and the imbecility of the House of
Patriarchs, and by some means or other to
scare the inmates of that asylum into sub-
mission. The latter device, however, is
open to one serious objection—there is no
reason why the patriarchs should scare
unless they wish to. The nation is not
likely to rush their premises with clubs and
nooses and bricks, and so long as it abstains
from such extreme measures, the Upper
House can block the way with absolute
impunity till it dies of old age. Therefore,
there remain practically only two devices to
choose from—both unpromising,
* # *
To turn the Council into a democratic
body, or one sufficiently democratic to be
reasonably in touch with the sentiment of
the country, it would be necessary to send
to that Chamber a contingent of, at least,
fifty new members. The division on the
Electoral Reform Bill proved the Council to
consist at present of thirty-two utterly
incurable bigots, one dead member, about
eight legislators who are too blind and bed-
ridden to attend, and twenty-seven alleged
Liberals, at least twenty of whom want “a
vote for Thrift ” and have all the other signs
of rampant Toryism—the name only ex-
cepted. It would take, therefore, fully
fifty new members to create anything even
distantly resembling a democratic majority,
and the Government which proposed such
wholesale wreckage of the Constitution
would be promptly blocked by the Governor
or the Queen, both of whom are as irremove-
able and irresponsible and as Tory as the
Upper House itself. But even granting that
the nominations could be made, which is
practically impossible except by violence,
the difficulty would only be removed a step
further. The average democrat deteriorates
fast in the atmosphere of the Council,
an I three months’ experience would pro-
bably convert at least two-thirds of the new
members into advanced Conservatives. No
man can be trusted with irresponsible power,
and no democrat ever has remained, or ever
will remain, a democrat when placed in a
position of authority from which nothing
short of death can remove him. The
number of liberal-minded men who have
been sent to the Upper Houses is legion ;
the number whose liberalism has survived
their elevation for any great length of time
could be counted on one finger. Therefore
if the Dibbs Government sent fifty reliable-
looking Liberals to the Council at the pre-
sent crisis, it is quite probable that, by the
time the Electoral Reform Bill again reached
that institution, so many of them would
have fallen awav from their faith as to
leave the Conservative majority almost as
strong as it is to-day. Moreover, the ex-
pedient is only a temporary one at the best.
It would raise the Upper House to 120 mem-
bers, and by the time the Land Nationalisa-
tion Bill came on at some period of the
future the spirit of Property would have so
permeated the entire body that it would
require 120 more nominees to swamp the
previous contingent; and at every change of
policy or public feeling the process would
have to be repeated ad infinitum. Thus in
course of time there would be 20,000 poli-
ticians in the Council—a prospect which is
too dreadful to contemplate,
# * #
The entire abolition of the nominee House
is, undoubtedly, the logical remedy for those
perpetually recurring difficulties, but un-
happily the nominee House cannot be legally
removed unless it abolishes itself, which it
is by no means likely to do as at present
constituted. No doubt, if fifty or sixty new
members were created for the sole purpose
of passing a Bill for the extinction of the
present system, and the nominee Governor
would sanction the creation of these mem-
bers, and the irremovable and irresponsible
British monarch would permit him to do so,
and the new members would all adhere to
their pledges when they were placed in a
position where they could break these pledarcs
with complete impunity, and the Governor
did not veto the Bill when it was passed,
and the Queen did not withhold her
consent, the required result might one
day be arrived at, but among all these
dangers the prospects of ultimate success
are limited. The nominee Council, the
nominee Governor, and the irresponsible
British sovereign are three obstructions
which cannot be removed by any lawful or
constitutional means; when the country finds
that it is necessary for its progress that it
should get rid of them, the only thing it is
legally empowered to do is to ask them to
commit suicide, which will assuredly prove
an unprofitable request. The representative
system in, at least, five out of the seven
provinces of Australasia is a lamentable
farce. Of the four influences which make
up the governing power of the country, one
—the Assembly—-is under the control of the
nation ; another—-the Council—can some-
times be bounced into submission and some-
times not ; while the Governor, in a really
serious case, can have his salary stopped,
and, should that fail to move him, can be shot
at or hooted or have his windows broken ;
and the monarch cannot be reached at all
and can only be made the target of strong
language in the newspapers. Meanwhile,
among all these obstacles the nation
scrambles along in a crab-like fashion
and trusts to Providence. Hitherto it has
dealt chiefly with small reforms, and some-
times it has howled down the Upper House,
and sometimes it has come to a dead-lock
with the Governor, and on other occasions it
has even remonstrated with the Crown, some-
tunes with success and frequently without it.
Now, however, it has a matter on hand
which may rouse the Conservative elements
of the State to active resistance, and if the
three irresponsible sections of the Govern-
inent should resolve to stand firm there
is absolutely no remedy save revolu-
tion. The one fatal defect about almost
all political systems is that they
contain no efficient legal provision for
their own abolition when the country has
got tired of them. They are all based on
the assumption that they will last for ever,
and yet each one has to be shifted in course
of time to make way for something new.
Then, if the original constitution-makers
have chained the State down too tightly,
something must be damaged when the old
institutions come to be torn up. The
superstition about the necessity for a “stable
form of Government” is responsible for
innumerable evils ; the trouble with most
Governments is that they are altogether too
stable. That of New South Wales is a case
in point ; it has been made secure by the
simple process of piling dead weight upon it,
after the fashion of pig-iron on a safety-
valve, and the more pig-iron, the worse will
be the explosion in the end.
The Civil Servant and the State.
Early in the beginning of the month, New
South .Wales Legislator M ‘Court moved that
Civil servants and all men employed under
Government should be paid their salaries every
14 days, instead of monthly as at present.
Thereupon Premier Dibbs and Lawyer Crick
felt called upon to make remarks derogatory to
the dignity of the Public Service, and injurious
to the character of the men whose experience,
attainments and administrative abilities have
hitherto somewhat safeguarded the community
from the consequences of Ministerial blundering
and legislative ineptitude. Mr. Crick sup-
posed that Civil servants would not object to
being paid two months’ salary for one month’s
work. As Mr. Crick —in common with every
other member of the New South Wales Legisla-
tive Assembly—is now a paid Civil servant, at
a salary of £3OO a year, we presume this to be
a personal explanation. He further remarked
that the life of a Minister must be becoming a
torture to him by reason of the number of
applications for employment in the Civil Service
" billy 50 per cent, of the population being
engaged in efforts to obtain Government billets.
We are interested to know whether Mr. Crick
includes in this estimate those of the community
desirous of entering the Government service as
members of the New South Wales Parliament,
for even the Minister whose fate is so feelingly
commiserated is a Civil servant—and frequently
an incompetent one that. Mr. Dibbs stated
that he had ten applications for Govern
billets in one day, and Minister Lyne de 9 nt
that he had an average of 50 p'er diem °
Crick advocated making the Civil Seiv- r ‘
unattractive that few would be anxious to * C ° S °
it, “ and live without soiling their hands ”° nter
* * #
Evidently the average member of Parliam
is thoroughly ignorant of what a Civil S( L. ent
should be, or of the nature of the work 1
obliged to perform. The Civil servant « 18
earn his living “without soiling his hand' »
but we fail to see any reproach in this if t
earns it efficiently by the exercise of his hr,; 16
Mr. Crick, as a lawyer and a paid legist
also earns his living “ without soiling i ’
hands,” but he would not on that acco US
refuse a legal fee, or remit to the p-.-m? 1
Treasury the £3OO per annum which his counV
pays him to occupy a seat in the Legist.,: ''’
Assembly Chamber, in fact, the whole mießtffi P
is obscured and muddled by misconception ’mi
the service is itself degraded by
patronage. The fact alone that Ministers hive
the power of making appointments to the Put];
Service is an evil. The whole administration
of the departments should be placed under tli
control of a commission, and no appointment
whatever should be made save from the rank
of persons who had satisfied the requirements
of a severe examination. As evidence of the
ludicrous light in which members of Parliament
ate all too prone to regard the Civil Service of
the country wc would refer to the report of the
debate in the New South Wales Parliament i n
committee of supply in review of the estimates
on February 17, when Queanbeyan O’Sullivan
morbidly anxious to reap a cheap notoriety bv
raising a cry for retrenchment, took occasion to
waste a deal of eloquence on the subject of the
salary of the chief Under-Secretary, Mr
Critchett Walker. Two years ago the
annual remuneration of this gentleman
was £960, which sum was subsequently
raised to £llOO, and Mr. E. w.
O’Sullivan was, in his newly-born enthusiasm
for retrenchment, desirous of having the
amount reduced to the old figure. It never
struck the “ keystone of the democratic arch ”
that not only Mr. Critchett Walker, but
every other under-secretary, is really and truly
the permanent minister of the department he
administers, and that, from any practical point
of view, or any point of view whatever having
regard to the interests of the community, it
would be far wiser and safer to reduce the
salaries of those designated Ministers in the
ostensible government of the country.
* * #
Mr. O’Sullivan hates the word “socialism”
worse than an Orangeman hates holy-water,
and, apparently, any approach to it by the
State enrages him to the utmost. Now, the
Civil Service is the thin end of the wedge of
State-socialism, and with the gradual growth
of the Service, and its absorption of various
industries and associations, now promoted by
private enterprise, beneath Government control,
the ideals of economists like Louis Blanc will
gradually be brought about. But this cannot
be done by making it a reproach and a by-word
to be a servant of the State. And it is inter-
esting to note that in defending Mr. Critchett
Walker against the attacks of the O’Quean-
beyan, Mr. Dibbs forgot his sarcastic references
to the Civil Service of a week before. On the
contrary, he paid a fine compliment to one
who had faithfully and ably served the country
for 34 years, who had climbed from grade to
grade until he had obtained the blue-ribbon
of the service, and whose whole time
was practically at the disposal of the
State, whose Ministers he had to edu-
cate and assist in the discharge of their
functions. The Premier made a capital hit in
reply to a question of some honourable donkey’s
as to why, if Mr. Critchett Walker were
overworked, two men were not employed to
fulfil his duties. Mr. Dibbs characterised the
remark as nonsense. The Principal Under-
secretary was entrusted with the most sacred
secrets of the Government, and received the
entire confidence of whatever party was in
power. The most delicate matters of State
passed through his office, and it was impos-
sible to divide such responsibility between two
officers. Mr. Dibbs might have added that it
would be just as reasonable to conduct the
Government of the country with two Premiers,
or two Ministers, or for two Ckmmidnders-in-
Chief to lead an army to victory. Regarding
the matter from a purely commercial stand-
point, the Principal Under-Secretary is under-
paid, rather than-over-paid. The sum of £llOO
per annum is not a great salary if we consider
the prizes offered in the professions, in,trade,
and in finance. The Principal Under-Secretary
is as much a specialist as*a doctor, or a lawyer,
or a clergyman, and it is a recognised principle
in political economy that the services of the
specialist, if wanted, must be paid for.
* # *
The Civil Service of any country, instead of
being rendered unattractive, as Mr. Crick sug-
gests, should be made the most alluring of
any service in that country. The State
should employ the best men. To achieve
this the Civil Service must be honoured
and honourable. The prizes offered must be
worth the getting. The salaries paid must
be over rather than under the ruling rates. Mr.
Dibbs and Mr. Lyne complained that they were
pestered by applicants for Government billets.
Cut down salaries and they would be pestered
still more ; the worst men and , the least com-
petent would crowd into the different depart-
ments, for the most capable would prefer
competing for the prizes of life in the open
market. As a matter of principle the State
should strive to become the ideal employer,
should endeavour to set an example to all out-
side employers whatever. But the State can
never occupy this enviable position while billets
remain in the gift of party, while the poli-
tician’s letter of recommendation is accepted as
a charter of competence. Every civil servant
should be compelled to pass a r
tion, and receive his appointment direct from a
commission responsible to no political clique.
How is it possible to maintain a high standard
in the public service while politicians are over-
crowding the offices with relatives and Hangers-
on whose only recommendation, in nine cases


. , is their total unfitness for any walk
out 0t life ? If the Civil Service is corrupt,
°f prac * o nly themselves to thank for it.
politick Government Printing-office the
E veU a itol . w ho wants work is compelled to
comp o ® letter of introduction from some
prcsem Parliament. The recent PaHkes

appom yixibition Commission are saturated

Wlt runble for billets Ministers’ offices were
tllC with placemen and parasites begging
ff something, more or less vaguely defined
would include a good “screw and
wll L nnd a trip to the world’s fair.
P erkS *
nf course, in a few isolated cases, where the
;- ceS of’a district specialist were required,
ordinary routine of appointment to Civil
« ,-vice offices above suggested would neces-
.. pc departed from. But even then it
Sa Id be preferable for the State to train its
lists rather than import them, and the
trulv valuable man is generally the officer who
has accumulated his experience through a long
nrse of years, and whose knowledge of the
inurements of his department has become a
riortion of his being. At all events the degra-
S, t j oll of the Public Service is in the main
attributable to the “ spoils ” system, and the
1 a bit each successive Government has acquired
r re quiting political services to themselves at
the expense of the nation. No good can, bow-
er be achieved by belittling the dignity of the
bile servant, or by the Government entering
into competition with outside institutions for
the best labour at the lowest wage : and the
sincerity of the Parliamentary apostle of
retrenchment will ever be in question who does
not include his own salary in a proposal for
” o rata reduction. Had Mr. E. W. O’Sulli-
van suggested an all-round pruning down of 10
or 15 cent., instead of taking specialised
cases his earnestness for reform might not have
been called in question, nor his position at the
present moment so supremely unenviable and
contemptibly ludicrous.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Samuel Griffith, Nigger-Driver.
The week before last Premier Griffith, of
Queensland, announced his conversion to the
black labour and nigger-driving interest, and
solemnly retracted the eloquence of a life-time
about the necessity for preserving Australia for
the Australians. Last week he rashly gave his
reasons for thus swallowing his alleged prin-
ciples. A deputation interviewed him at Rock-
hampton to protest against the raising of a new
nigger difficulty and the resumption of the
blood-stained slave-trade in Polynesia, and the
result shows that no politician of Griffith’s
calibre should ever explain anything, for when
he does so the results are disastrous
The Premier said that lie had formed his opinions with
the greatest reluctance and entirely owing to the way he
had been treated by those.for whom lie had laboured for
years. When everything he did was denounced by the
working class, it was his duty to consider the whole com-
munity. The deputation specially asked that the exten-
sion of the term for the employment of Kanakas should
not be granted without the country being first consulted,
but the Premier declined to give any assurance on that
point, or to disclose the intentions cf the Government.
Sir Samuel said he was certain from personal observation
that sugar could best be grown by small farmers, though
admittedly in some cases with the assistance of black
labour.
Nobody ever made a more fatal explanation
than this. Griffith admits that he lias become
an advocate of nigger-labour “entirely owing
to the way be lias been treated by those for
whom he has laboured for years.” In other
words, the democratic party in Queensland
isn’t sufficiently grateful to Griffith, and be
introduces the Kanaka by way of a fiendish
vengeance on the men who have found him out t
If the working classes bad worshipped Griffith
with sufficient abjedtness, he wouldn’t have
introduced the Kanaka. Therefore, there
remain just two alternatives : either the
nigger is necessary for the country’s pros-
perity, arid yet Griffith would have let
the country’s prosperity go to wreck so
long as the working men of the southern
districts lioorayed for him and voted for him,
and let off fireworks in lus honour—in other
words, he would have sold the nation for a
banner and a bonfire ; or else the nigger is a
curse and Griffith is deliberately bringing in
that curse and invoking ruin on the land in
order to make his small soul feel better, and to
get level with tire followers who have abandoned
him. Furthermore, he states that “ when
everything he did was denounced by the work-
ing classes it was his duty to consider the
whole community,” which surely means that if
the working classes hadn’t denounced him, but
had grovelled before him instead, lie wouldn't
have considered the whole community ; he would
only have considered the grovellers. Griffith
laid his whole miserable policy hare in one act to
that Rockhampton deputation ; lus one and
only political motive is a devouring vanity, and
to gratify it he would sacrifice his country,
his money, his friends, his principles, and his
prospects of heaven, if he has any. His career
has been one miserable scramble after notoriety
from first to last, and he doesn’t even know
how to scramble effectively. He never did
anything for the masses except to blow off
loud-mouthed sentiments and platitudes, and
now he goes back upon them because they are
not sufficiently thankful for the platitudes.
He wrote revolutionary essays in the Boom-
erang ; and he introduced an Eight-hour Bill
“of a permissive character,” which provided
that everybody ought to work only eight hours,
but if he had "to work sixteen it was a lament-
able circumstance, and the Government
couldn’t help it ; and he invented the Italian
cheap-labour scheme ; and he helped to flood
the country with the scum of the English work-
houses and gaols, imported at the expense of
the oppressed tax-payer; and he established the
most extraordinary system on record of faking
the electoral rolls in the interests of Property.
And now we are given to understand that
because the working men of Queensland are not
sufficiently grateful for these things, Griffith
has re-established the infamous Polynesian
slave-trade in these last days of his moral
decadence. Yet, despite all these things, Sir
Samuel’s socialistic sentiments are as fresh as
ever. There is no visible connection between
his statements and his actions; he is a Jew at
night and a hog in the morning, but he never
mixes the characters. It has long been a ques-
tion in Queensland which is the real Griffith
—the one who snutiles over the poor, or the one
that grovels to the rich ? Now it is beginning
to be apparent that one of them is no more real
than the other ; they are both transient and
bogus ; there is no genuine Griffith at all—
only two shadows, and Samuel will go back on
one shadow as soon as the other when his
vanity is concerned.
Tlie Murdered Kanaka.
Recent advices from Queensland cast a new
and still more unfavourable light on Premier
Griffith’s coloured labour policy. When that
apostate democrat proposed to introduce the
Italian, with Ids stiletto and his strange
assorted diseases and his dirt and his vast and
wonderful ignorance, he drew a moving picture
of the ruin and utter desolation that overspread
the North, and which could only be alleviated
by the wholesale importation of cheap labourers.
When, later on, he proposed the re-introduction
of the once-abhorred Kanaka he grew still more
pathetic about the deserted mills and the
abandoned fields and the towns that were fall-
ing into ruins, and almost shed tears over the
slime and the alligators and the pestilence of
tropical Australia. The one reason why the
Kanaka was to be re-established as a permanent
curse in the tropics was that the white man
could not work, but could only die, among
these awful surroundings, while the Polynesian
flourished and was happy in the sweltering
mud-banks and the swamps. And now,
strangely enough, it is emphasised that the
Kanaka—that ideal toiler of the tropics—dies
off just about three times as fast on the sugar-
plantations as does the white workman
similarly employed. The ancient story about his
capacity for flourishing in a malarious land and
under a blazing sun is lying pure and unadul-
terated. His fitness for the work consists
simply in the fact that he dies peaceably and
no one makes a row over his remains—we have
known 80 to die right away of dysentery within
a month on one plantation —and he can’t write
to the papei’S or appeal to the law because he
knows no English, and he doesn’t go on strike
to any extent, and he can be driven to work
even when he is dying, and when he is dead he
can be buried anywhere, and his decease
attracts so little attention that the local editor
will sit over his grave and write articles about
the way the happy Kanaka prospers and grows
fat in the canefields, and never realise the
awful incongruousness of the situation. The
islander’s only qualification for his gladsome
billet is that he is cheap and helpless, and the
oft-repeated assertion that he is employed
because Nature has evidently built him for the
job is utter, irredeemable perjury—he is the
one man above all others, as evidenced by the
hard logic of statistics, that Nature didn’t build
for any such purpose. And further, the same
statistics demolish in one act the dreary picture of
the utter ruin and disaster that are alleged to
have overtaken the sugar industry of Queensland.
The output of sugar in that province is in-
creasing every year, and the yield for the last
12 months is the largest on record. The disaster
that is always being foretold has never come,
and there is no sign of it coming. A few
planters who bought their properties at boom
prices have suffered heavily since the borrowed
money is gone, and the boom is over, and the
swamps have gone down once more to their old
valuation, but" the sugar industry itself is no
more responsible for the falling of land-values
on the Herbert River than it is for a similar
fall beside the Yarra. The whole theory of
nigger-labour is based on two atrocious false-
hoods. There is less need for the Kanaka than
ever there was, and at all times his employment
has been thinly-disguised murder —neither more
nor less.

The Tall of Australia.
Tiie official Libei’al organ, the London Daily
News — apropos of Australia’s want of enthu-
siasm over Booth’s army of diseased criminals,
rowdies, sweated blacklegs and hysterical
fanatics —thus composes the epitaph of Aus-
tralia, and prints “ Ichabod ” in large letters
over the gates of this continent: —
If Australia refuses to allow the admission of emigrants,
her brief record of progress will be practically closed.
This statement is a wonderful sample of the
arrogance of Bull —that inflated fat man whose
stomach overshadows the whole earth. Aus-
tralia, by the News’ theory, cannot progress or
form a nation except by means of British immi-
grants. It can grow its own population in the
course of years, and it can gather in Germans,
Yankees, Scandinavians, Russians, and other
sturdy elements, and absorb them to increase
its numbers and win “ tugs-of-war,” but unless
it takes the human refuse of the London slums,
its “record” will close up. The waste, igno-
rant and diseased humanity which England
wants to get rid of at any price, is still so
much better than any material that can be
locally grown that it is this country’s only hope.
Should this be true it occurs to us that Aus-
tralia might possibly do a lot better without
any hope at all. If the “ brief record of pro-
gress ” must shut up unless helped along by
Booth’s paupers then it had better shut. Aus-
tralia has progressed pretty well in the past
without Booth ; if it can’t get along without
him in the future then it will perish respectably
and be buried decently under the wattle. There
are some kinds of prosperity that are too
horrible to think of, and the kind Daily
News alludes to is one of them. Jo be a
rich man with a Salvation drum always banging
before your window, and a haggard, spindle-
shanked dwarf always trying to sell you a
War Cry, and a pallid female in burst boots
always appealing to you to drop a penny in the
tambourine, and with an eternal red-shirted
procession headed by an apostle with a red nose
always tramping —Tramping —tramping down
the street is not a prospect worth living for. The
“ record of progress ” call close if it wants to —
we aren’t particular. This is a country where
we can go naked, and sleep on the hillsides, and
Uve on rabbit and native bear in comparative
comfort, and the coming ruin of the Australian
nation really doesn’t matter.
Ik Premier Hums wishes to be a benefactor to
his country, let him pass an enactment pro-
viding that every British and other foreign
lunatic, criminal, or diseased person introduced
into New South Wales shall be promptly sent
back to the country from which he came, at the
expense of the shipping company which fetched
him. At the present time there are 3659
officially - recognised lunatics in New South
Wales, and no less than 2014 of these are
imported cranks from Great Britain, while only
1121 were born in the province. The native-
born population is nearly three times as great
as that of British origin, therefore, Britain
supplies something more than five times
its fair average of lunatics. If the im-
ported Englishman could be utterly abol-
ished, New South Wales upon these
figures would, have a smaller proportion of
insane than any civilised country on earth,
barring Austria, Portugal, and Spain. All the
other great provinces of Australasia have now
closed their doors against the horde of madmen
whom Britain sends out here and turns loose in
order that they may be a burden on any
country save her own, but in New South Wales
any kind of howling idiot can find free
admittance. British cranks who are stopped
at other points are therefore sent on to
Sydney, sometimes to be shifted straight
into an asylum, at other times to marry
and rear deranged families, and ultimately
beat their wives' brains out With the poker
or the flat-iron, and thus supply refresh-
ing “copy” for the evening papers, which
report the gory deed in three columns of
blood-gurgle and massacre. This sort of thing
is growing stale. New South Wales dislikes
the British lunatic, and dislikes still more the
British globe-trotter who comes out here and
inspects that lunatic, and then goes home to
state that the Australians are evidently a
degraded race, whose brains have been under-
mined by drink and all manner of horrors.

There is something thrilling and dramatic in
the Moss time-payment furniture episode which
is being gradually unrolled in Melbourne. The
story of Mrs. Moss’s time-payment furniture
began away back in 1882. In that disastrous
year she took a house in Fitzroy, and obtained
furniture from one Rosenthal on credit to the
amount of £1545. She paid a considerable sum,
apparently about £lOOO, on account of this
property, the amounts paid being described
as “ rent.” Then Rosenthal went insolvent,
and when he was gone his trustee promptly
seized Mrs. Moss’s sticks and sold them oft.
Why he did this is not explained, but
as the sticks only realised £159 75., or
one-tenth of the purchase-money, it would
seem as if the collapsed Rosenthal had taken
a considerable “rise” out of his customer.
Presently, however, the burst furniture-man’s
wife took up his business, and let Mrs. Moss a
house at £2 10s. a week. She also sold her
£4OO worth more furniture. Then the rent of
the house was raised to £4 10s., and shortly
afterwards it went up again to £6. Mrs. Moss
paid the £6 a week, and also paid for the bed-
steads and other things, but when the whole
£4OO tv as liquidated, she was floored by a
demand for £ll9 more, and on top of this came
another demand for £413, being the balance
due on the first lot of goods which the miserable
Moss had already paid so much for and lost.
At this stage the latter lady assigned her estate,
being willing to sacrifice the second lot of tables
and chairs, "provided the eternal bill was only
wiped out for good and all, but the Rosenthal
started ejectment proceedings, and Mrs. Moss pre-
sently knocked underand signed a new agreement
for £605 for more furniture. Meanwhile that
distracted person had got into further trouble
with a money-lender who lent her £l7O in 1887,
presumably ” to pay Rosenthal with, and
received back £1963 in interest, and who still
wants £515, mostly for “ goods delivered.”
The goods consisted of still another batch of
time-payment furniture —in fact, Moss seems to
have been a sort of sepulchre for furniture, and
the bill is made up chiefly of such items as
“window curtain, £l2 10s.;” “ umbrella, £4
145.;” “parasol, £3 10s.;” “pair of candle-
sticks, £l2 10s.” and the like. These are a
few of the items which the official assignee has
dug out of Mrs. Moss’s accounts up to date,
and he is still making good progress, and find-
ing more furniture almost daily.

The calm determination with which James
Munro has stuck to his self-appointment and
closed his ears to the bowlings of impotent
indignation, is intensely human under the cir-
cumstances. With political extinction near at
hand and his financial fame already gone for
ever there was nothing left for James but to
clear off to England with the last gift the gods
had given him—the gift of the \ ictorian Agent-
Generalship. In the hour of his distress the
played-out Premier found £3500 a year, and a
safe retreat from the execrations of unfortu-
nate shareholders, shoved into his grasp by the
beneficent hand of Providence, and his bony
fingers closed upon the good thing, as a matter
of °course. It was his last chance. The
country, he was well aware, would curse and
swear in a feeble, aimless fashion, even as it
had snorted and jeered when Graham
Berry, six years ago, seized the same billet,
after having taken the precaution to effect an
increase in the salary attaching to it. But as,
for all practical purposes, James was himself
the country and the premier representative of
public opinion, nothing could be more certain
than that the rest of the populace made no
difference in the matter. He was lawfully
entitled to select a new Agent-General, and his
choice fell upon himself. It is quite true, as
the single-minded and patriotic Sir Berry
remarked, last week, that the country ought to
“ elevate high principle as the greatest political
requirement from any party, ” but it is equally true
that James Munro hadn’t been elevated on that
understanding. Victoria took J. Munro for
what he was worth, assuming him to be worth
a little more for the moment than his prede-
cessor, Gillies. And now that he is acting
strictly up to his reputation, and in perfect
harmony with his record, by doing the best he
can for himself, the disgust of his compatriot;)
is much more unnatural than the conduct which
called it forth.. Munro, indeed, is merely
meeting the requirements of a political party.
Which the party is himself.

The Victorian Purification of Rolls Act is a
useful Tory engine for disfranchising numerous
common residential electors, and is being
enthusiastically utilised accordingly. Any
voter who moves from one district to another
even though he may only cross a street to
become located in a new electorate —becomes
the legal victim of the lynx, who forthwith
lodges an objection against the appearance of
his name on the roll of the district vacated,
while, under the old Act of ’9O, the defrauded
elector cannot vote in his now district until he
has resided within its bounds for three months.
After this fashion, over COO names were recently
objected to at the Fitzroy Revision Court, and,
as most of the men so challenged failed to
appear, nearly all those names were struck off
the Fitzroy roll. The monopolist knows his
vantage-ground. Many workmen cannot afford
to lose a day’s work to attend a revision court.
Others never receive the notices of the objec-
tions lodged against them, for these are held to
be legally served if delivered at the vacated
addresses described on the old rolls. Thus the
fat capitalist puts a little of his surplus cash
into the hands ot jackal agents and wipes out
his political foes with the jawbones of his
hirelings.
It is getting to be more and more probable that
there will he something very like civil war in
Queensland at next general election. By that
time the “ faking ” of the electoral-lists will be
finished, and hardly one name will he left on
the rolls, from Stanthorpe right up to the Gulf,
that isn’t the name of a monopolist or a mono-
polist’s flunkey. Then the Labour-party must
either submit to have no influence in the
election, or else it mutt take out ils share
of the rights of man by heaving bricks. And if
it does, the “military” will be called out,
and the editor of the Brisbane Worker will
be put in gaol (this item of the programme
is a dead certainty in any case), and
every fatted capitalist and his minion will he
sworn in as special constables to go about seek-
ing whom they may devour. The special con-
stable is a mean tyrant at all times and under
all circumstances, and with the Coalition to
urge him on he will be a roaring fiend. W lien
a crowd of his species were sworn in dv ring the
Melbourne strike—even under the retjime of the
fat and gentle Gillies, who always wore a
pleasant snigger on his shining red visage, and
wanted to please everybody, and had no more
ferocity in him than a feather-bed —they per-
formed tricks which were calculated to make
the angels weep. One day a harmless insurance
canvasser called on one of them while he w r as in
the full flush of his new-born dignity, and
the “ special ” promptly rushed out with his
staff of office, and arrested the visitor on a
charge of “ knocking at my door.” Then he
deposited his prisoner in the cellar to await his
trial. Later on he cooled down a little and
resolved to release him. But the humorous
captive declined to go unless legally discharged
by a magistrate, and the two men fought
desperately all over the place till at last the
“special ” managed to get his hardened criminal
out into the street. The rest of the amateur
force mostly went round and stunned people
promiscuously with their batons—barring*one
who chastised his wife with his insignia of
office. That is how the special constable con-
ducted himself even under the control of the
lazy, fat, old Gillies, who meant well, in a
fluffy sort of way, towards everybody. Under
the Queensland Coalition he would be simply a
pestilence, and when he had ramped round for
a few days somebody would start a full-blown
insurrection to get rid of him.
Writes a “ Sucking Alligator ”
Dear Bulletin,— You have so frequently advocated the
imposition of an increased probate-duty that I would like
to explain how easily even the present comparatively
light tax is evaded. Five per cent, seems a small enough
duty upon an estate exceeding £50,000 in value, but to
the Midas who purposes dividing his acres among his
children and whose paternal affection exceeds his
patriotism it is altogether too much. Instead, therefore,
ot devising to each son his respective share by will and so
charging the estate with the duty, he simply transfers
the land by deed of gift, deferring this operation perhaps
till advancing years warn him of the approach of A/.rael.
This, of course, means that the State gets a mere £1 out
of the estate (as stamp duty on the deed) instead of per-
haps some thousands. With any increase of the prohate-
duty this practice would no doubt become general in
large estates and could not easily be checked. Again, it
is quite a common thing for a man on his death-bed lo
transfer all his property to his wife, or any other person,
and as the transfer is an absolute one and not a donatio
mortis causa no duly is payable, but the usual £1 stamp
on a deed containing no, or a nominal, consideration. I
may say that numerous cases of both natures are within
my own knowledge. Max Nordau's simplicity as to the
practicability of enforcing a huge death-duty is somewhat
amusing ! — Yours, A Sucking Alligator.
This is all right so far as it goes. But the
habit formed during long years of greedy
acquisitiveness, in hundreds of cases, leads to
the rich man sticking to his goods and chattels
even at the very last gasp —dying with them
firmly in his clutches. Then that’s where the
State comes in. A big probate-duty can do no
possible harm. It will often rake a substantial
sum into the State coffers, and if it brings about
an earlier division of wealth, why, all the
better.
The efficacy of the strychnine injection in
cases of snakebite was farther demonstrated
recently when a patient badly bitten near Cainp-
belltown (Tas.) by a whip-snake was successfully
treated. Very little alcohol and ammonia were
administered.
The Tasmanian fruit-crop this season is
the largest ever known in the island—so large as
concerns the “ small fruit ” as to cause a ruinous
glut. The apple-crop is equally big, but a ready
outlet for this will be found in the London market.
Tasmanian orchardists—especially in the Huon
district—are faced with an enemy even more
destructive than the dreaded “ Codlin moth.”
The new pest is called the “Black Spot” and is
a fungus-like growth which when it attacks an
apple renders it unfit even for pigs. A scientific
country parson of considerable experience in
such matters has been subsidised by the Tas-
manian Government to give his whole attention
to this visitation for a period of 12 months in the
endeavour to discover a remedy.


It is most convenient to have a first-class
room near tram stopping places, where ladies
can rest themselves, and if they choose, have a
good cup of tea, or other refreshments. We
understand the Country Milk Co.’s “ Cascade,”
80 and 82 Elizabeth-street, near King-street, is just
the place; it is certainly the largest and best-
conducted room in Sydney, and is getting quite
the fashionable resort for afternoon tea. We also
understand that ladies who so desire can have
parcels sent there, so that they can call for them
just before taking the tram for home.*

BJC Macquarie-street North, Sydney, Feb. 24, 1892,
My Dear Moorabinua,—
There is snappy “f rooking” in “The Solicitor” at the
Criterion, and Emma Bronton (who has dyed her hair a
lovely golden, or else it was a very unwiggy wig) easily
bore away the bell. Her first dress was pale-grey cash-
mere looped over a dress skirt of grey brocade, fronts of
the brocade embroidered in gold bullion ; hat with grey
feathers atop. Her second garment was cream insertion
mull over primrose and trimmed with yellow ribbons;
hat of primrose and cream feathers. Marie Fraser wore
sulphur-green silk and black lace in the first act, with
three-cornered hat trimmed with pink flowers, and a
white gown bound about with blue ribbons in the last
confusing shriek—but the comedy was so funny that the
ladies would have got there all the same if they had been
dressed in sugar-bagging. On the night I enjoyed the
show the circle was quite full, and the prettiest dress was
(fortunately) on the prettiest girl—pink chiffon trimmed
with ruby jewelled galon, and a cluster of pink roses in
her hair.
Talking about the “Criterion,” reminds me that a fair
and uncorseted star who used to stride there, and who
posed as a profound believer in all sorts of uncanny
advanced ideas, but who bossed a very prosaic old man
and a small baby allee same, turned up her nose on a
recent occasion at the society women of Australia to a
bystander, who tells me all about it. “They have not
opened up the thoughtful side of life at all . . . and
when they find out the meaning of life, they will be all
the better for it,” she said, loftily, and she ought to know,
for, according to one {now) hears, the lady “had been
to school—rather,” as Pattie B. used to sing, and Society,
which cuddled her at first, dropped her like a hot potato
when they found out how much she could teach ’em, if
she chose. But it’s very ungrateful of her to snick at our
“ society ” women. They tried so hard to look owlish and
have “ideas,” while she was endeavouring to turn into
gospel the notions of that giddy old Swedish baldhead
that verily they should have their reward. “ Nobody goes
to Australia to make fame,” she concluded, “ they go to
make money.” And it must feel like a wicked waste of
time when they don’t make either—as was the experience
of jhe genius in question—mustn’t it ?
I lately received a weeping epistle in three pages and a
“ tiger,” as one may say, from a frozen-out governess who
heaps a lady-like malediction on all public schools to
which well-to-do parents, who don’t attach much import-
ance to social environment, send their olive-branches to
pick up crumbs of learning at the rate of a few coppers
per week. The consequence is that private schools suffer
pupil-hunger, and governesses are a drug on the market.
It is the expanded curriculum at the “Nash ” that works
the mischief: the pupils are taught all the accomplish-
ments for so many extra coppers—French, I believe, is
drilled into them for 2d. per week, and the schools are
filled with children for whom surely they were not in-
tended, and the little ragamuffins are crowded out like
the private teachers.
As for the poorer children who are pupils—the high-
flying subjects only serve to totally unfit them for the
positions which it is inevitable, in the course of life’s com-
petition, the great majority must ultimately occupy, and
as a result you have a hundred applications for a twenty-
shilling clerkship and two for a three times more remuh-
erative position as a skilled artisan. Ajiropos, I lately
heard of a little urchin who was sent to a hospital where
they coddled and cuddled him so that when he was sent
back to the parental hovel he howled to go back to the
hospital—turned up his nose at the simple joys of bare
boards and bread and treacle, and promises to be of
no earthly use to his relatives henceforth. Teaching the
piano to the washerwoman’s gal, who is born to cook, but
prefers to give “ lessons ” at a shilling a week, because it
is more “genteel,” has just the same effect. When a
girl is not a genius it is no real kindness to lift her above
the sphere where she is certain of getting employment
with a big chance of marrying some honest man who can
keep her, to the most hollow of callings in which even the
best educated people have to claw one another for a bare
living.
And how teachers of music have to claw one another
for a living!
Allured by the rumour that Gov. Jersey was going, I
patronised the tug-of-war one evening last week, but
“ George ”, didn’t appear, altho’ the inevitable fauteuils
and the unerring bouquet were all set out to do him
reverence. The elevated place of contest where puffy,
scraggy, sinewy, tall, andstuntedindividualshungdesper-
ately on a rope in a cloud of white chalk was, however,
haunted by lawyers—perhaps their idea was to be nice
and handy in case any of the teams wanted to bring
an action on the spot. The scene was novel, and conse-
quently interesting—from a distance. Close to, I am
told, the copious perspiration of the more or less untubbed
combatants set all the promenaders smoking in self-
defence. You couldn't blame them, but still the reek of
every sort of tobacco sent yours devotedly flying after an
hour of it. In conclusion, I must say that I don’t smile
on the tug.
I Zingaris and the Fleet met for cricket on Rushcutters
Bay Oval, the other day ; the seance was a great success
for those gyurls who dearly love a Merman in gold
buttons. One woman in putty colour tweed and a bunch
of tuberoses in her coat appeared well if wintrily turned

scarlet band, and Miss Nellie Hill encored sage-green silk
with great success. Another interesting spectacle was to
see tne women catching their gowns on a tent-peg that
popped up right in the frequented pathway towards the
buns and lemonade. Of course they didn’t say anything
that I would have to insinuate by blanks when they were
brought up by the peg, but I’m sure when they heard
their gathers ripping they all said, “Drat it!” in their
heart of hearts.
It was a dreadfully hot day, but the play was fast and
furious. I never heard of a cricketer getting sunstruck,
did you ? although most only have a flannel cap between
them and a direct ray. Perhaps that’s one of the blessings
of being an athlete with a thick skull—or is it that no one
gets sunstruck while he perspires? But revenons a nos
moutons —the teams ought to have run their flannels
through the steam laundry before posing on the wicket.
One officer especially—but he may have bought them
for cloudy white, and the colour has only kept fast in
patches. The band played, of course.
The second day’s cricket—All England v. New 7 South
Wales—w 7 as rather listlessly watched by the “ gallery,” on
account of the hopeless-looking score the enemy ran up—
but there was a bee-swarm attendance. The members’
portion of the ground looked something like a Quakers’
meeting—all the men in the members’ stand and all the
women in the little pavilion over the way. Lissome Marie
Fraser w 7 as there in an art costume of pink silk w 7 ith coral
pattern in white, and one of her stage hats to top off
with ; Miss Simpson wore a white silk gown with mauve
band; Miss Calvert’s gold straw bonnet trimmed with
pink and blue was becoming. A pale-grey gow 7 n mingled
with silver brocade was lit up by a yellow chiffon scarf
very effectively. The prettiest girl w 7 as dressed in pink
shrimp courduroy with zouaves of gold braid, flat hat
smothered in pink roses. Miss Dight had a well-fitting
gown of mushroom woollen trimmed with ecru silk, the
sleeves covered with cream guipure; her black hat
trimmed with peas-blossoms; and the only observable
heiress in the gathering w'ore a green silk frock and a
“Hamlet ” hat.
Apropos of the cricket-match—how quickly some women
are to fancy themselves insulted! “Big figures!”
innocently remarked a man waving his hand at the score
beyond, where the total stood at four hundred odd. But
just as he spoke, two of those expanded bolster ladies who
w T addle at every gathering happened to be passing.
“ Imperence!” said the one indignantly to the other.
“ Brute !” said the other indignantly to the one. That
was all.
A scintillating circle graced “ Trovatore ” at the Royal
last Saturday, and the frocking was fast and furious.
Mayor and Mayoress Manning (the latter wearing a gown
of cobweb-grey tulle and brocade) brought a son and a
daughter (she in a pink silk and chiffon gown, if you want
to know). A doctor’s young wife, who is a belle just at
present, wore pink chiffon and jewelled galon ; a white
satin gown, trimmed with chiffon and cut steel, fortu-
nately had a seat in the front row ; and a stout maiden,
in black lace over yellow satin with an Empire bow-and-
ends pinned high on her brawny shoulders, was happily
placed in the back one. On the stage, Leonora had non-
committal clothing as usual, and pendulated between two
graceful gowns of white and black, but I really must pro-
test against the lady wearing a white petticoat with her
black gown in the last act—it made her resemble a willic-
wagtail. Azuoena (and I must again rise wearily to sub-
mit that she oughtn’t to have left off the walnut-juice
when she came to her chin, leaving a white throat under-
neath—it looked as if she hadn’t washed her face) got all
the vegetables that were unchained for the occasion, though
all the singers deserved them—only Manrico shouldn’t
have turned and bolted up stage when he finished his
martial solo in the third act. It looked as if he expected
a brick.
But what amused me was to see the people devouring
the score of a familiar opera like “ Trovatore,” of which
you can’t help knowing every semi-quaver. It was a
charming display of ignorance, but, bless you, they
thought it was giddy heights of dillettante-ism to care-
fully follow the score with one finger while "Ah ! che la
morte ” wafted on the breeze.
In the blazing hot Sydney summer, when men riot un-
availing in iced liquors, women—whose duty or pleasure
takes them townwards—have a far cheaper method of
getting cool. A fashionable frock-builder confided to me
recently that it was the custom of many of her clients
especially large and perspiring clients—who always have
to wear tight clothes—to pant up to the fitting-on rooms
where they take off their bodices and cool off in the
directest draught they can find. Then when they are
thoroughly ventilated they get back into their armour—
and go round to the old man’s office, I suppose, and snicker
at him for working in his shirt-sleeves.

Two (educated) young gentlemen “ wishes ” to correspond
with one lady! Are women running short ? Or are they
becoming so expensive nowadays that it takes two men to
keep one wife ?
Ashfleld was agog the other day over the wedding of
Miss Annie Leaf Wilson, of “ Symington,” Ashfleld, to
Mr.. “Rod.” M’Donnell, of Millie-Millie Station, near
Young, N.S.W. The golden-haired bride looked well in
a handsome wedding-garment of bengaline, brocade and
chiffon, and her gown was softened off with lace at five
guineas a yard. A bridal bouquet and diamond brooch
were the gifts of the bridegroom, and the bridesmaids
(three in number) wore cream silk. Quite a shower of
presents—including a buggy-and-pair from the happy
man, numerous cheques, and silver in galore—shed lustre
on the proceedings, and the honeymoon was spent at the
“ Carington,” Katoomba, Mrs. M’Donnell travelling
thither in navy-blue picked out with tan and trimmed
with blue braid; tan shoes and a sailor hat en suite.
The latest whispered story has to do with the scion o
a poetic pa and somebody’s husband, and the moral, if
there is any, seems to be “ never take too long a walk
when the old man stays behind.”
Everybody will sympathise with Mr. and Mrs. Burdekin
in the loss of their second daughter, Helen, who died in
London from the effects of influenza. Mr. and Mrs.
Burdekin, it is said, were returning to Sydney sooner
than they anticipated on account ot the English vvintey
being “ too severe for the children.” The words were
almost a prophecy.
A Bulletin correspondent sends me this vice-regal
anecdotelet. On the occasion of a cricket-match (Hill
View v. Mittagong)at Government House, Sutton Forest,
the other Saturday, the visitors were telegraphed to
“ come early,” so an 11 a.m. start was obediently mad 6
and the enemy reached Hill View at lunch-time, hunger-
ing and thirsting for victuals after their 12 miles’ drive.
After a foodless pause of an hour the vice-regal team, led
on by Gov. J., strode on to the field, cricket was at once
declared, and the visitors sadly took in their belts another
hole and tried not to look as if they had been hollowed
out inside. At five-thirty pale tea and pallid sandwiches
of the most ladylike cut made an inadequate appearance
and were swallowed whole by the Mittagong martyrs, but
the spell of a nine hours’ fast was not broken and part of
the meeting went away with a stitch in its loyalty, so to
speak.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

Parkes says Critchett Walker, Principal
Under-Secretary of N.S.W., holds in his hands
the reputations of hundreds of prominent men. Let
Parkes speak for himself. No man holds in his
hands the reputation of any honest man.
The German Emperor visits Chicago
Exhibition. There are many millions of Germans
in the States and they don’t like emperors.
Which reminds us that we have talked to hun-
dreds of Americans and have never yet heard one
of them say a good word for monarchical govern-
ment.
Mr. Hancock, M.L.A., for Collingwood
(Vic.), says that “ Anyone can start a bank now-
adays with nothing more than a hook nose and a
counter.” This is understating the requirements
necessary. An absconding accountant would be a
sine qua non if the promoters of the bank intended
it to be up to date.
The O’Sullivan, M.P., considers that a
police-magistrate who requires the use of a law-
library is unfit for his position. Likewise the
doctor who consults a medical book is a quack.
Also, a barrister who looks up cases is a fool.
Similarly, a politician who uses the Parliamentary
library is an impostor.
Superstition lingers even in the cricket
field. On Saturday last M‘Donnell, retiring
after making five, crossed the in-going batsman,
Sam Jones, on the steps of the pavilion. A hard-
headed Sydney business man in the grand-stand
reserve instantly said : “ That’s unlucky. Jones
won’t top M‘Donnell!” And he didn’t.

Editor Bulletin— Sir,—An unveracious
correspondent has thought fit to describe, in the
columns of The Bulletin, my imaginary doings
at the MacKenzie-Balclutha banquet. Permit
me to embellish his fiction with a few facts. First,
I did not behave in the rowdy manner ascribed
to me; secondly, I was perfectly sober on the
occasion ; and. thirdly, I was not there at all.—
Yours truly, Vincent Fyke. Dunedin (M.L.),
Feb. 10, 1892. [Somehow all the good stories get
contradicted this way.—Ed. B.]
The Co-operative Irrigation Society, whose
advertisements appeared in The Bulletin a while
back, have obtained a promising lump of land at
Quantong, some 10 miles from Horsham (Vic.).
The sale of sub-divisions is already pretty lively,
and as both soil and climate are favourable to
fruit and vegetable production this new irrigation
colony should soon be started in active rivalry
with the older Chalfey country. Illustrated
pamphlets, plans, &e., may be obtained from the
secretary, at 42 Queen-street, Melbourne.
From Dorsetshire, (England) comes the
news of a farmer in that county being fined <£36
for starving his sheep to such an extent that 71
ewes and 500 lambs perished. Which brings to
mind the number of stations in Western N.S.W.
that are now heavily overstocked—with Egeson’s
postponed drought in the near perspective. If
Australian runholders are fined in proportion to
the penalty inflicted on their English brother, the
collateral assets of all our financial institutions
will not suffice to see them through the dry time.
Mr. “Jack” Want, Q.C., asserts re the
proposal to increase the pay of the N.S.W. police,
that six men could be got to take the place of
every man in the force at the present rate of
wages. The same can be said of the Colonial
Secretaryship or the Governorship, or any other
position. The Australian policeman is in point
of pay very small beer compared with the New
York officer. It is generally understood, by the
way, that the present policy of the authorities is,
as far as possible, to Select the force from natives
of Australia.
Andrew Fitzgerald, late manager of the
N.S.W. Trades and Labor Store, who got five
years last week for embezzlement, must be a
humorist, for between his committal and his con-
viction, he spent most of his time hunting up
discharged prisoners to ascertain “what solitary
confinement was like.” Another well-known
Sydney man very recently sent up for a long
“ stretch ” acted as if he did not mean, in Ameri-
can phrase, to “borrow trouble.” He arrayed
himself in purple and fine linen and “did the
Block.” “Hello!” cheerfully ejaculated a
prominent tradesman, seeing the soon - to-be -
jugged-one attired in a loud blue-grey suit and
aggressively new tan-boots with yellow sewing,
“ You arc a fool !” “ Why ?” enquired the law’s
prospective victim. “Well, you see, your rig is
all brand-new, and the fashions will have altered
a lot before you come put.” It is probable that he
was, at least, partially right. Blue-grey may be
de rigueur, but tan-boots will be as dead as hair-
nets at the end of a “ sixer.”

At Sydney Royal no less than three onem.
were presented during the past week, and two of
these were hrst productions in Sydney bv th
present company. “ Ernani ” was succeeded 1™
Donizetti s beautiful lyric of “ Lucia di Lammer
n l? or > , wlth an entirely new cast. This opera
afforded an opportunity for the introduction to !
Sydney audience of Signora Tancioni Cuttica «
light soprano with a voice of remarkable fresh
ness, purity, and culture, a lady, moreover who
sings with as little effort as a‘bird, and is nos
sessed of histrionic ability of a high order. He
reception was an ovation, and she was loudlv
applauded after every number. She is mi
doubtedly one of the finest and most accomplished
Lucias ” that have ever appeared in Australis
and her singing and characterisation of this rhk
will be remembered as among the local traditions
of Italian opera. It is interesting in this connec.
t lol i to note that Signora Cuttica made her debut
at Milan, about twelve years ago, in a small part
in Meyerbeer’s opera of “L’Etoile du Nord ’’ the
conductor on that interesting occasion being n n
other than Signor Eoberto Hazon, now of Sydney
The part of Edgardo in the present “ Lucia ” was

whose best effort was made in the closing scene
of the opera. Enrico, the baritone, was repre
sented by Signor Giannini Sisco, and Eaimondo
the basso, by Signor Coriolano lorio. Signor
Sisco has a rich and powerful voice, and his stage

would do well to revive “Lucia” before the con-
clusion of the season, for no other opera hitherto
produced has touched the same high-water mark.
* * *
On Saturday night “II Trovatore ” was
produced by the combined strength of the
Ernani and Eucia 3 casts at the itoyal before
a crowded house. This opera is always popular
and the airs are so provokingly familiar that
they ever suggest a reminiscence of the barrel-
organ of the prosaic side-walk. “II Trovatore ”
is, besides, irritatingly associated with a white
ostrich plume, nodding oyer the helmet of a tin-
cased nobleman of a Gothic type of architecture
and reviving boyhood’s recollections of the “ Castle
of Otranto” and the horrors of Monk Lewis.
There is also a lady in the show, whose propor-
tions are majestic, in love with a small tenor who
barely reaches her shoulder. Why is Leonora
always played by a big woman, and Man-
rico by a little man ? The heroine in ques-
tion was represented by Signorina Guidotti,
who achieved a genuine and well-deserved
success. This lady’s acting was as good as her
singing, and she was admirably supported by
Signor Yilalta, the tenor of the cast, who played
the part of Manrico with a vigour deserving of a
better fate than decapitation in the last act. The
gipsy, Azucena, was undertaken by Signorina
Cavalleri, who has sung in Sydney in the days of
yore, and who was literally overburdened with the
floral trophies with which her old friends pre-
sented her. She was, however, rather too light
for the part, and the first and second acts dragged
somewhat. In the third act, and especially in the
fourth, the performers warmed up to their work,
and the crowning tragedy of the final scene went
with a verve which seemed to satisfy everyone,
and make the assembled auditors feel glad that
they did not live in the ghastly days of a savage
like the Count di Luna. Signor Sisco undertook
this mail-clad effigy of slaughter, and it is not too
much to say that his singing held the first and
second acts together with an ability that caused
one to regret the sanguinary nature of the senti-
ments he clothed in sonorous song. On Tuesday
“Eigoletto ” was produced. “ Faust” is in pre-
paration, and in this masterpiece of Gounod’s the
combined strength of both casts will be employed.
It ought to prove the big success of the season.
* * * 5S
At Sydney Her Majesty’s, Gilbert Horatio
Parker’s special edition of the ancient fable of the
printer and the devil still waves its danger-signal
from the promp.-side flies. It is truly a wonderful
instance of literary atavism. Marlowe’s “ Doctor
Eaustus,” albeit stuffed with magnificent pas-
sages, but marred by vulgarisms and mediaeval
extravagances, was the model selected by Mr.
Parker in building up the present play, and he
has managed very ably to eliminate the poetry
and to retain the vulgarisms. A devil who is
always talking shop is not specially entertaining,
and although Mr. Geo. Rignold talks shop with a
great deal of unction and no small amount of
humour, he is not the sort of devil who would
succeed in business. He doesn’t coax, he bullies
people to the bad, and is as wearingly persistent in
recommending fire and brimstone, in their un-
adorned and crude condition, as an American
canvasser touting for subscribers for a bound
volume jewelled in four-and-twenty holes. Mr.
Jewett plays the part of Eanst with a commend-
able desire to do his best, but he is not the man
who would have conquered the love of a woman
like Marguerite as presented by Miss Kate
Bishop. This lady’s acting is a green oasis in a
desert of Gehenna, and redeems a wilderness of
crimson lime-light and demoniacal laughter from
unutterable woe. We shall be glad when Faust is
buried with his fathers and the long-promised
Shakespearian play is produced.

* * * *
At the Tug-of-war contest at Darlinghurst
Hall the different teams have been hauling their
W ay to fame, glory and prize-money, and excited
citizens of various nationalities have been cheering
and “barracking” in an infuriated manner for
the credit of their country. On the night that
Ireland put its hoof on the rope-walk, and pitted
the pratyof Brian Borru against the Caledonian
haggis, about 2000 sons of Erin foregathered on
the pavement outside, and grimly chewed the
shamrock of their wrongs. Luckily the Scotch
team went under at an early date, and Ireland’s
griefs were translated into victory. The descen-
dant of Erin-go-bragh is an uncanny customer in
a crowd, and he whoops in an awful manner when
things don’t go as Daniel O’Connell once repre-
sented that they ought to. The Italians made a
plucky and truly Roman stand, but weight and
a vegetarian diet were against them, and lager
beer gave the coup de grace with a gentle kind-
ness suggestive of the moral influences of the
triple-alliance. The crush at the doors between
intervals was a hideous scene of Dantesque dis-
order, in which women with children in arms
nearly furnished a tragedy. The tug-of-war
contest has, however, proved one thing—the only
people who ought to engage in it are those
accustomed to hang on to a rope, and in future
encounters of the kind only sailors should engage
—unless we make an exception in favour of the
hangman.
# * #
At the time of writing (Tuesday afternoon)
the only teams left in the contest are Sweden,
Russia, Ireland, Norway, and Germany. Sweden
is ahead with seven wins, Russia and Ireland
have six each, Norway has a score of five, and the
Fatherland brings up the tail of the show with
only four. Therefore, as the signs go, the best
hope of Ireland wauld seem to be that it may lick
Russia and get second prize, and the worst chance
is that Russia may lick Ireland, and then Norway
lick Russia, and Sweden wipe out Germany, in
which case, Sweden will be first, the Slavs second,
and the sons of the bogs will only have the plea-
sure of pulling off for the third prize, and if they
lose there will be a sad “ whirroo ” in Connaught,
and a dismal howl in the wilds of Connemara,
and their friends will rush in and knock the hall
to pieces, and kill everybody.

Now, La Cigale’s a saucy gal,
A rustic little lady, ah !
A singer rare who can’t abear
A life in calm Arcadia,
So goes to town and wins renown
And mashes Charley Ryley, O,
Which doth upset his former pet,
A duchess sweet and smiley O
(A married dame, who, tie for shame!
Is not at all a sainty bit);
But when that minx, her rival, sinks
To earth and has a fainty fit,
It works a cure—for, good and pure
As ’Vangelistio Varley, O,
The duchess turns and simply yearns
To give the maid her Charley O.
Of friends in need we love to read,
But such extreme civility
In womankind you seldom find
Amongst the old nobility.
And though, bedad ! we should be glad
To see them getting properer,
The human race can show no case
In which a like event takes place
Outside a comic opera.
* * * *
“ La Cigale” is running on greased wheels
at Melbourne Princess’, and the fame of Marie
Halton spreads around like a fashionable epi-
demic. All that was evidently catchy on the
first night has caught, whilst the items that
didn’t “go” very freely have been forced upon
the market by strict attention to business. Elton
is putting up another good record, and the inci-
dental ladies’ orchestra fiddles with increased
effect. In short, people of accredited intelligence
protest that “La Cigale,” taken in the bulk, is
the finest show yet produced at the headquarters
of comic opera, and the force of protestation can
ho further go.
