At the Bijou there was a necessary change
on Saturday night. “The Hobby Horse” wagged
his tail to rapidly diminishing audiences during
the week, so B. and B. wisely conducted that
attenuated animal off the track and turned him
out to grass. In the score of breeding, as they
observe in sporting papers, there is no fault to
hud with “The Hobby Horse,” but he has no
s peed, and he’s a difficult beast to manage, and
he can’t stay, and he isn’t worth entering for any
more engagements. In order to give an admiring
Melbourne a few final glimpses of George Anson
* n strong parts “ The Parvenu ” was revived on
Saturday for six nights only, but the admiring
offy showed its bad taste by stopping away con-
siderably from a really admirable performance.
Ch February 27th (if not earlier) the management
will revive “Caste,” also for six nights, and in
the saddened capacity of papa Eccles—a man who
once seen can never be forgotten—G.W.A. will
bid his friends adieu. He is off “home.” A
general election is about to happen over there,
(Hid Anson has decided to stand for Great Britain
m the hour of need.
# * * *
The return of Dampier and Co. to the
Alexandra was received with a shriek of satisfac-
tion, but here again the house presented a general
appearance of having gone out to get a drink.
“ Valjean,” all the same, is a drama which earned
respect in the long ago, and will probably com-
mand fair support when the weather cools down.
The name is the only thing against it at a theatre
where Victor Hugo is not so favourably known as
Garnet Walch. “The Repentant Lag” would
sound a lot better.
* -;if
The superiority of “Little Jack Sheppard’’
to every other Gaiety burlesque produced this
side of the line was easily asserted at the Royal.
The names of the librettist and of Meyer Lutz,
the composer, meet with no recognition on the
playbill, but it would be an act of courtesy to
pitch in a passing reference to the fact that their
work wasn’t blown together on the premises.
There is pretty music in “Little Jack,” also a
coherence of story and a liberal supply of humour.
“ Bluey ” and his commanding officer in crime
don’t meander out of the plot and reappear in
half-a-dozen idiotic capacities, nor does Jonathan
Wild forget that he is a villain with a purpose.
The show has method in its madness, and the
enduring quality of many melodies was proved by
the cheery reception given to them on Saturday.
In several places the present performance will
bear comparison with the old original success
under Brough and Boucicault. Royce is almost
as active as before, and Billee Barlow plays
the hero in a rollicking music-hall style which
suits the boys better than Fanny Robina or
Nellie Barren. They exchange confidences with
beautiful Billee between the verses, and when she
shows all her gleaming white teeth at one fell
swoop, they want to die rightaway and be danced
upon by the bouncing idol. Florence Harcourt,
as Thames Darrell, seems elegantly tame once
again, and the Mendez of Harry Daniels is another
tender reminder of the past. George Walton
makes a good Jonathan, whilst, in minor parts,
Edwin Kelly and Lilian Davies are many degrees
better than their predecessors. On the other hand,
thepresentMrs. Sheppard invites contumely, Edith
Vane is a hard, unsympathetic kind of Winifred,
and the wonderful dancing of departed Willie
Ward is sorely missed. In place of that famous
pas de deux by Ward and Emma Temple, at Blue-
skin’s “caffy ” the new Mr. Kneebone introduces
a quadrille-party whose results are more startling
than beautiful. The “ladies ” are members of the
tougher sex disguised in low neck dresses, and when
these bogus maidens begin to turn somersaults
across the stage the effect is very fin dc siecle.
However, the house was carried away by its feel-
ings and encored the novelty like one man. If
“Little Jack Sheppard ” is to run for the remain-
ing month of Billee B.’s engagement in Melbourne
the orchestra and chorus will have to be strength-
ened and polished up. Furthermore, there is
urgent need for better stage management and
stricter discipline at the Royal. Now, as in
“ Dick Whittington,” one is struck by the way in
which good material is not always utilised to the
best advantage.
At Melbourne Opera House they have
worked a large improvement in the programme,
cutting out some items that fizzled on the first
night and introducing a new juvenile trapeze act.
Undoubtedly, however, popular interest in this
high-toned variety show begins with Carl Hertz
and ends with the seven Graggs, who, in addition
to their own phenomenal turn, contribute all that
is worth seeing in a final ballet d action. Hertz
has a large reserve fund of tricks to draw upon,
and the Graggs are doing something which, if not
more wonderful, is more risky than any of their
first feats.
** * *
At Melbourne Athenaeum this week the
irrepressible Stanley, who has successfully ex-
plored Maoriland without leaving Srnythe to die
amongst the savages, is repeating his solemn
series of sermons on Darkest Africa and the pro-
per way to work it. The indomitable hero is sup-
ported by a fresh set of magic-lantern slides,
and if he could rig himself out with another voice
and a new batch of experiences, and a different
method of unfolding ’em, he might be earnestly
recommended as a warning to the young at
popular prices. As things are, he doesn’t go as
far as the Cycloramas in the entertainment line,
and the silk stocking company can run rings
round his dreary iron constitution. Frank Clark,
by the way, is showing a spicy assortment of the
aforesaid stockings at St. George’s Hall. Dan
Tracey has a nigger crowd at the Gaiety, and
other familiar places are sweltering in various
stages of prosperity, or otherwise.

Marie Halton’s floral tributes (well earned) on the “La
Cigale ” premiere were mostly provided by the lawful
partner of her joys and sorrows, who, with painful anxiety,
watched the state of the poll from a stage box. It is not
eiven to every woman to enjoy the triumph of mashing
her own husband—after the honeymoon.
That well-esteemed little woman, the late Miss Dwight,
was for a quarter of a century pianiste at Melbourne
Temperance Hall concerts, and her friends thought it hard
that the regular Saturday night performance could not be
suspended while she lay dead. She was buried next day.

You can back the “popular favourite” to look after
herself when a new prima donna threatens to monopolise
public appreciation. It was downright rich to see the
little lady, when the company passed before the curtain,
receive piles of gorgeous vegetable, while the newly-
imported prima got nothing tangible. And, moreover,
the latter’s husband had to vacate a stage-box while the
said vegetable was hoisted up to the other by a menial!

Dear Bulletin,— l emphatically deny that 1 wrote or
had anything to do with article in Melbourne Herald
referred to in a recent issue of The Bulletin under the
heading “Poverty Point.” The article in question is
thoroughly at variance with facts and does not in any
way coincide with my experience as Dampier’s col-
laborator. I have not quarrelled with him nor am I
likely to do so seeing that he has always treated me with
the utmost consideration. —Yours, Garnet Walcii,
Alexandra Theatre, Melbourne.
A dramatic co., which has “ new and elaborate scenery
specially painted for its new and original Australian
drama,” struck Albury the other night. One scene
depicted a city street. Two actors appeared on the stage
and placed two easy chairs in what was apparently the
middle of the street. Then one of them remarked,
“ What a pretty rustic scene !” The audience didn’t even
smile.
Melbournite wants to know’ what’s the dynamite that has
been planted behind the Sydney scenes, over a theatrical
wedding’.
Bill Leake recounts an unrehearsed effect at a back-
blocks theatre. There were no dressing-rooms and the
actors had to change their clothes between the solitary
scene and the back wall of the building. One of the
comedians was sans culottes during the progress of the
piece, when the only barrier between himself and the
audience fell down. A loud burst of applause followed
and the actor, imagining it due to the merit of the play,
turned to Leake, saying “By gord, we’ve hit ’em .” A
glance to the rear, however, caused him to realise his
mistake, and he disappeared off the stage.
A new star is coming to Australia, Barnum Bailey s
Starr, the man who does the bill-posting, a genius in his
way, like Ben. Lusby, “Champion lightning ticket-seller
of the universe,” who used to astound the patrons as he
slapped out the tickets with both hands, the Briareus of
the box. In like manner did Starr paralyse the myrmi-
dons of Partington, the mammoth London bill-posting
man, when the Olympia show came over. The Londoners
stuck on bills laboriously and painfully sheet by sheet,
but Starr’s men would have a 24-sheeter all in one piece,
and put it up in a twinkling, with things like hay-rakes.

Melbourne Age, reporting a meeting of the unemployed*
had the artful impudence to state—“lt was announced
that the benefit entertainment given on Saturday evening
at the Theatre Royal had turned out a failure financially.
This was attributed to the fact that no advertisement
respecting it appeared in the Age.” That David Syme has
refused to accept advts. from the Royal, for reasons already
explained in these columns, is quite true. But to publish
an ignorant hypothesis that the aforesaid “ boycott” had
anything to do with the state of the Royal on this parti-
cular occasion is like David’s “cheek.” The unemployed
were to get all the receipts over £l2O on the 47th per-
formance of “ Dick Whittington,” which would scarcely
be a fat sum, anyway. At a matinee performance on the
same day, the Royal had been crowded, a fact that, on
the same line of argument, may also be attributed to the
Age’s miserable spleen.

decent orchestra.

The old Australian actor, Richard Nesbitt M‘Cron,
when in ’Frisco in the rough days, had a quarrel with
Mrs. Hudson Kirby, afterwards Mrs. Stark. The lady
came to the front and complained that she had been
insulted by the “ damned Britisher.” A row arose, and
after repeated calls Nesbitt came forward and disclaimed
any intention of offending anyone. Some rough in the
pit sang out “Shoot the 1” Whereupon Nesbitt,
who was playing Richard 111., melodramatically walked to
the footlights and, opening his shirt, said : “ Fire and be
damned ! I come from a country whose men do not fear
bullets.” Hooroo!

A young and beautiful Australian actor married
recently into a “serious family.” He married a very
serious girl, and the girl had a serious mother—one who
alludes to herself as a “ vessel,” and there was a dreary,
pious aunt in the connection with flat, shiny curls and a
vinegar expression ; also a grandmother who was always
thinking of her latter end. The family objected to the
actor at first, but at length it gave way on the under-
standing that the girl had a “ call ” —we believe that is
the correct expression—to save him as a brand from
the burning. Parenthetically the present writer wishes
to mention that he also had a “call” once, but it
was a call of sixpence per share in the Golden Pyke
mine, accompanied by a lawyer’s letter, which was not a
case in point. After the marriage the bride went down
one night with a shudder to the theatre ; she felt that she
couldn’t rescue her husband from the unclean thing
unless she knew exactly what the unclean thing was,
and she went to take stock of the evil. She seemed
perplexed and discouraged by what she saw, and next
night she dug the ancient aunt out of her seclusion,
and took her along as an additional support. It is
painful to record such a fact, but these two have been at
the theatre almost every night since then. About a week
after their falling-off, the bride’s mother began to hang
round the altar of Baal—to quote her own expression ;
and then the grandmother came. And now a demoralised
“serious family’’—three generations of it —whoops
vociferously for that actor every night, and worships
him, and heaves bouquets at him, and their excitement
is a touching thing to witness. It is the only real amuse-
ment the old lady has had in all her 78 years, and she is
just beginning to be alive for the first time since the date
of Waterloo.

Cardinal Manning on the stage. In his well-known
book, the “Eternal Priesthood,” page 240, he quotes:
“We strictly forbid . . all ecclesiastics in sacred
orders to be present at scenic representations in public
theatres, or in places which serve for the time as a public
theatre, imposing upon the transgressors the pain of
suspension incurred thereby ipso facto, hitherto in force
in England, and reserved to the Ordinaries respectively”
(Cone. Westm. Dec. xxiv. 1). And he adds this comment;
“May this wise and wdiolesome tradition of our fore-
fathers never be relaxed. The theatre in their days was
high, intellectual, and pure compared with the modern
stage and its moral fail.” And yet we see their reverences
laughing at comedv
Not all of Gounod's works were great successes
amongst others, his “ Tribut de Lamora ” proved a per
feet failure. One day the publisher of “Faust” me.
Gounod in the street, and expressed to him his indigna-
tion at the shabby hat Gounod was wearing. " Qi.t
voulez-vous,” said the composer to Ids publisher, who bad
made a fortune of “ Faust,” “that is —the hat of Faust. ’
Afterwards Gounod sold his " Tribnt de Lamora” to the
same man for 100,000 francs (£1000). The opera was a
total failure. Gounod, in meeting bis publisher occa-
sionally, could not help expressing bis astonishment at
ids dilapidated hat; whereto the publisher dryly remarke d
“ Que voulez-vous, that is the hat of ‘ Tribut de Lamora.’ ”
Sydney city organist Wiegand has been incapacitated
for a fortnight owing to a bite from a poisonous spider—
in which was probably incorporated most of the little
soul of a venomous and still-living musical critic.
Maddison Morton’s posthumous pieces are being brought
out. The unlucky thing about him was that though hecould
write as well at eighty as at thirty, he was left stranded
high and dry by the receding wave of fashion. However,
when an author goes for ever, it always gives a fillip to
his work, as with Boucicault, whose “ Formosa,” “Streets
of London,” and “ Avrah-na-Pogue ” have been lately
revived in England with eclat. At one time, the pro-
grammes of the Haymarket, Adelphi and Lyceum
would hardly have been thought complete without one of
Maddison’s pieces. “Box and Cox” is a compound of
two French farces.
Here is how G. V. Brooke went on the stage. As a boy
of 15, looking like a man, he applied to the Dublin
manager for an engagement, but was, of course, laughed
at, and then it happened the Liverpool boat was late,
bringing over Macready. All was ready to produce
“ William Tell,” and, not to disappoint the public, the
management gave the “ stage-struck boy ” a chance, as
tire hero, by which he so profited that he jumped forth-
with into the saddle as a star, devouring all the crack-
parts with rapidity, and making a hit in every one.
While quite a boy, he acted “Virginius,” at the City of
London theatre, East End, with Cclat, but never would
begin at the bottom of the tree, as second to Macready,
like Phelps, Anderson, Creswick, and so on. Probably
bis Othello was the best ever seen. Yet, G. H. Lewes
said he never acted it so well as the first night at the
Olympic.

A great swell, who had accepted service
under Sheriff Cowper, and whose braided frock
seemed to lift him high above the common herd,
“got the sack,” after about 48 hours’ service,
thusly. Sporting-attorney Gannon was strolling
quietly into court when the potentate barred his
way. “ I am an officer of the Court,” said Gannon.
“ Nonsense ; where’s your uniform ?” exclaimed
the man in braid. Sack.
Dear Bulletin, —The worst of fat men is
that after each spasm of energy they require such
a long period of recuperation. Barton, after his
late exceptional demonstrations, is resting once
more, and thus Premier Dibbs is allowed to run
amok among his own supporters. The “ leading ’
N.S.W. politicians who have a rudimentary
notion of tactics can be counted on one hand-
with some fingers to spare. For Dibbs to insult
Toohey is the height of folly. And O’Sullivan
and Rose have done a heap more for Protection
than G. R. Dibbs.—W.
Have not the wives of the prisoners who
have died in Parramatta gaol of typhoid fever
(admittedly caused solely by the filth of the plate
and the neglect of the Government), a good action
against the State for compensation? The N.S. W.
Government had, no doubt, a right to imprison
the men, but it had no right to kill them. The
prisoners may have lost their civil rights, but
surely their wives have not lost their civil rights.
The men were not sentenced to death. If they
were poisoned by the filth of officialdom, let the
State pay the butcher’s bill. On whose head is
the blood of these men ?
When Graham Berry said to his ban-
quetters last week—“ You must resolve that, by
one great national effort, you will restore the
credit of Victoria,” he was blatherskiting. If any
wheezy old windbag there present had called
upon Graham to explain the nature of the required
effort, and the proper way of making it, he
couldn’t have fetched an answer. The credit of
the Cabbage Garden doesn’t want restoring, any-
how. That's all right. It only requires the right
gardeners, and not any further exertion from the
fat crowd of wrong gardeners who sat around and
cheered the knighted spouter.
The elevation of A.N.A. Peacock to a
real portfolio billet in the moribund Victorian
Ministry is gall and wormwood for poor Frank
Stuart, who quarrelled with his bread because it
wasn’t buttered soon enough. Everything comes
to him who waits (provided there is something to
come and he doesn’t die before it arrives), and
Frank might have been a nominal Minister of
Education for five or six weeks but for his rash
quarrel with Munro. Now the “ Hon.” Peacock
spreads his plumage before the eyes of his erst-
while senior Minister (unattached), and Frank
feels like a “ dead bird ” that showed temper at
the post and came in with the crowd.
Last Friday Mr. Abraham Friedman,
erstwhile prince of Sydney money-lenders, ap-
peared in N.S.W. Bankruptcy Court to explain
his dealings with W. T. Muston, the bankrupt
proprietor of the partially-built Lyceum Theatre.
The solicitors’ table was loaded with piles of
Friedman’s huge, brass-bound ledgers. The first
question was, “ Are you not bankrupt yourself ?”
But Abraham replied not—only burst into a
flood of tears, which ran down his furrowed cheeks
and spluttered on the floor of the witness-stand.
The scene was an affecting one. The great
Mokanna of the bill-of-sale industry, who had
complacently put Gentiles through the mill by
the dozen, had himself practically realised what
bankruptcy meant. Truly, one touch of bung-
ness burns the whole world’s skin. After,
sobbing for some time, Abraham pulled
himself together and went on, with an
occasional sigh and tear, to explain his misfor-
tunes, which began, as is usual in such cases, with
over-prosperity. He and Muston bought a pro-
perty in 188 G, and at once sold it at a profit of
<£lo,ooo. Then he started out to own the whole
of the earth, and when he had spent <£loo,ooo on
it and locked up all the capital of his bill-broking
business, he began to borrow on mortgage. When
he got into a tight place he resumed bill-discount-
ing—and incidentally lent money to Muston at 25
per cent. Times got bad, and bills were almost
invariably dishonoured. Friedman’s bank frowned,
winked wickedly with both its eyes, opened its
mouth wide, and brought together its jaws with
steel-trap snap. Abraham had got there.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The National Association say that they
oppose class-legislation, yet they support the dual
vote, which gives one class one vote more than
the other’.
The Labour-party in Victoria hopes to
slip in the majority of their candidates against
the forces of the Conservatives, who will
probably be divided on small questions.
When Premier Shiels eulogised Berry’s
Agent-Generalship at the banquet given to
Surgrame, someone ejaculated “Why didn’t you
leave him in London?” Then Shiels got nasty
and asked for “courtesy.”
There is a decided movement to retrench
in the Victorian Civil Service —another Black
Wednesday. In Maoriland they apply to the
Service M. Catalani’s recipe for an opera Co. :
“My wife and four or five puppets.”
Once more it is rumoured that Judge
Boucaut (S. A.) intends to resume politics—that ho
will resign, and run for a country constituency on
returning from his leave. It is his ambition to
ultimately sit in the Federal Parliament.
The Armidale “ Freetrade and Liberal”
Association has asked the parent association in
Sydney to drop the word Liberal.” There is a
sense of eternal fitness in the request. In Aus-
tralia, Freetrade has always meant Conservative.
In Great Britain, in 1864, the last return
given under the old Masters and Servants Act,
recorded that for that year alone 10,246 working-
men had been imprisoned at the suit of their
masters. And masters at the suit of the men ?
None !
Note how Griffith and Playford kootoo to
Kintore! All this is an outcome of his trip North,
and his memo, on foreign labour. One feels un-
easily that the whole is a “ put-up job,” and it is
memorable that Kintore expressed his annoyance
at the memo, being published.
With the new railways now in course of
construction, W.A. has nearly 600 miles of State
lines. This with private lines makes a total of
1200 miles in the province. Treasurer Forrest
anticipates that for 1892 the Government railways
will have a surplus over expenditure.
In the revision of the Melbourne and
suburban rolls, for every elector removed from
the manhood suffrage list by reason of criminal
conviction, there were thirty-three taken off the
ratepayers’ and property-holders’ rolls. After
this, the National Association had better advocate
a vote for Theft.
The Brisbane Chamber of Commerce has
passed a unanimous vote of thanks to Psalmyell
Griffith for his resolution to start black labour
once more in the North. When an institution
like the Chamber of Commerce congratulates a
person like Griffith it makes us think somehow of
a sweater congratulating a hangman.
Sydney Daily Telegraph’s parliamentary
humorist, airing his literary erudition, alludes to
Premier Dibbs’ attitude on the rejection by the
N.S.W. Upper House of the Government’s Elec-
toral Bill, by paralleling it with that of the hero
of a novel by James Payn, entitled “ What Will
He Do With It ?” And this is fame !
According to a Maoriland cablegram, Mr.
A. J. Oadman is now to be known as Minister for
Marine and Stamps. This is getting things down
pretty fine. By-and-by, the Maorilanders will
find that the wretched Cadman is overloaded, and
they will make him simply Minister for the pic-
ture on the front of the stamps, and appoint
somebody else Minister for the gum on the back.
The cabled resignation of Premier Frey-
cinet and Government, arises out of their pro-
posed measures divorcing State and Church. In
the last census, eight millions of French declined
to specify their religion, and were so returned,
but in all former censuses this class was returned as
Catholic ! A dodge not unknowm in Australia—
in another way.
A big attempt is being made to secure
Indian immigration for the Northern Territory.
J. L. Parsons, M.P., ex-Government Resident,

The London Evening News on Booth’s
agricultural “colony
In the wood-cutting department the hands are very
badly underpaid, and the commodity sold under market
price. At this rate Booth will be a social curse. He gets
his capital for nothing, sweats the worker, undersells the
traders, and does all the mischief we predicted.
If we must have sweaters, let us have them with-
out religious trimmings.
Item by one Johnson, of South Australia :
“If everyone who showed his head above his
fellows was to be hit on it, a man would put his
wealth into a stocking and sit over it with a club.”
Quite true, Johnson. And at the present time
any man on the downward track who shows his
feet underneath the rest gets his corns shot at.
There are two sides to every question, and they
ought both to be considered.
The greater the increase in wealth the
less its purchasing power and the higher the
standard of comfort in the community. This is
the law of compensation which shows that human
contentment is neither possible nor desirable.
Socialists ask for the right to lead a comfortable
existence, not believing, as is popularly supposed,
that this carries with it that oysterlike sense of
satisfaction which precludes humanity from
striving eternally at the realisation of higher social
ideals.

grow free from rust ? ” demanded Three-Hat
Davies when the N.S.W. Council was considering

which is quite as sensible a remark when you look
at it carefully. And, for that matter, Parkes’dead-

the old Casual Labour Board, would it ?

One of the conditions of the contract for
the construction of the West Australian Midland
Railway is the introduction of 5000 adult
Europeans. They are engaged in London,
as railway-laborers, at ss. per day, and when
they commence work, ninepence" per day is
deducted from their wages to repay the passage-
money advanced to bring them and their families
to the province. The “truck system” is per-
mitted— i.e., the contractor supplies his own men,
from his own stores, at his own prices. The
imported labourers now realise that they are not
as well off as they were in London.
That big, hunky statesman, Patterson,
seems to have practically vanished out of Vic-
torian politics. Among all Australian politicians
he was the nearest to Bret Harte’s portrait of that
“strong man,” General Gashwiler, of the TJ.S.
Congress. People took him for an intellectual
giant because he used to talk so loud and frown so
villainously, and bang his fist so fearfully when he
talked about the unemployed, but he was only a
screamer and banger after all. While he held office
he was a great man on account of the horrid row
he made with his feet; now hardly anybody seems
to feel sure that he is alive—or that he ever was
alive for that matter.
An ardent Freetrade fanatic asks in the
press how it is that in the professedly Protec-
tionist Australias constant efforts towards recipro-
city are being brought forward in self-stultifica-
tion? Just as in the U.S. interested reciprocity
is desirable, so in Australia is interprovincial
reciprocity beneficial because the members of these
neighbouring States all live under a similar
standard of social comfort. When the world’s
workers comply with what Australians and
Americans consider to be the essential conditions
of domestic comfort then and then only will they
meet the world’s producers on the broad platform
of International Freetrade.
Froude writes to Parkes a letter con-
gratulating him upon “his splendid efforts for the
Confederation of Australia.” The irony of it —
Mrs. Candour in breeches complimenting Tar-
tuffe ! But the letter proves nothing except that
Froude doesn’t understand the least bit of what
he is writing about. The “Confederation” of
the Australias was proposed by no one. Parkes’
plan meant a unification —one strong Government,
the rest shadows. The Draft Bill implies a
federation with a strong Executive. A Confedera-
tion is a temporary and loose union with a weak
Central Executive. Parkes’ vanity, of course,
prompted the immediate publication of the absurd
letter.
Dear Bulletin, — Speaking to a Sydney
friend the other day on political subjects I was
struck by the strange opinions he held, and his
estimate of the personnel of the present Protection-
ist N.S. W. Ministry. I asked him if he had read a
certain speech delivered from the Ministerial
benches on the question we were discussing, and
his reply was, “No! I never read the speeches.
I find it easier to read the leading articles in the
morning papers—in fact, I get all my information
on political subjects by doing so—it is the
quickest way.’’ J ust imagine the state of a man’s
mind who derives his political information solely
from reading the articles in Sydney’s morning
papers ! If there are many more like him, and no
doubt there are, is it any wonder that the
imported-shoddy-party has reigned so long in
N.S. Wales? When are the protectionists going
to wake up and produce that new morning paper ?
—Yours, F.T.
What might have eventuated into a pretty
dispute as to the supremacy of the Central
Government has just been avoided in Canada by
the knuekling-down of the Dominion Cabinet.
The Quebec Government instituted a royalty on
mine-yields. This the Central Government—
patently in the interests of English investors—at
first disallowed, but, afterwards finding that the
Provincial Legislature stood to its guns, approved.
The tremendous centralisation of the Canadian
federal system reduces State autonomy to a mini-
mum ; and when a province can, single-handed,
defeat the Federal or Central power, it is a con-
vincing sign of the break-up of the system. The
Canadian Federation was, in short, Sir John
Macdonald, and as soon as “Old To-morrow”
was numbered with the defuncts his system began
to totter to the grave after him. Australians
should not forget that it was Parkes’ idea—the
one for which he has just been congratulated by
Fronde—to federate the Australias on the Cana-
dian model.

Common clay;
And, ’twixt them and the highly-bred

America is unapproachable in agriculture,
According to Mulhall, the value of her yearly crop,
still growing by leaps and bounds, is no less than
.£778,000,000 against Russia’s £563,000,000,
France’s £480,000,000, and Britain’s £251,000,000.
She has 222 acres under grain for every
100 inhabitants against France’s 97, Britain’s
28, and Australia’s 125. In America,
chiefly by virtue of superior machinery, nine
millions of farm hands raise nearly half as much
grain as the 66 millions of European labourers.

Sir G. Campbell, M.P., whose death is
cabled, made a sensation at the British Associa-
tion with his paper on homooulture, about which
he talked as coolly as a gardener talks of porno-
culture. His ideas, involving a capsize of mar-
riage, were further audaciously developed by
Grant Allen, in the Universal Review.

Mr. M‘Corraack, the Adelaide artiste, has
just finished a life-size portrait of the late Dr.
Adela Knight, to the order of her father, Rev. S,
Knight, who is greatly pleased with the likeness,
although the artist never saw her, and worked
solely from photos. This portrait ought to be in
the National Gallery, as that of Australia’s
pioneer lady doctor. In a press notice we read
that she was spurred to a medical career by her
keen Socialism.
The Sydney murderer, Bertrand, has now
been in gaol 26 years. He has had all the time
the most powerful influence at work for his
release, but unavailingly—because a ihost influ-
ential person (against whose family Bertrand com-
mitted a grave offence) has worked in the opposite
direction. Bertrand cannot live much longer, we
are told. His case shows that after all it is pos-
sible to substitute imprisonment for life for
capital punishment.
Victoria’s new Minister of Defence, George
Davis, is a farming squatter from Tinamba,
Gippsland, and is loaded up with poetry and the
drama. In the early days of his sprouting ambi-
tion Davis would mount his steed and retire to a
lonely aboriginal cromlech on his run, and harangue
an imaginary host, declaim against the foul in-
gratitude of his enemies, and threaten the quiver-
ing caitiffs within the Capitol. He was a fine-
looking fellow, physically a Macbeth ; he was
always in the heroic, denunciatory business, and
the immediate result of it all was that he became an
alderman !
There died last week in Hobart an old
“ Swinger ’’—that is to say, a convict transported
(in 1832) for incendiarism. When the terrible
agricultural depression of the Early Thirties pre-
vailed in England, numberless farmers’ ricks and
barns were burnt—and the farming hands all
over the country accused an imaginary person,
“Swing,” of having caused them. The man who
passed away last week was at John Price’s wed-
ding in old St. David’s. Being then one of the
Government House garden hands (under assign-
ment) he had taken some green stuff and flowers
to help decorate the chancel. He had only to go
a tew hundred yards, but two sbldiers were told
off to escort him.

own with any man now at the Southern Bar;
and, as for Parliament, his avowedly popular
sympathies would have given him a tight grip on
the democracy. Perhaps, though, he would not
have stayed in Melbourne. The close of his life’s
romance came with a sudden tragioness. The

Editor Bulletin.—Sir,—l should have
liked to add to your truthful and eulogistic
remarks concerning the late Dr. Ulrich Hlibbe,
of South Australia, draftsman and originator of
the Torrens’ Act, the story of how the S.A.
Government was badgered year after year to do
something to assist the poor old scholar in Ins
declining years. It resulted eventually in the
grant of a lump sum of .£3OO, and a certain M.i ~
seeing that the doctor was very old, blind*, 1 and
infirm, offered, on deposit of this sum, an annuity
of .£SO per annum, which was accepted. To the
surprise of all, however, the old man lived some
14 years afterwards. The Doctor was one of the
most unselfish, unbusinesslike, and gentle creatures
that ever lived, and I am not sorry that he has
gone where he will be better appreciated.—Yours,
Artist.
Allan M‘Lean, the new Victorian Chief
Secretary, is Scotch, and comes of a Catholic clan;
but from a devout Roman he became a Pres-
byterian. The M’Lean family settled in Gipps-
land in the fifties and long occupied the bleak
and sterile run called the “ Lowlands,” between
the Ninety-mile Beach and Sale. Next to them
were the Macdonalds, and on the Latrobe River
were the Campbells, who, ignoring the reason
why they should adopt any other name than that,
especially in such propinquity, called their squat-
tage “ Glencoe.” The M'Leans were starved out
of the “Lowlands” by foot-rot and fluke, and
Allan took to auctioneering at Sale and
Maffra, and incidentally to political economy. In
the evolution of time he became a Minister in
Munro’s Cabinet, and now he.is ancient to Slnels.
Mr. M‘Lean bears the reputation of being honest,
genial and modest.
George Cheeke’s father (brother of the
late N S W. Judge) and the late Lord Sandys are
the two benefactors to whom we owe that
universally-popular compound, Worcestershire
sauce They were old cronies, and hons vivants to
boot, and between them they concocted the sauce,
They used to get it made up by Lea, who was a
chemist in Worcester, and after a time he asked
for permission to sell it, promising to keep the
mixture secret. Little thinking what they were
giving away, they consented. The rest is known,
Lea died a millionaire, and big fortunes have been
made out of the sauce since, Mr. Lea’s gratitude
ran the length of sending to each of them a dozen
’ bottles every year, and that is all they got out of
it. On each bottle may be read “ Prepared from
the recipe of a nobleman in the county.” That
was Lord Sandys ; but it was Mr. Cheeke who
suggested the ingredient which really made the
sauce what it is.

The intimation of the tolerably lar 0 e
amount (,£34,000) left by the late Bishop t erry,
formerly of Melbourne, recalls that he refused
large grants of land from Gov Latrobe for
“ glebe” or church purposes. Latrobe a regular
stickler for precedents, and a goody-goody to boot,
believed in the State endowment of religion
especially of the Protestant section He gave
Perry to understand that a formal application for
glebe lands in every parish would meet with Ins
strongestapproval, and as constitutional machinery
was then only just getting into working order,
such enormous powers rested in the Governor’s
hands that there is no doubt the grants would
have been made. To the huge loss of the Anglican
Church in Victoria, however, and to the corres-
gain of the community a. large, the
Bishop held the extreme evangelical belief which
trusts directly to Providence. He refused to act
on Latrobe’s suggestion, and assured his Excel-
lency “ that the Lord would provide for the wants
of His servants.” Fine old Alfred Woolley (to
whose intelligent philanthropy Melbourne charities
of the genuine sort owe so much—he used to give
first and enquire afterwards), when the Bishop
told him of the offer, months later, remarked
“Why, Bishop, the Lord was then and there
making provision for the Church !” Woolley,
speaking of the incident to the writer, said that
the Bishop was confounded at that view of the
matter—“it had never occurred to him!” The
Victorians ought to be grateful to Perry’s mental
density. The first Anglican Bishop of N.S.W.
was dense enough in all, conscience, but when it
was a question of getting at the State, he was all
there—and always.

David Lindsay, the commander of the
unlucky “Elder Exploring Expedition,” is a most
religious man, but his incessant prayers for rain
seemed to have such an effect upon his subor-
dinates (Afghans included) that when they
reached the first settlement (Geraldton) they
resigned almost to a man. And this costly expe-
dition, fitted out by Sir Thomas Elder, has accom-
plished nothing of any consequence. Apropos ,
Explorer Forrest, in 1875, had neither camels nor
Afghans to accompany him across the desert.
Yet his journals aye full of interest and scientific
discovery. His mm never mutinied nor resigned.
Neither did Giles’, and both crossed much of the
country explored by Xmdsay.

She met Jacques and she loved him. It lasted
six months. They were brought together m
springtime, they parted in the autumn. 1 rancxne
was consumptive ; she knew it, and Jacques knew
it also. A fortnight after the young girl came to
him he learned it from one of his friends, who was
a doctor. “She will leave you when the leaves
become yellow,” he said.
Francine surprised this confidence and perceived
the despair of her friend.
“Why trouble about the yellow leaves?” said
she, with all her love smiling on her lips. “ Why
trouble about the autumn —we are in the midst of
summer and the leaves are green; profit by it,
my friend. When you see me preparing to leave
the world fold me in your arms with a kiss and
forbid me to go. lam obedient, you know, and
will stay.”
And thus, for five months, Francine endured
all the miseries of a Bohemian life with a laugh
and a song on her lips. _ .
As for Jacques, he let himself be deceiyed. His
friend would sometimes say : “ Francine is getting
worse, take care of her.” Then Jacques would
scour Paris seeking the wherewithal to carry out
the doctor’s orders; but Francine would never
hear it spoken of, and threw their drugs out of
the window. At night, when the cough laid hold
of her, she would creep from the room and on to
the landing so that Jacques might not hear her.
One day when they were together in the country,
Jacques noticed a tree whose foliage was already
fading. He glanced sadly at Francine who was
moving slowly—dreaming by his side.
She saw him change countenance, and under-
stood the reason of his pallor. ...
“ How foolish you are ! ” said she kissing him,
“ this is but July ; there are three months yet to
October ; loving one another —as we do —night and
day, we shall double the time that we have to pass
together. Besides, if I feel myself getting worse
as the leaves turn yellow, we will go and live in a
wood whose leaves are evergreen.
In October Francine was not able to leave her
bed. Their friend, the doctor, attended her. The
little room in which they lodged was high up in
the house and looked out upon a courtyard, where
grew a tree whose foliage disappeared day by day.
Jacques had hung a curtain across the window in
order to hide this tree from the sick girl; but
Francine ordered him to remove it.
“ Oh, my love,” she said, “I will give you a
hundred times more kisses than it has leaves,”
and she added, “besides, I am getting much
better ; I shall be able to go out soon ; but as it is
sure to be cold, and I hate to have red hands, you
must buy me a muff ’’—and through all her illness
this muff was her only whim.
On All Saints’ Eve, seeing that Jacques was
sadder than ever, and in order to cheer him, she
got up.
Just then the doctor arrived and forced her
back to bed.
“Jacques,” whispered he to the artist, be
brave ! this is the end. Francine must die.”
Jacques burst into tears.
“ Give her anything she wants now,” added the
doctor, “ there is no hope.”
Francine heard with her eyes what the doctor
had told her lover.
“Do not listen to him,” she cried, stretching
h.er arms towards Jacques. “Do not listen to
him, he lies. We will go out together to-morrow.
It is All Saints’ Day ; it will be cold, go and buy
my muff. Do go, I am afraid of getting chil-
blains this winter.” . ...
Jacques was going out with his friend, but
Francine kept the doctor beside her.
“ Go and get my muff,” she said to her lover ;
“ get me a nice one to last a long time.”
And when she was alone with the doctor
“ Oh, sir, I know I am dying, but before my time
comes can you not give me back my strength for
a night—only make me beautiful again for a
night, and then I can die, since it is the will of
the good God that I may live no longer ?”
As the doctor was trying to soothe her, an
autumn breeze swept into the room and threw on
the sick girl’s bed a yellow leaf—torn from the
tree in the little courtyard.
Francine threw the curtain aside and saw that
the tree was completely bare.
“It is the very last one!” said she, slipping
the leaf beneath her pillow. #
“ You will last till to-morrow,” said the doctor,
“ you have still a night before you.”
“Ah, what happiness !” said the young girl;
“ a winter night; it will be long.”
Jacques came in with her muff.
“What a pretty one,” said she, “ I will put it
on when I go out with you.”
The night passed.
The morrow, All Saints Day, at the midday
Angelus, convulsions seized her, her whole body
trembled.
“My hands are cold,” she whispered, “Give
me my muff.” And she buried her poor little
hands in its fur.
“ This is the end,” said the doctor to Jacques—
“ go and kiss her.”
Jacques pressed his lips to those of his lover.
At the last moment he wished to draw the muff
away, but she held it tight.
“No, no,” she said, “let me keep it, it is
winter, I am cold, oh, so cold ; ah, my poor boy,
ah, my poor Jacques; what will become of you ?”
And Jacques was left alone.— From the French
of Henri Murger.

LIFE OF C. S. PARNELL,
By T. P. O’CONNOR, M.P.
Price (post jree), Is. 9A.
“Mr. O’Connor hgs certainly surpassed himself in the
writing of this intensely interesting work. . . . No
newspaper notice, however full, can give any adequate
impression of the career as is here depicted of the great
Irishman who has gone. Mr. O’Connor’s hook itself should
be in the hands of everyone who is interested in the
brilliant and stormy career which is ended for ever.
A delightful, graphic and biographical sketch of one of
the most remarkable figures of the century.”—Dublin
Freeman’s Journal,
Finn £3 a.- o . &: Co.,
W Ogo roe Street, SYDNEY.
Two absolute essentials for the existence
of the human race are—land and air. Many
economic and political systems have been invented
and are advocated for obtaining land for each
individual to live upon, but it has been left to the
Hydraulic Cooling and Ventilating Co.to intro-
duce pure air into every habitation in Australia,
by means of their “Perfection” Cooler and
Ventilator. By attaching this machine to your
water supply or tank, and simply turning on the
tap, a constant stream of pure, fresh air can be
obtained in any chamber you desire. Punkahs
are thus superseded, perishable goods can be kept
at a small expense without ice, and you can enjoy
the luxury of a cool chamber on the hottest day or
night. The machine, which is simplicity itself,
works at the cost of one half-penny per day, while
the price brings it within the reach of most.
Send for particulars or inspect the machines work-
ing in our offices. T. A. Knight and Co., St.
-James Chambers, King-street, Sydney.
Society
Thus a London correspondent There
has always been a wide-spread belief in Sydney
that a certain individual whose name it will be
unnecessary to mention is a full-blown baronet in
his own right, and, if I am not mistaken, when
his son some years ago married a wife, he an-
nounced himself in the first column of the Herald
as “Son of Sir So-and-so, Bart.” I have never
been able to find the name in any copy of the
baronetage which I have come across, and I
happened to mention the fact to “ Tony ” Foster,
who is a recognised authority on such matters,
being the proprietor of one of those “ books for
snobs.” He laughed and said: “My dear boy,
you may depend upon it that every man who lias
an atom of a claim to a title is in my book. You
may take it from me that your friend is not a
baronet.” Thus was another idol shattered;
another cherished illusion dispelled. Now, if my
friend (the mention of whose name brings back
recollections of delicious cocktails, which he so
deftly concocted) has any wish to have his name
recorded in Debrett or Burke, he must at once
send in his claim properly authenticated.
* * *
There is a certain Victorian baronet, by
the way, whose right to a title is extremely doubt*
ful, and one at least of the compilers of a baronet-
age has serious thoughts of omitting his Ham®
from the new edition.
* * *
In a cricket match played last week
between ladies and gentlemen at Neutral Bay,
Sydney, the local parson played on the side of the
gentlemen. Of course the ladies—included among
whom was W. G. Grace’s daughter—won by one
run.
* * *
The Maoriland Primate is dead against
giving the ecclesiastical franchise to women, and
says that if the bdl proposing to do so, now before
the Synod, be read a second time, he will resign.
Poor man ! The opinion of the general public id
that the ecclesiastical franchise should be pos l
sessed only by the parsons and the women,
* * *
A recent big Sydney defaulter has, it is
said, been assisted by his father-in-law to the
extent of many thousands. However, when all
was disclosed, the old man, finding his inclina-
tions and his resources alike unequal to so gigantic
a strain, was forced to throw the ruined gambler
to the wolves. One of the worst aspects of the
case is that the honourable and popular men with
whom the offender has been professionally asso-
ciated suffer severely by his misdeeds. The
defaulter’s fall again illustrates the fable of tiro
boy and the nuts.
* * *
The servant-girl question is greatly exer-
cising the gilded dames of Hobart, who would im-
port the genuine article from ’Ome wholesale only
for the preliminary difficulty of putting down ,£lB
passage-money per head for the abigails. As a
warning to importers of general servants from
England the experience of a prominent Tasmanian
M.P. stands high. He and his wife recently
brought out with them three picked girls for their
household. Tiring of the quiet monotony of
Hobart, the three damsels advantaged themselves
of the missus’s absence to depart silently for Mel-
bourne after obtaining their cheques from the
master—who has since been rescued from his
mansion in an emaciated condition whilst trying
to eat chops of his own cooking brought home in
his hat.
# # *
In a new English novel called “A Jealous
Woman,” the hero (an Australian), is described
as “very simple and unsophisticated, like all his
countrymen who have not had the advantage of
attending an English school.” The same novel
first describes the hero as only having one arm,
and at the finish says that “ he sat with his head
upon his hands, the personification of despon-
dency.”
* * #
A painful sight in Bourke-street, Mel-
bourne, last week was the collapse of a splendid
collie dog from the heat. The animal dropped
suddenly dead while following his master.
* * *
The insolvency of a certain money-lender
recalls an early incident of his career as a fin-
ancier. Two youths, relatives, one nineteen, the
other twenty, scions of wealthy families in the
Mudgee district, found themselves “stone-broke”
on the last day of a big Sydney race-meeting, and
applied to Mr. (let us call him) Napthali for .£2OO
on a jointly-signed bill. He “did” it—at one
month, and they had to sign for .£3OO. They laid
the .£2OO on the family horse, won <£Boo, and
within three days turned up again at Napthali’s
to tender him £250, and ask him to cry quits.
He declined, demanded his pound of flesh, and
said he could afford to wait the full month. Then
they told him he was a disgrace to his tribe, that
they were both under-age, and that they would
pay him nothing—to which resolution they have
religiously adhered ever since. Had they paid
him his .£3OO, and had he been able to invest it at
a similar rate of interest, and stick to the pro-
ceeds, he would now be a millionaire instead of a
bankrupt. There is nothing meaner than a bank-
rupt usurer.
* # *
Under the style of The Royal Auglo-
Australian Society of Artists a local syndicate
has just opened what is practically a foreign
picture-gallery and market at Melbourne Exhibi-
tion. It is mainly Anglo, and in a minor degree
German. Where the Australian colour is to come
mit would be difficult to say. Melbourne artists,
anyhow, had no official intimation of the Society’s
existence until five days before the opening, when
they received a circular asking them to kindly
send along any works not previously exhibited.
Obviously the promoters have not laid themselves
out to assist Australian art.

Seventeen persons were reported as lepers
in N.S.W. during 1891, and ten of them were
found really to have leprosy. One of the five
whites died very soon after his discovery. The
number, detained on 31st December was 21, of
whom eight were whites—six male and two
female. Blessed be the Chow cheap labour wlvch
hath brought about this promising commence-
ment ! A few more years of unrestricted Chowism
and all Australia would-be feeling its ears every
morning for signs of leprous thickening.

GRANDCHILD: “G’ma, me loves ’oo.”
GRANDMA : “My love, ray pet.”
GRANDCHILD: “G’ma, has ’oo got any beenanas? ”
We have received the first number of the
Woman, a monthly journal issued in .Sydney and
intended to advocate the interests of the sex. It
is a nice-looking little publication, and is run by
two men. There is no cast-iron reason why a
woman's paper should be run by a woman—if
there was, then a pastoral journal ought to be
edited by a hen and a bull; but still, as Woman
must be to some extent a protest against the over-
bearing attitude assumed by man, it seems a pity
that it should have to hire two men to enter that
protest. Nobody can ever kick himself half so
well as he can be kicked by somebody else.

Someone enquires what has become of the
alleged musician who, when the health of his
successful rival was proposed at Sydney Town
Hall, boorishly remained glued to his seat.

The other morning a Sydney surveyor
found in answer to an advt. for an axe-man, about
200 men outside his office. He engaged a duly
qualified civil engineer at a weekly wage of 255.
and “ tucker ”■—a good man, out of work, glad to
get anything to keep his family from starving.

At Elsternwick (Vic.) a girl suicided the
other day by lying down in front of a train. “ A
slight disagreement with George Snooks, brick-
layer,” is announced as the cause of her death.
# *
During the recent convulsion in Japan the
shocks continued for eight days at an average rate
of 34 earthquakes an hour. Evidently Pastor
Sol tan was praying at very high pressure.
# # #
Some heavy rains which took place
recently in Dunedin (M.L.) led to a revelation of
one more of the Unsuspected horrors that hang
like a pall over otir provisions. One of the sewers
in the principal street got blocked, and as the
adjacent buildings were rapidly becoming Hooded
tremendous efforts Were made to get at the
obstruction. Then it was discovered that a
gentleman in the fish trade, probably with a view
to saving the cost of ice-chests, was in the habit of
enclosing his fine fresh soles and flounders and
barraconta in boxes, and lowering them into the
cool, damp, clammy drain among the refuse, and
the rats, the cold mephitic gases, the ancient pig’s
feet, and other horrors. Truly the only decent
way to live is to be a fasting man, or else to set up
as a cannibal and select your dinner fresh every
day as it runs past your door after taking its bath.
If you eat meat the chances are that tin- butcher
has sat on it in his cart, and if you eat fish it may
have come out of a sewer. When you buy tea
some Chinaman has had his feet in it, and Heaven
only knows what kind of individual made the
bread —whether he did it with his fingers or his
toes. Then strange niggers have been fooling
around with the sugar and the coffee —in fact
every kind of provision has some ghastly horror of
its own attached to it. And, now we come to
think of it, it is only a few weeks since we saw
an advertisement in the Age about an owner
wanted for a set of false teeth discovered in a
mud-punt.
* * *
There is a very large Australian contingent
at Nice and Monte Carlo this year. “ Colonials,”
however, as a rule don’t gamble.
* * #
Martin Loughlin was run in for the Junior
Athenfeum when he was last in London, and
entertained a good deal there. Martin had a
narrow escape of being hooked by a certain buxom
silver-haired widow, the disconsolate relict of a
well-known University professor.
* * #
There is in a Sydney suburb a married
religious crank who will eat fire or break eggs era
long. The other day he planted his month-old
baby up to its darling neck in the cold earth,
then, kneeling beside it, he lifted up his voice in
exultant hallelujahs, and holding a watering-pot
in his right hand turned a shower-bath on the
cherub’s desolate head. He wished to force its
growth; The poor, helpless, blessed &c., was
rescued by its rna. This crank, however, can’t
compare with Brisbane’s highly original and
humorously-demented religionist, who often
interrupts a whole congregation with “Mr.
Parson ! My Lord, ask the dotty rascal in front to
leave the church. Yesterday he told me to go to
- . ’ Or else, “Lady Angelina Simper, stop
that cursed grinning while I pray.” Or, to some
Urchin : “ Master Impudence, refrain from thrust-
ing out thy damned tongue at me.” Religion
plays the devil with some men.
* # #
Mr. James Harp, a very old identity who
died recently at Auckland (M.L,), aged 89, was
celebrated in earlier days-for the way in which he
kept his accounts. He owned about the first store
that existed in the city, and traded in almost
everything. In those primitive times Harp
couldn’t afford a book-keeper, and, therefore, he
kept his own books on a plan of his own invention.
Being the owner of a much-neglected education,
he was accustomed, when he gave Credit, to make
a sketch of the customer in a huge, dirty ledger,
and alongside it he drew the article sold. Very
often nobody save himself could identify the
customer, for Mr. Harp’s cartoons were wild and
erratic ; sometimes the artist himself lost the run
of the subject, and then he trusted to his memoiy.
In one case, when the buyer positively refused to
recognise an account for a cheese, Harp lost his
temper, and sued him for the money. He pro-
duced his ledger as corroborative evidence, and
there, sure enough, in one corner was a sketch of
a horrible-looking man, with no body and his legs
joined right on to his head, running away with a
rollnd object which might have been a cheese, or,
again, it might have been a wheel or a hoop. The
defendant cheerfully admitted that the man was
probably meant for him, but he positively denied
all knowledge of the circular substance. There
was a long pause while the court thought hard
over the evidence. Then suddenly Mr. Harp
lifted up his voice in considerable excitement.
“My word,” he said, “it wasn’t a cheese, it was
a grindstone. I disremembered to put in the
hole.” The defendant remembered the grind-
stone, and Jim got his money.
» # #
The name of bachelor is sometimes a
reproach among men. A bachelor in N.S.W. Civil
Service, who lately applied for an increase in
salary, was informed by his official chief that
although his abilities warranted an increase, such
increase could not be granted because the appli-
cant was not a married man. A certain member
of the Queensland Land Board used to take the
same stand towards his officers when he filled the
post of Surveyor-General.
* # #
He was 80 and wealthy, and she 20 and
penniless, and she married him, notwithstanding
she had a fond and youthful lover. He died and
left her .£l5O weekly so long as she remained un-
married, the sum to be reduced to one pound if
she remarried. And she won’t re-marry—to the
profound disgust of his adult children, who would
then come in for the cash. But the course of true
love smoothly runs for all that. The lover has
his lass, and they snap their fingers at Mrs.
Grundy and live happily, if unconventionally.
* # *
In Tasmania they are asking what the
decorated old gentleman means to do with the
twins—whether he intends to exhibit them. They
are not alluding to the twins of Pastor Soltau,
of earthquake celebrity, but to a case in which
twins constitute ill themselves an earthquake—a
social one.
* * #
Two leading N.S.W. families have just
received a very shocking surprise through the
wayward conduct of a very pretty and fascinating
country cousin.
* * *
Wo took up the other day a newspaper
containing a list of directors of a proposed labour-
bank, published but a few short months ago. One
of the directors is now doing seven years’ gaol for
conspiracy ; another has absconded, it is alleged,
with money. Labour’s! riends! Andrew Fitzgerald,
who stole a large amount of money from the
N.S.W. Labour Co-operative Store Co., of which
he was manager, protested his innocence, though
the case against him was as clear as daylight.
Judge Coffey gave him five years—which is pretty
rough on an innocent man.
* # *
It is understood that the prosecution of
the V.D.L. Bank authorities will depend on the
result of the investigation now being made by a
Government commission, as to the alleged issue
of a false balance-sheet.
# # *
People are wondering at the indecent
attitude assumed in a Sydney court last week
by Stipendiary Giles in a case in which police-
men were charged with having accepted bribes
from a totalisator-man. The Bulletin expresses
no opinion concerning the particular case under
review, but considering the enormous amount of
business openly and illegally done by the totalisa-
tors, &c., the conclusion is almost irresistible that
somebody has been well “greased.”
* * #
One who considers the material utilised to
make telegraph-operators, will not wonder at
orthographical errors in telegrams. The assistant-
manager of the Sydney office is grievously worried
through the stupidity of the boys who are sent up
to the operating-room as messengers. Having
exhausted his patience one day, he complained to
the manager, characterised the boys as a lot of
blockheads, and asserted that not one in twenty
could tell him where London or New York was.

Somebody sends us
the programme of a
Catholic church art-union
or lottery swindle, and
one of the statements on

and contributors will
participate in the numer-
,ms Masses and Prayers
of the Fathers.” This
ooks well; the man who
Iraws a shapeless pair of
naby’s shoes or a toast-
rack will have his soul
poked after into the
oargain, and the more
plentiful individual who
doesn’t draw anything
will at least have the
unseen spiritual influence
of the Church thrown in.
But we knew a man who
■vent into exactly the
.s..iue kinri of a raffle once,
and someuow lie seemed
to have got the numerous
curses ot the Fathers in-
stead of their prayers. In
the first place, he never
heard the result of the
drawing Secondly, lie
took ten tickets in a
Cup sweep, thinking that
the Church might get

married a blu-hing girl

after only three days’
acquaintance, and found
immediately afterwards
that she was a widow
(with four children in
Sydney). And now lie
wants to know where the
assistance of the Fathers
comes in, and so does the
writer, for the only time
heeve r had the assistance
of a Father was when
he asked one of them to
tell him the shortest road
to Mansfield (Vic.).
Whereupon the good
man, being buried in
profound contemplation
and thinking of the
saints, directed him on the
wrong track, and he
walked 16 miles over a
horribly rough- country
and fell over a precipice,
and arrived in a strange
place where he was nearly
32 miles from his clean
shirt, and he had to sleep
in a deserted hut and
walk back next day.

A daily literary organ
informed the Mel-
bourne public the other
day that the Rev. C. H.
Spurgeon was “ laying in
state.” Which reminds
us that the dailies, with scarcely an exception,
always say “different to” instead of “different
from.” By the way, when the Prince of Wales
wrote to his friend, Lady Mordaunt, he told her
to be sure and lay up.
# # #
Amongst the deaths recently announced
was that of little Levi Mandelson, of the Tumut.
His father used to keep the best hotel in Goul-
burn in byegone days. Old Mandelson was a
great man in Freemasonry, and the local lodge
was held in his house. A certain Civil-servant
had been induced to join the mystic brotherhood,
and, of course, had to undergo the usual chaff
about hot pokers and branding, &o. He was
nervous and when the eventful evening arrived
was very much excited, and his friends, seeing
this, of course did little to calm him. Mandelson
was a very fussy old fellow, and most particular
about keeping his furniture in good order. Whilst
the candidate was being prepared Mandelson was
busy getting the lodge fixed up, and some clumsy
person upset a candle (there was no gas in those
days) and spilt a lot of grease on the seat of a
chair. The old man at once rushed out of the
room and called at the top of his voice to the
waiter downstairs ; “John, quick, bring up a hot
iron and some brown paper.” The candidate
heard this, visions of a speedy realisation of his
fears flashed before him, so he bolted out of the
tyler’s charge and disappeared, not waiting
to get back the small articles of which he had
been “ deprived,” nor did he even take his coat.
Nothing would ever persuade him that they did
not brand Masons, nor did he ever become one.

• # •
It is time for reporters to drop the sensa-
tional heading of “Sacrilege!” The Croajingo-
long Cathedral may be robbed of a pewter-pot,
but that is no reason why the law should add an
extra three months for the “sacrilege.” Just let
theft be theft, or otherwise you will have sectarians
declaring that acts of sacrilege with respect to any
other sect are good works.
# * *
A good yarn of two dead Melbourne men
tells how Archbishop Goold was disappointed in
his hopes of inducing millionaire Tommy Monahan
to give freely of his substance to the one true
Church. Tommy’s extreme backwardness in
this respect was a matter of such painful
notoriety, that the prelate decided to attack him
in person, to which end he consulted with the
Monahan family confessor, and arranged to give
a little dinner at the palace in Tommy’s honour.
That wily old bird accepted the invitation, rolled
up for his free feed, and had put away a bottle of
the best before Dr. Goold broached the subject
nearest to his pious heart. The advantages
appertaining to the laying up of treasure in
Heaven were pointed out to Tommy with due
solemnity. “A man of your means,” said the
Archbishop, courteously pushing the decanter
along, “has it in his power to accomplish
a work that, whilst pleasing God, would
hand down his name to the admiration of
posterity. Provide the funds necessary for the
completion of St. Patrick’s Cathedral, and com-
mand the honour and gratitude of future genera-
tions ! ” The ungodly capitalist scratched his head,
and filled his glass slowly as his countenance
sickbed o’er with the pale cast of thought. “ And
about how much would it cost to com plate ? ” he
enquired. “ Fifty thousand pounds would do the
work handsomely,” says Goold. An awful pause
ensued. “ I’ll let you have it,” says Tommy, and
the Archbishop’s nervous hands congratulated one
another under the table. “Yes,” continued the
remorseless Thomas, “I’ll let you have it—at six
percent.” The meeting broke up shortly after-
wards, broke up entirely.
Which reminds us that several land-boom
“donations” to the St. Patrick’s building fund
have remained on paper up to the present day.
The wife of Arthur Wells, of Condowie
Plains (S.A.), has had triplets. Latest advices
report all well—except Mr. Wells.
# * *
The Melbourne Charity Organisation
Society, having made an exhaustive examination
of the “unemployed” question, finds that “no
information has been placed before it which jus-
tifies the establishment of any special relief
organisation.” The men of charity cheerfully
admit the existence of unemployed, in large
numbers, but their neglect to answer circulars is
greatly against them. Also the Society considers
that there may be “ more real misery quietly and
uncomplainingly endured among clerks and shop-
men who are disengaged owing to dullness in
commercial circles than among any other class,”
although no satisfactory particulars on this point
have come under the secretary’s notice. Conse-
quently the Society regrets its inability to prepare
any reliable estimates of local distress and starva-
tion, but enjoys the smug satisfaction of believing
that for every hungry man out of work there is
another man even more hungry, wherefore it is
best assisting the cause of charity by standing
afar off and issuing pamphlets.
# # *
English photos, of the late Duke of Clarence
and his betrothed, arms linked, form the latest
draw on Melbourne “block.” Deceased’s collar
is certainly the tallest bit of laundry work ever
photographed, and the lady is as plainly pleasant
as he is pasty and lustreless. In the next row,
Prince George, with full beard and flowing
moustache, looks several degrees more healthy
and wholesome.

They put a steaming hot poultice on a
Kanaka at Prince Alfred Hospital, Sydney, the
other day. At once he tore it off. He wasn’t
going to be cooked and eaten.
* # #
A correspondent points out that Chief
Justice Higinbotham, in his letter declining the
responsibility of the presidentship, didn’t recom-
mend the exclusion of newspaper proprietors from
the Victorian Institute of Journalists. He merely
told the pressmen to elect, not a figure-head pre-
sident, but a man .who would take an active
personal interest in the Institute.

One of the most painful sights at a first
night of Italian opera is the lady critic with the
score, who gabbles incessantly in a loud voice to
the gentleman sitting beside her, wags her head
to the time, taps her toe, and sways her body in
imitation of the conductor, and, at the conclusion
of a number, informs everybody within ten-feet
radius what she thinks of it. The lady who eats
• cliocolate-creams out of a tinsel-box, and uses a
powder-puff between the acts, is an angel of
dazzling sweetness compared with the other
variety of harpy.

the Argus. Two gentlemen drove in a buggy tc
the Red Bluff, and attracted an audience of ladies
and children by practising athletic feats, aftet
which they stripped and had a sea-bath, winding
up by measuring each other round the chest.
But why didn’t the ladies run away ?

The Launceston Exhibition Committee
charges a fee of one guinea upon each wine exhibit
that is offered for judgtnent. The Wodonga (Vic.)
Wine and Fruit Growers’ Association met the
demand for fifteen guineas in fees with a request
that their collection of tipple might be promptly
returned. Why, an average exhibition medal is
seldom worth twenty-one shillings, if you happen
to get your “Extra first-class double special
award of Superior Merit.” They seem to be verra
Scottish indeed down at Launceston.
“All branches of the Windham family
have a seat reserved at each meal for the Second
Coming of Christ, and have kept up the custom
for years.” Faith or egotism?
Angus and Robertson send us an extraor-
dinary production entitled “ Australia Felix ; or,
Sir Valiant Love.” This booklet is called a
drama, and the author wisely withholds his name.
The hero is an impossible caricature called Lord
Chumley, with an alias, and the story is carried
on in blank verse, rhymed couplets, bald prose,
and various cuts and slices of every description of
metre.
Mr. J. L. Hance, with the assistance of
Messrs. Edward Lee and Co., steam printers, 53
Market-street, Sydney, has taken the Cosmos in
hand, and published a book. The title is sonorous
and voluminous, and the book comprises much
encyclopaedic information. The “First, Second,
Third, Fourth, Fifth and Sixth Numbers of the
Atlas Series of Australian Pamphlets entitled the
End of the World or Prophecy, a Lost Science,
Recovered,” by J.L.H., is a wild, weird, wonder-
ful and erudite production, dealing with theology,
theosophy, democracy, theocracy, prophecy, poli-
tical economy, Israel, Assyria, the third power,
the fourth dimension of space, Joshua, revelation,
restoration, electro-chemistry, sesthenogen and
zoogen, einaigen, mind and matter, the solar
system, the spirits of the spheres, the Mosaic
account of creation, the origin of organic life, the
origin of animals and man, the origin and destiny
of the mind, physics a*nd metaphysics, an address
to the world’s workers, allegories, analogies, exhor-
tations, the mischief of apathy, vigilance com-
mittees, local tribunes, strikes and lockouts, the
Single-tax, labour organisations, industrial regi-
ments, woman’s rights and female suffrage, the
education of youth, Freetrade and Protection,
religion and politics, &c., to., illustrated by
marvellous diagrams educed from biblical history.
The only thing the writer has not touched on is
wash-leather, and we are not sure that he has not
tackled that somewhere in connection with the
mystery of the Nowheresomenessof the Somehow.
All this lore can be purchased for the moderate
sum of six shillings, and any purchaser not satis-
fied with his bargain can obtain enlightenment on
any other subject under or over Heaven by
addressing a letter to the gifted author.
O, listen to our tale of woe!
Religion hasn’t got a show
’Gainst childhood’s carnal follies ;
And pennies for the heathen meant,
Too oft, alas, dear friends, are spent
In apples or in lollies.
For sweet religion’s truths sublime
(Which ought to “ get there” every time),
The kids don t care a fig; and
Each Stiggins gets upon his feet
And howls, because he can’t compete
Against the ice-cream brigand.

“ The War-Atah ; an Australian Legend,’ 1
by Luden Henry, published by Neal, Paris, is a
romantic conception of the origin of that flower,
told in the guise of an aboriginal myth. The
language employed by the author is rich and
florid, and evinces a remarkable command, in a
French artist, of the resources of the English
tongue; while the story itself shows a wide range
of reading, a vividness of imaginative power and
an originality of treatment too frequently dis-
sociated from works depending for their interest
on themes selected from the folk-lore of savages.
The author illustrates his own volume with five
original designs, the reproductions of which, how-
ever, are not so fine as they might have been,
M. Henry is well known in Sydney as Art
Lecturer of the Sydney Technical College, and
is now in Europe preparing for the publication
of his great work on the Australian Decorative
Arts, a paper on which, by Mr. J. R. Tranthim- j
Fryer, was recently read by Lady Hamilton, at
Hobart, before the Australasian Association for
the Advancement of Science.
An Adelaide jury has found a verdict of
“ not guilty ” in the case of the proprietors of our
h vely contemporary Quiz, charged with libelling
Gerald and Duff, actors. Quiz stated that the
theatrical pair got up a benefit for the Broken
Hill fire brigade and “bolted with the proceeds
down to the last halfpenny.” As Gerald and
Duff did not, it would appear, bolt with any half-
penny save their own this looks a most
comprehensive and self-evident thing in the way
of libel—in fact, it would seem to any ordinary
intelligence that a defence in such a case was use-
less. Nevertheless the paper’s lawyer (a rising
youth named Piper) blandly asserted that
the words “bolted with the proceeds” were
absolutely innocent and harmless; he almost
implied that they were complimentary. Then he
claimed that there was a want of evidence to show
who were the owners of Quiz, and presently it
began to appear as if the paper simply walked
about loose with no owners at all. After that—
we here respectfully salute that lawyer-the jury
brought in a verdict of “not guilty” which either
means that the men whose names appear on the
paper and who take the money, if any, are in no
way responsible and can say what they like with
impunity, or else that it isn’t libellous to say
falsely that one man has bolted with another
man’s money. The points on which the defendants
relied, and on which, apparently, they got off,
remind us very much of the point on which another
man is alleged to have got off in New Caledonia
years ago. He was about to be guillotined, but at the
last moment he raised the objection that the basket
into which his head was to fall contained sawdust
instead of bran as provided by a custom having
the force of law. He was remanded that bran
might be obtained, but none was procurable, and
the authorities decided, after a week or so, that he
was entitled to bran and must have it. Then,
bran having arrived he was brought out again for
execution, but this time raised another point—
that having been ordered to be guillotined on a
certain day, and that day having passed, he
couldn’t be lawfully guillotined unless the same
day c uld be brought back again. This time he
got off altogether. Somehow the proprietors of
Qnz set us thinking of that man ; it is never hope-
less to take a law point.
