SPORTING NOTIONS'
When the Irish team were training for the
Sydney Tug-of-War, the greatest difficulty experienced
was in obtaining opponents who could tax their powers to
«nv extent. First fifteen men were pitted against them,
hut they flew after the Irishers like chaff before the wind.
Then twenty-two were tried, but with little better suc-
cess and a trial of twenty-five only gave them trouble for
fifteen minutes. This was not considered good enough
to iret them into condition, so next night the end of the
roue behind the twenty-five was passed by their trainer
through a hole bored in the fence, and a couple of turns
taken round a post. For over an hour did the Irish team
perform, the rope being paid out to them an inch at a
time, when the twenty-five fresh men would make it good
again for the men at the post to take up the slack. The
best performance of the team, however, was with their
tongues when they heard about the post in the next yard.
# # *
Alluding to Mr. B. R. Wise’s departure for
England recently, a contemporary significantly says he
was the greatest mile-runner in the world. Now B.R.W.’s
victory over George in 1881 was creditable, but George
was off-colour at the time, and the year before or the year
after could have shown Wise a clean pair of heels. We
have now in Australia two men who were better than
Wise at the distance. These are Walter Slade, ex-
champion amateur, in a prosperous Melbourne business,
and Livingstone, at Newcastle, who in Scotland was
almost equal to “Wullie” Cummings. By the way,
Petley, the second swiftest sprinter ever seen at Sheffield
is somewhere about. What a show he in his best form
could have made of our Botany heroes—black and white !
# • •
Parks is the correct name of the Australian who
went home and met Nat Langhain, although he always
fought under the name of “Sparks.” The old man is
Btilfalive and lively, and the descendants of the brothers,
Tom and Bill Parks, are as plentiful as blackberries out
Hurstville and Canterbury (N.S.W.) way.
* # •
The pitting of Ironbark Burge against clever
Jack Hall is in the interests of neither boxing nor
humanity. It Hall is in anything like his old form the
fight will simply resolve itself into a test of the amount
of smashing Burge can endure before Hall falls weak or
breaks his hands. That Burge can beat Hall -ifhe is the
Hall of old—is too remote a chance to waste time in
discussing. Such a fight is sheer brutality and should be
stopped by the police.
• # *
English Boxer Baxter gave Australia a bad
name when he returned to England, and conveyed to his
interviewers an idea that Hades is not a circumstance
besides Australia for heat. Had he told the truth he
would have confessed that it was the boxers and not the
climate which he found too hot.
* * »
The deaths of the ponies, Sophie and Windsor
Lass, and the injuries to their riders will probably be the
cause of an order issuing that ponies are in the future
only to gallop one way on the Sydney Driving Park track.
# * *
A weekly says a faro hell is carried on in a
dummy tobacconist’s shop in the heart of Sydney city,
and the description of the premises leaves no doubt as to
the locale. Mr. Inspector-General Fosbery should not
give faro a chance to get hold in Sydney, it being one of
the worst gambling games ever invented.
* * *
Jack Barnett, after many years of travelling
with, and abuse from, John L. Sullivan, has severed his
connection with the champion boozer. Barnett and Sul-
livan have never forgiven Australia for guying their show,
and the former says that America bets £2OO for every £1
betted by Australia, notwithstanding the booming of
Australia as a land of sport.
* # •
The old-time Sydney light-weight, George
Powell, beat Barnes, of New Yor—we moan of Maoriland
in nine rounds at Newcastle (N.S.W.) on Saturday
night. One of Barnes’ ribs was broken in the fight.
# * *
Dear Bulletin, —Referring in a recent issue to
a swallow killed by A. Trott while bowling you say “ This
is the first ball-bird shot on record.” W. Alexander, play-
ing for Windsor against Emu on the latter’s ground a few
years ago, while batting drove a ball which killed a
swallow.— Saddlestrap.
* * *
Slavin boxes Corbett in New York in March
for a stake of £lOOO.
• • *
Rutherglen (Vic.) Sun solemnly announces
that nominations for the Wahgunyah races must be
accompanied by a statement of previous performances,
name, age at time of entry, pedigree of the owner, and
colours of the rider. “Burke’s Colonial Gentry” is
doing its deadly work.
# # *
W. G. Grace still bowls a good ball—when it
bears the brand approved by the champion.
• * *
“Vigilant,” of Melbourne Sportsman, who is
at ’Ome, lately told an interviewer that Carbine was
inferior to an English second-rater. This is the man who,
himself weighing 28 stone, had the front to compare
Tradition to “ a bullock.”
# # *
Stanbury and Sullivan have at last agreed to
scull for £2OO a-side and the world’s championship.
• # »
Joe Goddard, pugilist, has filed his schedule;
liabilities, £l5O, assets, £l. What a result for so much
printers’ ink and punching!
• # *
The star of the South Sea Island nig. is in the
ascendant in Australia. Sam. Griffith and the banks, and
the insolvent planters who mopped the Queensland sugar-
country at boom prices, are going to allow Malicolo Jimmy
to work again up North ; and the latest Sydney pugilist,
“Toby Travers,” is a Kanaka “ with a dangerous right.”
# * *
The ball with which demon Spofforth took
either the batsman or the wicket in the historic seven-
runs-victory of Australia over All-England, in 1882, is
now the property of Mr. Gaspard Munro, enthusiastic
cricketer and amateur actor-vocalist, of Brisbane. Geo.
Palmer, when leaving the whisky-punching capital of
Purgatory, presented the trophy to a Mr. Nathan, who
afterwards gave it to its present owner.
• * •
In prize-ring etiquette the second’s spray-
dispenser is his mouth. He draws a mouthful of water
from a bottle, and treats the panting slugger to a
sumptuous shower-bath. It is a crude substitute for the
barber’s elegant spray-dispenser.
• • •
One N.S.W, boxing god is now threatening to
“do for” certain pressmen who have shown up his
delinquencies. It was such conduct which brought about
the downfall of the old P.B. However, it must be
admitted that some pressmen deserve no better treat-
ment, considering the way in which their papers have
cringed to some of the meanest and most brutal ruffians
unhanged.
• • •
The glorious uncertainty of cricket is exemplified
by the fact that the admittedly strongest cricket team
which has ever visited Australia has done worse than its
weaker predecessors did in international matches.
• # *
The Irish team won the Adelaide tug-of-war,
alter splendid tussles with Sweden. The Irishmen were
heavy-weights, and, like the sons of the historical
Mrs. Canty, “ mostly polacemen."
•#• . *
The death of a Melbourne sport gives a peg
for reminisoents to fetch up the Newmmster scandal.
This was an attempt to do Boucicault’s “ Flying Scud ”
business, and Wilson Barrett’s “Nowadays,” over the
nobbling of a certain Newminster, very hot goods in Mel-
bourne years ago. Dakin was the trainer, and frustrated
the hocus-pocus in a scene which Darrell ought to write
up—or Garnet Walch.
* # *
Dear Bulletin,— Recently you asserted that
“ a newspaper managed by a directorate for a company is
imerally cheap and nasty.” There's no doubt about it.
The “message” and “galley” boys on certain papers
down South are regularly “on the book” to “do” the
leading cricket-matches on Saturday afternoons, and the
copy they send in—well, it’s a treat for the comp.
Apropos, it will be interesting to learn if the galley-boys
aforesaid are eligible to membership of the recently-
formed Institute of Journalists.—Anti-Rats.
* * #
Percy M'Donnell’s “fad” used to be that
“ Lohmann was no bowler,” and that he “could knock
him out of the ground whenever he faced him.” Times
out of number did he come to grief in the old days whilst
attempting the performance, and Lohmann must have
chuckled on Saturday when M'Donnell was caught off
him, although not in an attempt to knock the ball out of
the field.
* * *
If the Sydney Amateur Gymnastic Club is
resurrected, it should define two classes of amateurs, as
in the rowing world -.—Bona-fide amateurs and manual-
labour amateurs. At present, all the amateurs - bar
George Seale—belong to the manual-labour class.
# # *
Valentine should now meet Partridge for the
manual labour amateur boxing championship. The
writer understands that Valentine refuses to meet Par-
tridge, on the ground that the latter has fought for a
money prize, but the objection does not imply that
Valentine is over-anxious to make a match, as the money
prize referred to was a couple of pounds, fought for in a
knuckle fight when Partridge w 7 as a mere lad. A meeting
between Valentine and Partridge at reasonable prices of
admission would draw an immense crowd.
» # •
If New South Wales is to hold its own on the
cricket-field a period must be put to the picking of worn-
out men, on the strength of past performances, to the
exclusion of young men of present performances. It is
to be hoped that the Sydney public have seen the last of
Mr. “ Sammy ” Jones as one of their representatives ; and
as to Percy M'Donnell, the picking of him for such a
match after a couple of years complete retirement, when
there are brilliant young cricketers only too eager for the
fray, is about the strongest argument yet adduced that
some radical change is required in the selection of the
selectors of N.S.W. representative teams.
* # *
Only a fair attendance patronised the meeting
of Valentine and Beckett for the amateur championship
at Sydney Amateur Gymnastic Club’s rooms on Monday
night, the scale of charges, £1 and 10s., being more suited
to a match between world’s champions than the pair
under notice, one of whom, Beckett, had never before per-
formed in public. Valentine scaled 10 stone 101 b., and
Beckett, a very powerfully built young man, 11 stone Bib.
The latter is a fighter of the whirlwind order, but his
rushing tactics availed not with Valentine, who knocked
him down in the third round with a body blow', and he
was counted out. Valentine had nob a scratch to show,
but Beckett had both eyes closed and was very badly
marked.

canter by Tirailleur.

to Camoola.

A certain Sydney pony-trainer, finding that he

* # #
The cableman has fallen low when he must lie
about a set of blustering, drunken pugilists.
• * #
A N.S.W. surveyor tells a story of getting
rather “ happy ” in the bar of a country hostelry on the
night of a race-meeting which had killed one jockey—a
stranger in the town. The “survey” had drunk every-
body's health over and over, and when at last he was
going upstairs the housemaid said that as they were full
up, she was sure he wouldn’t .mind another gentleman
sharing his room with him ? “ Shertingly not,” amiably
hiccoughed the surveyor. “ Hope won’t disturb you, ole
feller,” to the “other gentleman,” who had already
turned in, and the elevated man of plans threw himself
beside the sleeper and went off at once. But voices woke
him in the morning. “ Where did they put the poor boy,
Liz ?” said one, and when the other voice said “ In here,”
the sobered surveyor reached out one trembling hand,
touched his stone-cold bed-fellow, and turned a double
somersault into the middle of the room. He had slept
peacefully cheek by jowl with the dead jockey.

Despair is done, we shout Hurroo,
Victoria boasts a new-born Hope !
The Phojnix.

Sydney, claims to have issued during lt jtlio last
year 105,978 policies—twice as many as all the
other life-offices in Australia put together, with-
out incuring the hostility of any of its con-
temporaries. It paid in claims .£9776 7s. £d. and
added £41,483 19s. sd. to its funds, making a
total of £115,158 3s. 2d. irrespective of its uncalled
capital of £lBO,OOO.

Answers to Correspondents.
•R.B.’ (Sydney): Maori war-stones arc al
much the same. There is always the pakeha in them,
and the rangatira, or else the rangitoto, and the Ngati-
maniapotos and little things like that; hut there is no
humour in any of them, and we get tired of reading how
Hinemoa rushed after Titokovvaru, with Tokomairiro a
bad third.... ‘Errol Dene’: Too long. When you write
a bush story don’t start with the heroine's childhood in
England, and lug in the old cottage, and the bed with
the white dimity curtains, and the honeysuckle ; it’s all
quite superfluous ‘ Thoughts on the Inner Life ’: You
seem by your verses to be actually bad because there is
no perdition. Do you want it for yourself or for some-
body else? We'll lend you our office Gehenna if you like.
‘ II, Butler ’: Your mass of horrors and corpses is too
improbable.... ‘ W. Lewis’: You are a shrieksome poet,
and you keep on saying you are “forsaken,” without
mentioning once who forsook you..., ‘Charles Mutton’:
Your “ Essays of He - Liar ” firmly declined ..
‘Majuba’: What you call your “rambling statements”
are both unsuitable; they are rambling right enough,
but we have our doubts if they are statements ‘ 11.0.’;
You state correctly that your tale is “a la Hudyard
Kipling.” The only thing in it that is not a la Rudyard
Kipling is the gory adjective which you insert so fre-
quently'. ... ‘ Thistles ’: The man who supplies information
of that sort is out, and the present w'riter doesn’t know
“ Violet Fane ” or the other lady r either. Moreover, the two
ladies don’t know him, which is a much more serious loss.
‘ J. R. Tighe '; Nothing in it. One man who dies in the
bush is so very like any' other man who dies in the bush
that he isn’t worth publishing as a rule “A J.P.’
(Alberton); A groan, a dull thud, a spectre beckoning
you to follow to the spot w here its bones are lying—it is
too stale. We have over 100 such groans and thuds on
hand, all waiting for the authorities to call and take
them away'. Thuds are a drug in the market.... ‘ W.L.M.’
(Melbourne): Letter received with thanks.... ‘ Wan-
derer’: Your verse is simple and touching, especially
when you say :—
And I eased a lady of her purse ; O God I turned to go
The crowd to leave, the path was full, I was too slow.
You are also too slow' in a whole lot of other
things ‘Q.S.’ (S. Brisbane): The story of how you
“padded” tip to Joe Wilton’s place is just as thrilling as
the tale of how the cow' walked across the road —

cause. It is a pity that an assassination which is calcu-

of Federal Government,” is the most philosophical by any
distinctively English writer, but only the first volume,
that dealing with the ancient Greek federations, was
published. The result of the American Civil War, it is
supposed, stopped the publication of the second volume,
by disproving the conclusions to which the author had
oome. Still, the fragment is magnificent. The greatest
works, however, on the federal principle are those of
Germans ‘ A.K.’(Melbourne): Ask any attendant of
Melbourne Public Library for the volume of Australian
pamphlets formerly labelled “328-6-17.” You will find
what you want there ‘ L.F.C.’ (Launceston): (1) Apart
altogether from the dirty Bryce business, Rusden is pro-
nounced by all authorities to be the most grossly biassed
Of all Australian historians—who include Wentworth and
Lang, and no one suspects them of impartiality; (2)
“When will the N.S.W. History from the Records be
completed?” Ask us ten years hence, please.... ‘ Nar-
ranghi Boori ’: The “ Brace of Sundowners” is the most
wonderful literary production we have ever read. There
is nothing like it in the Sydney Public Library, but it
won’t do for The Bulletin. ... ‘ Edw'ard Morley’; The
arguments you adduce in support of the woman’s-rights
position have been stated over and over again, and there
is absolutely nothing in them that will hold water. The
position of woman is a question of gradual development
and evolution. No amount of legislation in the world
will equalise the sexes. A woman is not a man, nor is a
man a woman, and things unlike cannot be compared. A
man and a woman are no more equivalent quantities than
half-a-mile and Is. Bd. The functions of a woman are
peculiar to herself, and no man can fulfil them. Neither
can the woman perform the work of the man. To say
that there are powers and capabilities which men and
women hold in common is to beg the question. The
differences arc real and marked, and cannot he bridged
over... ‘M. Morton Your humour belongs to the geo-
metric period when men were accustomed to smile by
means of an equation.

Read —‘ A Golden Shanty.’
The Q ueens l an d Aboriginal.
Dear Bulletin, —I have just read your
story “ Malchook’s Doom,” and taking out the
remorse, which characters of his sort seldom have,
and the visitation of retributive justice by the
blacks upon Jacky the Span, which seldom or
never happens, it very well describes the treat-
ment “ the niggers ” receive in Queensland. There
are few men bad enough, even if allowed by their
comrades, to go to the extent of burning a nigger
alive in the spinifex, though to shoot them at
sight is the usual thing in the outside parts. To
be true to nature, if everybody on the Nicholson
said that Jacky the Span deserved his fate, it is a
hundred to one it wasn’t because he killed blacks
they thought so. I venture to assert that they
would say that such a fate was deserving of the
man who spared them.
* * #
I have known a man have his life
threatened out Coen way for having informed a
camp of black men and women and children
located at a station that they were going to be
shot down in the morning at daylight, because
depredations had been done on the neighbouring
run and they were handiest for the native police
and run-holders to shoot. As it was, the owner
dismissed him. And the general all-round abuse
he got, and still gets, makes, him often ask him-
self if he is not a hangman or a murderer that his
fellow-men abuse him so. Nearly every native
police officer is a Jacky the Span, fox', to kidnap
boys and to make troopers of them and allow
them to kidnap gins from the bush, and then
shoot either troopers or gins if their love of
country or father or mother induces them to run
away from a slavery worse than death, is the
usual pastime of these gentlemen, intermixed with
kidnapping, shooting, frightening and catching
run-away boys for influential squatters. Do the
reverend gentlemen who waited upon N.S.W.
Premier Dibbs, the other day, with a request for
the xxative police of Queensland to be let loose on
the South Sea Islands know what they are asking
for ? If they do they are simply fiends in human
shape.
* # «
And for all this wickedness the Govern-
ment that is asking for moixey from the Austra-
lian provinces to carry on the same game in New
Guinea is alone responsible, for the squatter and
the black would never have come into antagonism
had the Government in renting new country pro-
vided that the blacks should get a periodical
supply of beef oi| mutton at their camps to supple-
ment their native foods for a few years until
settlement increased, when they could (with
their hunting) get enough work to maintain
them. This would not have been as expensive as
the native police for shooting them, and is not
like the missionary and reserve plans, which are
simply enslaving them, for they cannot bear to be
shifted from their own part of the country to
another or be changed from hunters into agricul-
turists. If worked in this way the black would
be an assistant to the squatter while his own
liberty would not be interfered with ; for as a
hunter and desti’oyer of marsupials he is a gain to
the squatter and the Government, and if he had
not been too much “dispersed” in the South, as
he is now being in the North, people need never
have feared a rabbit-invasion.
* * *
The above is the only practical and just
way of settling the black question. The black
does not covet the squatter’s grass ; the squatter
does not want the blacks’ game. The only tiling
to overcome is the black’s craving for beef or
mutton, which they will have while it runs about
like other game. In their wild state, they have
no idea of property in anything.
» # #
The blacks are now few. Let the Queens-
land Government disband its native police in
favour of white men only, and then bring (he
blacks at once under the same law as the white.
At or after the periodical distributions of be. f,
depredators can be arrested and sentenced to
terms of imprisonment in proportion to crime, for
it is well known it is the easiest thing to get the
truth out of blacks; and one will assist to arrest
or even shoot another without the least compunc-
tion, provided he is not a blood relation.
# # #
If the Australian blacks had been a
numerous and warlike race, they would have got
far more concessions than these for giving up so
beautiful a country. But while things are as they
are, Australians and Englishmen need not point
to the Spanish conquest of America for an
example of wanton cruelty.— Queenslander.
When Ollivier Pain disappeared in the
Soudan years ago the French Anglophobes said
he had been made away with by the English. Th<
truth is now disclosed. While being escorted
across the desert to the Mahdi’s head-quarters he
was taken ill and became so weak that he fell from
the back of his camel. The escort, to save trouble,
quietly buried him alive.
A Bathurst paper tells a picturesque story
of old Port Arthur, at the tune when padded
cells were introduced to prevent “ solitaries ”
from dashing out their brains against unpadded
walls, and which padding prevented the rats from
swarming in, as had been their wont, upon the
dark-cell occupant. Briefly put —Warder Dodds
playfully put into Convict M‘Pherson’s cell a
“Tasmanian devil,” a vicious tiger of an animal
well-toothed and strongly clawed, “as a bed-
mate tocaress and keep him warm.” M'Pherson,
at the expense of much blood and flesh, managed
to kill the fierce beast, and, when the matter was
reported, Dodds was reduced to his former con-
vict status —not for “brutality,” but for acting
contrary to the regulations in “ supplying a
prisoner with a companion to beguile his soli-
tude.”

The Wild-Cat Column.
Tq-day there is hardly a newspaper in Australia
outside of such towns as Bendigo, Ballarat, Charters
Towers and Gympie with any appreciable real mining in-
fluence. The influence of the press has been lost in lying
or what is often worse—the suppression of truth. News-
papers—morning, evening and weekly—have been the
medium for the wheezes of the mining-bummer, the
phenomenal estimates of the low-level bounder founded
on assayers’ experiments, and manager’s periodical ex-
aggerations otherwise known as “ reports.”
# * •
For a time, when the boom swamped the news-
papers with prospectus-advertisements, nearly everything
sent in “went in"—until the mining correspondence
grew so prodigiously that it had to be “cut down”—a
work almost invariably entrusted to somebody who had
never seen a mine in his life.
* * #
To exemplify the density of the oblivion of
daily newspapers on the subject of mining, sometime
back a man perpetrated a joke on one of them
which has never yet been quite seen through.
He sent to it reports of what we will now call the
“Homeward Bound.” It had no real existence. For
the first three weeks it was a gold-mine, afterwards a
silver concern, and, in subsequent reports, the changes
were rung in turn on copper, tin, antimony and other
minerals. When the joke had been play'ed for a twelve-
month, and every report had been published with great
regularity and little condensation, the public were one
day soberly informed that all work had been abandoned,
as the coal-seam had disappeared. Every wild cat in
Australia has received columns of newspaper laudation of
its astounding richness in some form or other, and most
of the mines floated during the last few years were wild
cats.
# # #
At last, too, has arisen the newspaper mining
tipster, who has gratuitously made so many fortunes for
others, that we should not be surprised any day to hear
of his death by violence.
* # #
The average metropolitan mining newspaper
correspondent on the field is a daisy 7. Had his dictum
even a 10-per-cent, correctness there would be 50 mining
fields in N.S.W. alone with an average population of
50,000 each. He is sometimes a local editor, and often
the local bellringer.
* * *
Other mining heroes are the man with the new
patent for treating “ refractory ores,” the patent grind-
ing and amalgamating man, and the patent automatic
concentration genius. They have dealt with the man
who has lost his money under the blissful idea that “ the
battery lost his gold,” and now we propose to occasion-
ally deal with them. We shall interview the “mining
expert,” and subject him to occasional jibes concerning
the millions we were to get out of “ Taranganba,”
White Rock,” “Lewis Ponds,” “Toms,” “Castlerags,”
1 Last Galls,’ and other mines the public find it so easy
o remember.
# # #
Will the Broken Hill Proprietary mine collapse?
That is the question—never allowed to Crop prominently
ap, and dispelled In a thousand Ways whenever it
threatens—but it has, nevertheless, agitated the minds of
many substantial shareholders for a long time. If per-
fectly safe, why, then, so many assurances of safety after
every “creep?” Every other imaginable reason has
been given for the tremendous fall in the value of shares.
The drought, forsooth, when its returns and dividends
are uninterrupted, and the foregoing of one of the latter
would furnish a permanent water-supply, while the fall in
values represents £6,000,000. Why is it that no news-
paper ever stated that the fear is rapidly gaining ground
that the time must come, possibly this year, perhaps ten
rears hence, when the timbering must give way, where-
upon the yields would be long locked up, and the
American system of open square set timbering in a lode
iveraging some 60 feet thick will only stand to be ever-
lastingly 7 condemned. Unless you fill in a mine like the
Rroken Hill Proprietary it will one day fill itself in. The
11 creep ” in the mine is nothing compared to the “ creep”
Jut of it, hence the “ big fall ” in stocks.
* * #
An old hand, by adeptness and finesse, had
so raised himself in the estimation of his fellows that
be was regarded as an artist in salting a mine,
Having elevated it to the position of a fine art,
be very quietly 7 made it his profession. His ser-
vices Were retained by a party who had a some-
what difficult piece of “salting” to accomplish.
Having received his instructions and fee, he was, as
usual, of course, entrusted with a very heavy parcel of
jold—the wherewithal to sweeten the crushing and
the cake. The washing-off was a ghastly disappointment
to the intending purchasers, while the owners looked at
tach other and whistled. They interviewed the artist,
who instantly displayed all the diplomacy and tact for
which he was distinguished. He, in a tone of great
lignity, asked them if they really meant to impute that
be had been hired to manipulate a crushing and that
they had given him gold for so villainous a purpose.
Having condescendingly taken their names and addresses,
evidently with the object of an action for defamation, he
bade them an, revoir , and told them to go to h , The
gold weighed 6loz,
* * •
The death of mining companies and the incuba-
tion of new ones is now the order of the day. Share-
holders are informed that “ all the money has been called
up,” which often, in a capital of £lOO,OOO, means less
than £5OO. At Pambula there are companies with a
capital on paper of £60,000, and working capital of £2OO,
and no liability even for that,
» # #
New South Wales, above all other places in the
world, has been the home of the mining “mug.” The
slowness of the N.S.W. man’s nature, and his remem-
brance of the gruelling he received in 1871-2 by
the Hill End boom caused him to be the “last man in’’
the boom of 1889 —90. The last man in a boom usually
comes in to stay as a sort of wet-nurse liquidator to sick
and dying companies. In N.S.W. hundreds of thousands
of solid cash have been absolutely wasted inasmuch as it
has been spent on mines the ground of which should
never have been pegged out. Other hundreds of thou-
sands have been thrown away on machinery for which,
when completed, there was no use. Hundreds of thou-
sands, too, have been sunk in the sinking of shafts and the
driving of tunnels for lodes which, in many cases, had no
existence, and, when found in others, were worthless.
For years the cornstalks have been asses big enough to
believe every word sent in by mining managers. Now
they only believe in two or three expressive characteristic
phrases such os “ still sinking,” “ the pumps are working
well.” These are homely truths which carry their own
convictions.
* * #
Taranganba is dead, and now the Last Call is
rapidly dying. Both, as everybody knows, were “salted”
mines—nobody can be found bold enough to declare who
“ salted” either, although anybody could put his finger
on the persons concerned. Whoever heard of a conviction
for “salting?’’ Nevertheless, a very large percentage of
mines floated in a boom are more or less “salted." The
successful “sailer” always escapes, and usually with a
fortune, while the unsuccessful “salter,” the man who is
detected, pays the small penalty of not being able to sell
his mine. We will allow till the end of the present cen-
tury, and give odds, that the persons who “salted” the
Last Call mine will never be gaoled.
# # *
What earthly use, from the standpoint of the
general community, is the Prospecting Vote? Hundreds
of thousands of pounds have been wasted by this means
by the Australasian Governments. Judged by results, it
stands condemned. It engenders fraud, extravagance,
State robbery and jobbery. What is wanted in its stead
is the institution of a State Mining College, with extensive
metallurgical works for the treatment of test lots of
every class of ore at a small cost. Erected in the metro-
politan districts and well conducted, such institutions
would command the attention of students, and open up a
new profession to the Australian youth. It could be made
self-supporting when well established. Large premiums
from students could be readily obtained. To send big
consignments of concentrated ores to Freiburg, an inland
town in Germany, for treatment is a costly anomaly. It
is because 6f the absence of such works that Australian
mines, in which hundreds of thousands have been
expended, remain idle to-day—£loo,ooo spent in this
direction would mean the money saved in others. The
Bulletin means to push this question ahead.
The wild cat mines of New South Wales are
being weeded out by liquidation. The bracketed words
(in liquidation) haunt almost every legal manager’s office
plate. In some places it is squeezed into a corner; in
others it stands boldly out as if the contingency had been
well in mind when the plate was originally designed.
The liquidator is a soulless man, who sees nothing but the
liabilities of the company, and sells for what it fetches
everything on which he can lay his hands at a time, of
course, when few want to buy. Batteries, engines,
boilers, smelting works, concentrators, and every other
form of mining machinery, on which, in some cases, many
thousands have been spent, are sold for fewer hundreds.
A limited liquidated mining-plant syndicate, to buy up
these sacrifices, would undoubtedly declare big dividends
in the next mining boom.
# * #
Fortunately for British investors, the Broken
Hill and Mount Morgan mines were not floated in Eng-
land. Had they been their results would have poured
ten of millions of English money into Australian wild-
cats. The British investor only wants a few dividend-
paying mines to make an ass of himself. He did so in
the Transvaal, and will do so anywhere. It is an axiom
that the conditions surrounding the floating of mines in
London are such as to render profitable raining almost
impossible. A gold or silver mine in any part of the civi-
lised world always commands its apparent intrinsic value
locally. If taken to London, be it big or small, it must by
the traditions of the brokers, promoters, financiers,
underwriters, provisional directors, and other sharks, be
so loaded as to render dividends out of the property only
possible by striking something phenomenally richer than
was ever anticipated. It is the man and not the mine
that floats in London- hence, as a rule, the man gets hold
of a worthless mine and is quite prepared to exchange his
little reputation for big money.
* * #
Four shareholders in a claim wanted to “get out,”
each determined, unknown to the others, to “salt"the
crushing and thereby boom the shares. Each, unknown
to the others, put into the box a quantity of gold, and all
were surprised at the size of the cake. Each started at
arithmetic deducting the amount which he alone sub-
scribed, and all came to the conclusion that the crushing
was an astonishingly good one, and that dividends were
certainties. Each rushed away and bought shares heavily',
and all at last discovered that they had “had” each
other. This is the only case on record where salters
were, by their own hands, salted. Let us hope it cured
them.
Captain’Osborne, who married the heroine
of the pearl-robbery case whilst her character was
already under suspicion, has received something
to console him for the disgrace of his adventurous
wife. The Duke of Gampridge declined to accept
his military resignation, on the ground that
“Her Majesty cannot afford to part with the
finest representative of chivalry of the century.”
The Duke’s limited conception of chivalry is
easily accounted for, by the way. As the husband
of an ex-dancer whose “maiden” name was not
unspotted before the world he naturally likes to
think that a man who makes an imprudent
marriage is necessarily the beau-ideal of a noble
animal.
A certain Australian M.P. lately had some
transactions with a “financier,” and when one of
his p.n.’s fell due he wanted it renewed. The
financier agreed to do it, “if he provided two
good names.” The M.P. hurried to the Assembly
and without the slightest trouble procured the
signatures of two prominent politicians. “ Here
you are,” he said to the financier, “will these
do?” “ Gott in Hirnmel, no,” exclaimed the
Hebrew When he saw them ; “ I’ve got a mort-
gage oVer eferything dey possess—barring their
souls ! ” Apropos, the schedule of Abraham Fried-
man shows that only 18 members of the N.S.W.
Assembly—in other words, about 13 per cent,
of the total members —owe him money. The
amounts range from <£3ooo down to =£4o.
G. R. Sims tells a yarn that has a moral
not only for successful litterateurs , but more
particularly for men who fancy themselves much
bigger roosters in much smaller spheres. “ Dago-
net’s ” yarn is to the effect that at a time whert
his name was on the bills of four London theatres,
he joined a party of old fellow students in a
private box at a theatre, and sat out one of his
own dramas. Over a little supper, after the
performance, he had to listen patiently whilst the
play was voted blank “rot,” after which he
gathered up his hat and slunk sorrowfully away.
“ These were men of the world, and went about
town,” remarks Sims, “but not one of them had
ever heard of me in connection with the theatre;
and, although they took a programme as they
went into the box, not one of them studied it
sufficiently to notice my- name upon it.” The
vanity of “fame” is soon understood by a man
who ventures outside his own circle. Only the
prosperous pompous tradesmen, who sticks strictly
to the town which elected him councillor, has the
satisfaction of living and dying truly famous to
the best of his belief.
There is a silver lining to every cloud.
During the Sydney Savings Bank scare, when
several hundred people were screaming in the
street because the door of the bank was too
narrow to let them in, at least one gentle Hebrew
did a good trade by buying up depositors’ books
at the rate of 15s. in the £. He Went home that
night with quite a little pile, and came back the
second day to pursue his avocation. By this time
the crowd had so much diminished that there was
no earthly reason why the people shouldn’t go in
and receive payment in full, yet in quite an
unaccountable way he was fairly rushed by sellers.
He bought at 25 per cent, discount till his loose
cash was all exhausted, and then ho went into the
bank to settle up his accounts and—so the story
goes—found that he was left. Another son of
Israel had also been buying up deposits cheap,
and making fresh entries in the books on his own
account, and selling them to him by various agents,
and he was the worst-defrauded Hebrew that ever
came out of Palestine. Next time there is a run on
the Sydney Savings Bank a different “financier”
will be buying up credit balances in the gutter at
Barrack-street; the present one h.as retired from
the trade. He says that the Australian depositor
is “ too unbrinzibled,”

Benevolent Asylum. There was a man named
Gibson lost during Giles’ expedition of 1873, but
there is no manner of doubt about his death. The
asylum hero, according to his account, found his
way home on foot accompanied by a retriever slut
from the interior of Western Australia. Passing
over the wonderful sporting abilities of the dog as
simply a Munchausen effect thrown in, let us see
to the remainder. He lived on mussels and
’possums in a portion of Australia where there are
neither ’possums nor mussels ; he had a retriever
slut with him, which is a canine that will stand
neither heat nor thirst, and could never have
reached the point indicated alive. This slut
caughtfish, in acountry where there are no fish, and
a lot of other absurdities too numerous to mention.
The water supply of thq region where the real
Gibson perished simply consists of occasional
oozing springs, with a few rock holes filled by
passing thunderstorms. Permanent waterholes
are unknown, and in the dry spinifex country
there is no life but that of ants ; not even a kite is
visible. No white man ever found his way in on
foot from that region. Our friend G.G. must
have been sitting at the feet of Jack Dick
Skuthorpe.— Delcomtn.

CONSULTATION FREAK,
Impudence Personified.
In the Field of January 26, we sounded a
warning note to the public relative to a consulta-
tion promoted under the heading of the Sydney
Jockey Turf Club, and we are pleased to find that
our article has had a good effect in many country
districts. This week it becomes our duty to call
the attention of the public to a consultation
promoted by a person calling himself G. Adams,
and who gives his address as Box 591, G.P.0.,
Sydney, and by many this person is confounded
with Mr. George Adams, of Tattersall’s Hotel,
and who is the promoter of
tattersall’s monster consultations,
so well and favourably known throughout the
colonies. From information to hand we find that
some time back a man employed in one of the
ferry services of the harbour "discarded his own
name and caused circulars to be issued under the
name of J. Adams, setting forth particulars of a
monster consultation, his address at this time
being Darling-street, Balmain, but this was care-
fully kept back from the public, and the address
given as the G.P.O. The public, ever ready to
Speculate, patronised the affair in great style, and
in a few months “J. Adams,” in addition to his
position on the ferry steamer, found himself very
well provided for as regards funds. Yet, like
many others, the “ferry man’’did not think the
money was coming in fast enough, and he there-
fore caused more circulars to be issued in the
name of “ G. Adams,” and one of these precious
documents is lying before us as we write. The
public fell into the trap, and for months money
rolled in from all'parts of Australia, and it was
some months before the authorities were made
aware of the deception. Then an order was issued
stopping the payment of
POST-OFFICE ORDERS
which were made payable to the so-called “G.
Adams,” and it may be mentioned that the proper
name of the latter was then made known to the
powers that be, when it was found that he held a
certificate under the Marine Board in connection
with ocean service under quite another name.
When the promoter found which way the wind
was blowing he betook himself to a leading bank
and paid into his credit a number of cheques and
Post-office orders, thinking no doubt to get the
latter cashed through this channel, but the G.P.O.
people still stood firm, and the result was that the
manager of the bank referred to, demanded an
immediate withdrawal of the account. Not to be
outdone, this self-called
G. ADAMS
wrote to three of his clients, who had forwarded
to him £2), in sums of .£lO, £7 and £3, stating
that for some unexplained reason the Post-office
authorities declined to cash P.O. orders made
payable to sweep promoters .and asked them to
withdraw their order from their local Post-office,
and forward him a cheque or cash for the amount,
charging him (the promoter), of course, with “all
expenses.” The local Postmaster, however, could
not interfere, and the result was a communication
with head-quarters; and the final was reached
when the authorities detained some scores of there
r rders; and at the present time there can be
found a great number lying in the Money Order
Office, unpaid and unclaimed, the latter being
caused by tire G. Adams, of Box 591, G.P.0., not
being forthcoming. Since the present
POSTMASTER-GENERAL
came into office the right of G. Adams to use Box
691, G.P.0., has been withdrawn, and this in-
dividual has been put to considerable trouble
thereby. Nothing daunted, however, the man of
“fertile brains” issued other circulars, one of
which came from Victoria to us a few days ago,
and is headed as follows :
Adams’
Moxster Cash Prize Consultation
on the
Newmarket Handicap.
And this document sets forth that the event ia
open to 100,000 subscribers at 10s. each, and,
according to particulars on the back, every sub-
scriber of .£1 has fourteen separate chances of
drawing two horses or twelve cash prizes. We
commend this portion of the business to our
readers, and leave them to judge for themselves of
the result. But the most interesting part of this
circular is contained in the following paragraph :
As Consultations
are Illegal,
and the authorities trying to stop them, please send
Gold, Notes or Cheques in payment for Tickets, Instead
or Post Office Orders.
Now, for real cool cheek this paragraph takes
the cake, as, having failed to get the authorities to
with him in the name of a respected
citizen, the promotor has the impudence to try and
coax his clients to do business as above. As
showing the immense business done by this man
and others of the same ilk, we may say that during
the past week over 300 registered letters have been
received by this “G. Adams and as showing his
impudence, we can state that a few days ago a man
came direct from him to the G.P.O. to claim these
very letters, which, for various reasons, had been
withheld for a few days. During the past few
weeks something has happened to disturb this
unknown sweep promotor’s peace of mind, as he
changed his place of residence in a very hurried
manner, and his whereabouts just now is a matter
of doubt. We trust that we have now given the
public sufficient warning, and have also to inform
them that in all probability fac-similos of the
genuine Tattersall’s circulars will be issued to
them within a few days, and they should there-
fore be upon their guard and see that the proper
address is placed thereon. Fortunately for some
of the up-country folks, their moneys have found
their way into the hands of “ Tattersalls ” instead
of into the hands of the so-called G. Adams of
Box 591 G.P.O. We have omitted to mention
that in December last a client wrote to this Box
591 man, asking to be supplied with the name of
the person who drew the big prize in the Mel-
bourne Cup, and the man of “mystery” actually
had the effrontery to send one of “Tattersall’s”
printed slips with the name thereon, but was very
careful to cut off the name of “ Tattersall.” Need
we say more.— Vide the Field, Feb. 9th, 1892;
and tlie Australian Star, Feb. 10th, 1892.
WHAT EVERYBODY SHOULD KNOW.
Sprains are injuries done to the ligament
tendons, and other structures surrounding the
joints. They are produced by jumping, falling
lifting heavy weights, or other causes, which
forcibly stretch or lacerate the tendons, &c. The
joints most liable to sprains are the wrist and
ankle joints. The latter are more frequently
sprained by persons in the act of jumping or
running or by the foot turning under the weight
of the body, while the wrist may be sprained in a
fall, or by handling heavy objects, and so on.
Injuries of this kind are of the most common
occurrence, and everyone should know how to best
treat them. Sprains are attended with great pain
at the time of the accident, and also with consider-
able swelling and discoloration of the injured
part; the swelling and discoloration arise from the
effusion of blood into the surrounding structures.
In the treatment of sprains the injured limb must
be kept in a perfect state of quietude, and in such
a position as to relax the muscles in connection
with the affected tendons, as well as to favor the
return of the blood to the heart. The bowels
should be kept in a free state, and hot fomentations
applied to the sprain by means of a sponge or
piece of thick flannel. Next, dry gently with a
soft Turkish towel, and immediately after apply
St. Jacobs Oil, which should be rubbed as the
patient may be able to bear, over and around the
part affected, with the palm of the hand ; then
bandage tightly with strips of old linen, saturated
in the same liniment, and let the operation be
repeated at intervals until a cure is effected. By
this treatment the disease will rapidly subside,
and the affected part will return to its usual
healthy condition. This was the experience of
Mr. James Grant, of No. 2, Kent-street, Sydney,
who writes : “ I formerly worked on the Margaret-
street Wharf, and while there the sinews of my
forearm gave way, and I was unable to lift any-
thing, and so prevented from following my employ-
ment. I was advised to try St. Jacobs Oil, and
did so with good effect, as my arms are now quite
strong, and I can carry heavy weights without
feeling anything of my former complaint.” Cut
this out and paste it in your scrap-book.

HOW TO IMPROVE YOUR FORTUNE.
If you are not up to time in all your engage-
ments, small as well as great, people soon begin to
lose confidence in you. A trifling sum invested
in a Waterbury Watch will help to save you froth
being frequently misjudged, and may be the meahs
of materially improving your fortunes. It is th 6
very trifles of life on which success or failure
generally hinge. And if anything goes wrong
with the watch the owner has only to take or send
it to the offices of the Waterbury Watch
Company, at 56, Margaret-street, where it can
be repaired at a cost according to its requirements,
but in no case exceeding 2s. 6d., unless by mutual
consent. The cost of transmission by post or
parcel delivery on watches sent from the country
must be prepaid, and a sufficient amount to cover
return charges and cost of repairs forwarded at the
same time. If sent in the box in which the
Waterbury is sold the postage fee (including
registration) is 6d. each way.

Tales of the Early Days.
BY PRICE WARUNG.
No. II.—Captain Maconochie’s "Bounty for
Crime.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The most remarkable experiment, all
things considered, ever made with the noble pur-
pose of reforming criminals was Captain Macono-
chie’s attempt to adapt his “ mark ” system to the
monstrous conditions of penal life at Norfolk
Island. And being in principle humane, and in
method an arraignment of all notions current in
British and Colonial officialdom, it met with pre-
cisely that degree of success which was prophesied
for it by Mr.—not then “ Sir ”—B. Deas-Thomson,
Colonial Secretary of New South Wales.
“Speaking, your Excellency,” said that vener-
able if somewhat pragmatical gentleman to Gover-
nor Sir George Gipps, “ from a lengthened
experience of—h’m—convict disciplinary methods,
I have—ah !—no hope that Captain Maoonochie’s
system will achieve the least good. It must fail,
sir !”
And fail it did. To the undisguised delight of
the Colonial Secretary’s Office, Sydney, and the
Deputy Commissariat-General’s Departments of
Sydney and Norfolk Island, it failed. You see,
the first maxim of the Captain was the reforma-
tion of the criminal, while almost every other
person connected with the System, from Lord
John Russell to the meanest scourger on the
Island or at Port Arthur, thought the criminal
was a mere thing to be locked up, and fettered,
and flogged into purity of life and integrity of
conduct.
Now, Maconochie’s success would have meant
the System’s condemnation. And his failure
meant that the System was right and its adminis-
trators were wise. Therefore the failure was only
to be expected. Men do not care about being
proved' wrong, even if it could be shown that a
few dozen souls were saved in the process of
correction.
For instance, Mr. Assistant-Deputy-Commis-
sary-General Shanks would have had to confess
himself egregiously in error had Convict Tobias
Tracey, per ship John, third trip, kept continu-
ously on the path of rectitude which Captain
Maconochie marked out for him.
And that was not to be thought of. Convict
Tracey had to fall once mor» in the slough of ill-
doing in order to prove A D. O. G. Shanks
right.
• * #
To enable you to understand how terrible
was that fall we must measure the height which he
had attained. Step by step, climbing upwards,
now taking firm foothold on a dead sin, now
clutching such aid as came from the opportunity
to do a kindly service to a brother-felon ; again
slipping back into the pit of corruption because
a messmate jeered at him ; yet again striving
against the tremendous alliance of the forces of
evil, till he gained a new standing-place on the up-
track— of Tracey during
1840, and the early part of 1841. In the
later part of ’4l he fell—thanks to A. D. C. G.
Shanks—irretrievably. In the early months of
’4l, he had first come under the notice of the Cap-
tain-Superintendent, and had begun to aspire
towards a manlier existence. During the interval
between those first tentative stragglings to mount,
and that last dreadful fall, his life was epic in its
storms and its battles, its victories and its defeats,
* # #
Look at his record.
His original conviction was on the Bth Septem-
ber, 1831, at a London gaol delivery. His crime
was burglary, and his sentence, transportation for
life.
His sojourn in the hulk obtained for him the
distinction of “a bad report,” and the voyage out
to Sydney gave him still higher rank in the aristo-
cracy of vice. “He was doomed,” the Surgeon-
Superintendent of the John (third trip) told him
one day in mid-ocean, “to become a Black Nor-
folker as sure as Fate.”
And Fate is unerring, as everyone knows. Two
years after landing in Sydney, he was sentenced
by the Supreme Court for highway robbery and
housebreaking. Once more his time was life, with
the added distinction of irons—irons always—
sleeping, waking, at work and at his meals—irons
to be knocked off only if he mounted the scaffold,
or on freedom coming to him otherway—say,
through some kindly shot or kindlier blow. Free-
dom by process of servitude would never come to
him. He was destined by Nature as a Retrograde.
Chaplain Taylor spoke of his “Retrogrades ” to
Governor Gipps.
“Your ‘ Retrogrades ? ’ What are they ? ”
“ Men who are further away from freedom each
succeeding day they are on the Island—who are
never nearer freedom than when they set foot
here ! ”
“But, Mr. Taylor, in the nature of the case
such characters must be few?” rejoined His Excel-
lency.
“Few, Your Excellency? Sixty out of each
hundred!”
Now, at the precise moment the Superintendent’s
clerk at the Island gave a receipt to the master
of the Governor Phillip, from Sydney, for the
body of Tobias Tracey, No. 33,149, per John (3),
that hardened villain might have expected release
in 25 years, if the Devil and the System would
only allow him to keep his hands from picking
and stealing, and breaking-in stores and warders’
heads. Being predestined, however, to the ranks
of the Retrogrades, within 24 hours of his arrival
he was no nearer than 30 years to his freedom.
He had knocked a gaol-turnkey down.
# # • ,
In 1835, his “ police history” was extended
by five offences of the serious order. “Light
offences ” were invariably at this period and till
the arrival of Maconochie, sentenced “on view.”
“On view ” punishments—that is, without trial—
are supposed to have numbered, during 1833-4-5-6-
7-8, between 8000 and 9000. They were never
recorded; formal trials only were recorded ; and
these last during the years specified totalled 2483,
and the awful mutiny year calendar was embraced
in this sum. Of these trials Tracey was respon-
sible, in 1835, for five.
In 1836 he was credited with four. The cir-
cumstance that he was ironed in gaol for the
major part of this year accounted for the diminu-
tion of 20 per cent, in the number of his heavy
offences. When a felon was double-ironed in a
6x4 cell, when his right hand was manacled to a
staple in the wall, when he saw a human face four
times daily for ten seconds each time, it cannot be
said that his opportunities for outraging the peace
of the realm were numerous. Still, ho enlarged
his record by four entries.
In 1837 they varied his gaol privileges by
flogging him, and the scourging ground was the
best place possible for adorning a man’s record.
He had only to swear at the superintending officer
to be credited with another crime ; and if, also, he
struck the honourable scourger with his fist or
head, or bestowed upon that official a sadly-
needed kick, well, there was a second offence on
the same day. Consequently, Mr. Tracey, who
was in gaol from January to 7th March, was in
gaol again from 3rd April “till further orders.”
In June and July he was in gaol, but in Septem-
ber he must have been out of gaol, for he
absconded. He was arrested three-quarters of an
hour afterwards at the gate of the Superinten-
dent’s quarters —said he had never been out of the
gaol precincts—-nevertheless, was presented with
300 lashes in one dose. And from 14th October
onwards to the end of the year he was in gaol
again, his record for the year being seven crimes.
In 1838, a variation in policy took place once
more. He added no more than five offences to his
history, but one of these earned him 100 lashes
and another four months in the sweet seclusion of
his iron-cells—and the others ? Each won for him
a long term of “solitary.”
The second Earl of Limerick, then plain Mr.
Pery, and a humble “Superintendent of Agricul-
ture ” on the island, spent a brief space in one of
the same solitary cells. He stayed till it was a
question of his going mad, or going out. He went
out, and found that he had been secluded 17
minutes ! Tracey had three terms—14, 14, and 20.
Minutes? No—days!
As for Tracey’s 1839 record, here it is as full as
the books give it. There are no “ sentences on
view ” included, and he must have had his full
share of them. You will not fail to notice the
dreadfully heinous character of the recorded
crimes.
At the end of 1839, with a double-life con-
viction over him, Tobias was exactly 53 years
distant from freedom. But in February of the
succeeding year Captain Maconochie arrived,
and, to the amazement of the well-informed
officers of the System as before established, almost
immediately chose that prime rascal by the John
(3) for special experiment. As soon as the new
Super, was possessed of Tobias Tracey’s police
record, he.ordered the man’s irons to be struck off.
Now, Major Bunbury, the previous commandant,
had never dared approach Tobias except under
the escort of two soldiers.
But the amazement of the staff was nothing to
the surprise of the notorious fellow himself.
“Lord ! What a fool the new ’un is if he thinks
as he’s got a softy to deal wi’.” Thus he laughed
with a coarse mockery, as he passed into the
Super’s, presence.
The Superintendent’s tall and erect form filled
the doorway of the Gi’ass Hut where he was hold-
ing a preliminary inspection. In a few weeks he
would turn the hut into a school and a Catholic
chapel, but at present he proposed to use it as a
court-house. “Why?” The old officials asked
the question—and cackled hilariously when they
received the answer. Sothat prisonersfor trial, who
might have been in the local court before, might
not be unpleasantly reminded of their past mis-
deeds ! “I want to start every man with a clean
sheet as far as possible.” Laugh! Of course they
laughed.
The Overseer in temporary charge of Convict
Tracey saluted, and presented that ruffian.
“ Transport Tracey, Tobias. No. 33—149, y’r
Honor —bad k’racter —3 B’s, f sir—suspected ”
“ Of unnatural crimes—one murder—three bur-
glaries—an’ a heap to’ther things, Super.—same
ol’ list,” concluded the convict himself. “ One o’
th’ worst men on th’ Island, y’r Honor, now
they’ve turned orf Westwood. There ain’t a
crime on the list, Super., that I ain’t committed,
’cept those I’m goin’ to commit. An’ now yer
know all, ol’ cove ! Give us my five hunderd
quick ah’ ’a done wi’ it. Look slippy now, Ol’
King-o’-th’-lags ! ”
Accustomed as the penal officials comprising
Captain Maeonochie’s little suite were to outbursts
of reckless speech from the more hardened “ old
hands,”! they scarcely expected Tracey to uncoil

Two—three minutes elapsed before the Superin-
tendent spoke. Tracey himself had expected an
instant order for his removal to the triangles, and
stood doggedly waiting for the command—which
did not come.
All they saw was that Maconochie drew his
handkerchief from his tail-pocket and blew his
nose. Then—
“ Let it be understood, Overseer—are you Over-
seer or Warder ? ”
“ Overseer, sir—of the gaol-gang—Tuff, sir.”
“ Very well, Overseer Tuff—let it be understood,
if you please, that you are never to report a man’s
police-history till it is asked for by me directly,”
“ Yes, sir ! ” answered the sub-official, with a
sullen respectfulness, “But ’twas the Major’s
reg’lashun, sir, wi’ all transports, ’specially des-
p’rate ones ! ”
“ Ho, ho ! I be a desp’rate one, be I, Mr. Tuff?”
grinned the transport. “Well, I know I be—an’
’ere’s to keep up th’ k’racter.” With a mighty
cuff he struck Tuff to the ground. “ There Super,
Mac-wot’s-yer-name, give me my five-hunderd
lashes, an’ ’a done wi’ it, as I said a-fore ! ”
The ex-private secretary to noble Sir John
Franklin answered the appeal. He stepped into
the school-room, and called to the transport to
follow him. Some of the officials who had looked
on the incident just described would have entered
likewise, but the Captain quietly waved them

Captain Maconochie might be a crankbut,
certainly, he was no coward.
* * *
As he walked in, Tracey had, obeying the
mechanical instinct which, in spite of himself, the
System had implanted in his nature, took his cap
off, Then he recalled the act. He was not going
to submit as “ a softy ” to the new super. Not he.
He replaced the cap defiantly as he faced the
Captain. However, the Superintendent gave no
indication that he was aware of the insult. Bun-
bury would have chained Tracey down for the

“My man !” said Maconochie. I wish to have

Maconochie measured almost word he
uttered in a transport s presence. Constant
dropping of water wears away a stone. The
habitual use of forms of respect to any even the
most hardened-prisoner will insensibly by wear-

nature room for play.” That was what he used

II hat. Lifer Tracey could not believe his

corrigible’record—sir!”
“Look, sir—” Maconochie began again.
“None o’ that foolin’. Yer not a-goin’ to make
a softy o’ me, 1 tell yer !” Tracey raised his hand
threateningly, as though in defence of that precious
possession—his reputation for eminence in evil,
“ I be’nt no parson’s or Super.’s pet!”
“I am sure of it,” rejoined the Captain. “But
you are a man, and not a devil—l am sure of that
as well!”
A second of wits’-gathering silence. Then
“ No, I be’n’t no man- a devil I am, or th’
Systuui hasn’t done its work !” He laughed, with
the reckless, sardonic laughter of the hopeless,
“No, you are a man, and no devil! And
because you are a man, obey one of the first
instincts of manhood, and that is to behave with
respect to your just and legal superiors.”
“ Just—legal!” Again the laugh—and then a
storm of mad, tumultuous speech. “ Just is them
wot justice do—legal is as legal act ! Be’n’t truth
’bove all things ? Cana man be a man if he’s a
liar? Be’n’t a liar alius a coward? An’ no
coward’s a man ? An’ ’udn’t I lie ef I paid respec’
w’ere respec’be’n’t doo? An’ is respec’ doo to
nnybody unner th’ Systum, for wot cove is there
unner th’ Systnm as acts justly, as acts legally ?
Answer me that, 01’ Kmg-o’-th’-lags !”
“ Poor man, poor man !” exclaimed Maconochie,
and p ac i n g forward, he held out his hand. Tracey
struck it aside passionately
' “Yer’ve left ‘ me nothin’ but-oh, nothin’,
nothin’but hate—an’now yer give me your pity.
To the devil who made yer an’ th’ Systum ! ” He
leapt tigrishly on the Superintendent—and was
felled by a blow. There was nothing effeminate
about Maconochie’s muscles or his nerves, if there
rwasjusta suspicion of that quality attached to
his judgment. No. 33-149 went down, and for a
minute stayed down, dazed.
No word was spoken till Tracey, with eye-balla
glaring redly instead of whitely, drew himself up
t 0 a sitting posture.
“Didn’t!—say as the nubblin’cheat’ud end it
soon ? B y th’ Lord, sir, I thank yer !”
Maconochie had faced a mutiny from a quarter-
deck ; had, as a lad, confronted, with only a toy-
dirk in hand, a howling circle of barbaric moan-
tam tribesmen thirsty for his blood ; and yet he
was wont to say, never did human look appal him
as ffiat transport s glare of gratitude,
( H s death—this—you know, my man.
, Death— tis—an welcome . It was a hyaena
s h rie k made articulate.
* * *
My friend, let me help you up !” Again
Captain Maconochie stretched forth his hand.
Tracey looked up. “ D’yer mean it ?” The
softening of the words passed in the next instant
f rom } ds f ace and tone. “ Mean it!” he continued,
“ 0 > cours e yer mean it! Be’n’t it yer dooty to
, and me to th > g allows ...
Maconochie pressed down his hand-lower-
lower still—till it touched Tracey’s shoulder,
“Tracey !” he whispered, “Tracey! it is my
du ty to save you from—the gallows. That is
w hy lam here. Tracey—let us be friends !”
No . 3 3-149-his agony wried his mouth as he
spoke—simply repeated “Friends!” and then
bent , lis head upon his sca rred hands. The up-
beaval of his universe had come, for, from the
vortex D f hell had sprung a voice-an official voice
—that had uttered a kindly word. Kind words
he had sometimes had before, but they were from
clergymen ; and the gospellers were bound to say
something kind sometimes to earn their stipends.
While a man might draw twenty breaths
he sat so, challenging his consciousness whether or
no his world was altered. And then, from the core
of the beast-nature with which the System had
superseded that granted unto him by his Creator
—John Price said once “there were doubts as to
the Creator, but there were none as to the System ”
—he spumed once more a torrent of volcanic
hatred and suspicion.
“ It’s all a trap; yer think I be a double
softy to be taken in so? Yer playin’ the forgivin’
to get time to call the guards, ’cos yer afraid I was
a-goin’ to kill yer ! O, yer devil!”
He stood with parching lips and clenched hands,
and the knots bulged on his temples as when a
gymnast braces himself for a feat that will cause
the heavens to resound either with Homeric
plaudits or with his death-scream. Had he sprung,
the help of soldier-guard would have come too late
for the Super. But —
“Stay!” cried Maconochie. “What proof do
you demand that I am not the monster you fancy
me ? How can I show you my truth—that I mean
to be as true a friend to every man here who will
allow me as I can with God’s help be —how can I
do this ?”
The appeal held Tracey in spite of himself.
Scarcely knowing what he did, he gazed through
the one window-space (unglazed) of the hut.
Crossing the parade-ground leisurely were a lady
and a maid-servant. A soldier followed them ten
paces away. He pointed to them, and cried,
hoarsely:—
“ Is that your wife ? ”
Maconochie’s look followed the pointing-finger.
“Yes ! ” he replied.
“ Then, I’ll believe yer if—you—trust—her—
your wife, I mean—in this room alone wi’ me for
five minutes by your watch.”
The Superintendent’s face grew haggard in the
tremendousness of the ordeal. But his soul
answered to the test.
He went out —and said a few words to his wife.
She went in to the room, all unknowing, but
believing utterly in her husband’s wisdom.
* # #
Maconochie lost, in the next moment, his
composure. He thrust'his watch into the hand of
the soldier escorting the lady, and in the same
action, seized the man’s musket.
“In four minutes—four minutes and a-half—
call ‘Time!’” he exclaimed, while he himself
cocked the musket —and waited. So strained was
his hearing to catch any sound from the room,
that though but five yards distant from the
group of officers, he did not hear Mr. Commissary
Shanks say :
“ Well —I’m damned ! Maconochie’s just offer-
ing a bounty for crime!”
* * •
He did not hear that, or indeed ought
else, till the soldier cried, “ Time !” loudly. Then
he started forward, but the appearance of his wife
on the step momentarily arrested his rush. With
the musket still in his hand, he ran to meet her.
She motioned with her head. Through the door-
way he saw—and heard —Tracey. The transport
lay huddled, his head on the floor, his arms out-
stretched in the abandon of despair—or, remorse.
And he was sobbing tearless sobs.
• * *
Not all at once did Tracey, after that
supreme instance of trust, come back to the path
of well-doing. His fight, however, against his past
was strenuous.
It was in February that Cantain Maconochie
trusted him. And till the 23rd October of the
year following, he kept almost a clear record.
“View ” sentences had ceased entirely, and to the
extreme disgust of the gentlemen possessing a
more extensive acquaintance with the System
than Captain Maconochie, Mr. Tobias Tracey
declined to preserve by frequent attendances at
court his average of “ offences needing formal
trial.”
Mr. D. A. C. G. Shanks, on 23rd October,
applied to the Superintendent for a trusty man tu
handle some stores.
And Mr. D. A. C. G. Shanks, being in an un-
bending, not to say affable mood, condescended ta
pass a pleasant word with the trusty man com
missioned by the Super, to wait upon his Oommia-
saryship.
“ You’re Tracey ? ”
“ Yes, sir ! ” with a salute.
Mr. Shanks laughed. “You’re the man tha
Super, trusted the day after he took charge-
trusted his wife with ? ”
There was a conscious pride in Tracey’s voice as
he replied, “ He did, sir !”
“ Why, you donkey, he held a cocked musket in
his hands all the time ! Fine lot of trust in that
wasn’t there ? ”
Convict Tracey was stunned. “Are yer a-
speakin’ o’ th’ truth, sir ? ”
With a brutal oath, Mr. Deputy-Assistant-
Commissary-General Shanks affirmed he was.
* * *
That same evening Tracey broke into Mrs.
Whologhan’s store with a crowbar.

THU BEALE PATENT
HIGH ARM SEWING MACHINES
Have, by their continued merits and the general
satisfaction they have invariably given, won the
first place in public esteem, and are on all sides
admitted to be the best and most useful sewing
machines in the market.
FOR USE IN THE HOUSEHOLD
The Beale Machine presents innumerable advant-
ages. The mechanism and the adjustments are
perfect, while the working is most simple; and
the addition of all the most recent inventions and
improvements renders the manipulation of every-
thing connected with the machine a matter of
perfect ease. The result of this is, that with
ordinary care the machine will last for many
years, nothing can get out of order, and no time
is wasted in tiresome meddling with the parts.
It is a servant that will never grumble, never
rebel, never want nights out, willing even on
washing days. It only asks for a careful cleaning
now and then, a snug, dry corner and a little
genuine sewing machine oil. Any other oil will
make it sick, and dirt of all kind it abhors.
The Self-threading- Shuttle, of solid steel,
saves the trouble of threading by the end of the
cotton. It has an excellent tension, is strong and
simple.
The Bobbin Winder is a marvel of in-
genuity, winding and guiding the cotton by
itself, as evenly as upon the reel whence it came,
and stopping of its own accord when the bobbin
is full.
For all kinds of family sewing and fancy the
Beale Machine has the greatest capacity.
The Improved High Arm. A great de-
sideratum with ladies has been the raising of the
arm of the machine, so as to allow the goods to be
sewn freely to pass underneath. This had to be
accomplished without increasing the weight of the
machine. In fact, the Beale runs with wonderful
ease and rapidity. It is almost silent, all clicking
sounds having been obviated. As is well known,
a good test of the finish of any mechanism is its
silence when in rapid motion.
With the Wonderful Beale Machine,
whether for hand or foot use, you can hem any
width, make double seams or fells, braid stitch
with chenille, Berlin wool, or gold cord, and such
like fancy work; you may make a pretty lace
stitch, tuck ever so finely, bind dresses, coats, or
carpets ; sew on trimmings without guiding, make
frilling, ruffling, or ruching; quilt, weave your
own braid with any combination of colours and
stitch it on at the same time; make thick, woollen
door-mats, embroidered antimacassars, window
curtains,-and so forth ; you can easily and rapidly
perform the fashionable shirring or gathering so
popular for sleeves, bodies, or skirts of dresses
with an appliance supplied gratis ; lay the small
cords in hoods and stays ;do large cording; gather
with or without a band, and generally do all
kinds of straight sewing upon any material what-
ever. You can darn upon any woven goods, and
do crewel-work. The Beale Patent will pass
from eight folds of tweed or twenty of calico to
two of thinnest muslin, or even mosquito-net or
tulle, without the operator changing needle,
cotton, stitch, or tension, and the fine material
will not be gathered in the slightest. You may
make button and eyelet-holes, do over-casting,
make herring-bone stitch upon any materials.
This work is absolutely unique, and can only be
accomplished upon the Beale Patent Machine.
Any of these performances will be shown at Gin-
shops at any time. The Beale Patent makes
a lock-stitch, which cannot ravel, yet by a momen-
tary alteration the sewing can be done so as to
rip from end to end, if desired. This peculiarity
is sometimes useful when children’s clothes are
intended to be altered.
Nickel-plating 1 . —In our climate the exposed
parts of the machine, and the applicances, need
to be protected from rust. We have, at consider-
able expense, added a handsomely nickeled wheel,
nickeled plates, screws, appliances, treadle-bar
and cones. Heightening the appearance, these
improvements also tend largely to preserve the
parts. This plating is imperishable.
A special feature of The Beale Machine is
that
ALL THE CABINET WORK
is made at our factory in Sydney from registered
designs, and of the choicest
AUSTRALIAN WOODS,
some of which are without doubt the most beauti-
ful in the world. Suitability to the climate is
thus insured, and we are enabled to offer a greater
variety of styles, most handsome in design and
conveniently fitted for all the requirements of a
lady’s work-table.
These machines can be inspected at any time at
our principal show-rooms in Sydney, or at any of
our suburban and country branch shops, or from
travellers who are continually visiting all parts.
All the machines may be had upon easy time-
payment, or for cash less three shillings in the
pound.

Brickfield Hill .
A Run on a Bank.
The daily press has stated that the run on the
Savings Bank of N.S.W. was probably the only
occasion on record where a run on a bank was
started for no apparent reason. However, it
hapoens that an even worse and yet more
silty and purposeless panic springing from ascer-
tained causes took place once in London, and
caused a bigger run on a bigger bank for less
reasons.
• • •
Away back in the gold-digging days a party of
Maorilanders “made a big rise” and started for
England. There were ten of them, seven men and
three women; and they had a letter of credit on
the Bank of N.Z.’s London agents for some =£3ooo
or <£4ooo. They got to the big smoke all right,
and presented their letter of credit, which was duty
cashed. Then they wanted to divide the money
and open separate accounts for their shares. It
so happened that the bank which then represented
the Bank of N.Z. in London did not handle the
latter class of business, and the clerks told the
miners so, at the same time recommend-
ing them to another place a few doors off
—the London and County Bank. This is
one of the most solid institutions in London, a
concern alongside of which the Savings Bank of
N.S, Wales is as a farthing candle to a bush fire.
The miners accepted the advice, walked down the
street to the office of the London and County, and
then pulled up outside, and had a good look at
the place ere going in with their money.
• * #
The spectacle of ten odd-looking people, the
men bearded and burned, the women garishly
dressed, and one and all gaping steadfastly at the
Bank, soon attracted a crowd. It was in
one of the busiest London streets—Lombard-
street. The Maorilanders gazed placidly on,
making up their slow minds whether or
not they Would enter. Meanwhile, the
crowd gathered and surged, all gaping up at
the bank. Questions were passed round the
crowd —“What is it?” “What’s this all about?”
And then someone, at a loss for an answer, said,
“Oh, there’s a run on the bank.” Soon gathered
a seething, surging, howling mob of depositors
outside that bank, and outside everyone of its
branches all over London, fighting to get in. All
day long and all night, and all next day, the people
fought for places in the scrimmage. The bank
officials had barrowloads of sovereigns wheeled out
of the vaults into the banking-rooms, but nothing
would stay the tumult. All sorts of extrava-
gant rumours were afloat as to the cause
of the run. The manager had bolted, the
bank had lost heavily in foreign stock, the
directors had committed suicide, the clerks had
embezzled all the money—there were all sorts
of reasons for the panic. But no one ever thought
of the handful of New Zealanders who deposited
their money and retired, and when the scare
started came back to look after it. They thought
they had struck hideous luck for their bank to be
rushed just as their coin was in—it was clearly a
plot on the part of the Londoners to “ do ” them
out of their money. They joined the crowd, got
their assets back, and not one of them would
afterwards have the slightest faith in any London
institution. The Bank of England might be all
very well, but give them the Bank of New Zealand
for their money.
* « *
Talking of bank rushes, a rather curious inci-
dent occurred in Sydney a few years ago. It was
mooted that a certain bank was in difficulties; an
old man and his wife who had nearly .£5OOO there
rushed off to draw the money. They took it in
notes, tens, twenties, and fifties, after the
manner of Charles Reade’s prudent depositor
in his tale “Hard Cash,” and for a fort-
night or more they guarded this treasure in the
house, fully expecting the bank to break, and
chuckling to think how they had protected them-
selves. One of them used to sit up at night to
watch the treasure while the other slept. Every
step near the house throw them into deadly fear
of robbers. At last they got so tired of it that
they took the lot to a well-known Sydney solicitor
and asked him to invest it for them in some way
—anyhow, so that they shouldn’t have it
on their minds. He explained to them that
if the bank had burst the notes would
have been very little better than the original
deposit receipt, and then he redeposited the lot
with the same bank. They had nearly billed
themselves with worry over paper that would
have been worthless had the bank collapsed,
• * *
Towards the end of the N.S.W. Sayings Bank
scare the bank was paying with sovereigns minted
in 1892. Which looks as if the Mint had been put
into requisition to turn out coin for the occasion.
The Evidence Amendment Act, whereby,
in more than one Australian province, a prisoner
is now permitted to testify on his own behalf,
applies to cases where he, at inception, pleads
guilty. The reason of this is obvious : he might
wish to offer evidence of extenuating circum-
stances.
General Booth announces that the only
area for his over-sea colonies, as yet, is South
Africa. This is what people said before he
started, and his whole “Darkest England” scheme
had its germ in some degree of success achieved
by Arnold White, with Viscountess Ossington’s
money, settling two small colonies of London
paupers in Africa.

tn lankeeland there is a “Rainmaking
Co., Limited.” The crux of its difficulties is—
how to prevent the rain they “ make ” from falling
upon the lands of non-subscribers. As soon as a
