_ ,s Snaky George ’ Jaynes has, we read,
IfiO acres in Tennessee, lvhe,re he has a preserve of
10,000 rattlesnakes! He kills 2000 a year, atld
makes large profit out of their oil. The stench of
the place is about the most hellish on earth, and
the rattling that always goes on is like that of the
valley of dry bones.
The Ailora (Q.) girls the other day rigged
up an effigy of a married man who had been
playing at brighamy. The police seized the
dummy, but the girls made another instanter and
their cavaliers rolled up and burned it ill fine
style. Then they rushed for the veritable Turk
in his hotel with tar and feathers in view, but he
wisely struck a bee-line for Greenmount.
At a township on the Lachlan (N.S.W.),
the other day, a young fellow died, and a relative
set out to the next town to procure a coffin. The
messenger was rather long away, and the hot
weather necessitated summary burial. A mes-
senger was sent out to find the relative, which he
did next morning—under a tree, drunk. The
Gdffin Was on the roadside hard by, and, the cart
Which conveyed it had broken down. The pair
got home ; leaving tile coffin—Which has beert
lyiilg there eVet since in frill view of the passing
teams and coaches.
A FRAGMENT
Across the creek the camp-fires blaze.
And weird the curlew cries,
While blood-red gleam the sums last rays
“ Peace,’* for the Icing day dies;
Hard by the reefs the tents gleam white,
Like peaks all crowned with snow,
Beneath the pale-faced moon's cold light,
“Kest,” now the night winds blow.
Across the creek the morning breaks,
And gilds both ridge and plain,
From slumber now the reef awakes,
“ Toil,” for *tis day again.
Lane Cove, 21/11/91.
A. C. FONTAINE.
WRECKED ON THE GOODWINS.
Erasmus Darwin, grandfather of the famous
Charles Darwin, the naturalist, inscribed upon his
seal the significant words, “ Omnia ex conchis” —
all things arise from oysters. But from what do
oysters arise? What is the origin of matter?
Ah! nobody knows. Yet one thing is getting
plainer every day. Let this man's story show.
He goes on to say : “ On the 17th of December,
1872, whilst acting as ship’s carpenter on board
the steamship Sorrento, I was wrecked on the
Goodwin Sands. The shock to my system was so
great as to produce an effect I never felt before.
I fell into a low, weak state, and was seized with
giddiness and a sinking, fainting feeling. I had a
bad taste in the mouth, weight at the chest, and
great pain after eating, and ray face would burn
like fire. I had difficulty in breathing and palpi-
tation, and often a pain seemed to run from the
heart through to my shoulder. I had a kind of
rumbling all over me, and a choking sensation at
the throat, with a rush of blood to the head.
“I slept very badly, and after a time I got so
nervous and weak I was afraid to go about, and
would sit in a chair quite powerless. I saw a
doctor, who treated me for a time, and then sent
me to a hospital in Rattlebone Place, London,
where I was under treatment for three months,
but got no better. I then put myself under a
local doctor, who said I was suffering from
nervous debility. He patched me up for a little
while, but said to my wife, ‘Your husband is in a
critical condition, and may die at any time.
“ However, I got to work for a short time, and
then was as bad as ever. I lingered on in this
way for years—now a little better, now worse, but
never well.
“ About 1880, whilst working at Messrs. West-
wood and Bailey’s, Millwall, a mate who saw my
sufferings told me of a medicine called Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup, and he brought me a
bottle. After taking the first bottle I found my
food agreed with me, and I began to improve.
All nervous feeling gradually left me, and by per-
severing with the medicine I was soon cured.
From that time to this I have been a strong man,
and done harder work than ever in my life before.
I have since kept Mother Seigel’s Syrup in the
house, and my wife, who was a gre'at sufferer
from rheumatism and dropsy, has found more
relief from it than from anything else. If any of
my family ever ail anything, a few doses soon sets
them right.”
The statement from which the foregoing is an
extract was made by Mr. William Hill, ship’s
carpenter, 108, St. Anne’s Road, London, E.
Now, what is the thing which we said is getting
plainer every day ? Why this : that almost an
infinite number of different results arise from a
single cause. Like the majority of people in all
ranks ot life, Mr. Hill had in his digestive organs
the seeds —or what women, when they buy dress
goods, call “ the makings of dyspepsia. Up to
the winter of 1872 it may not have troubled him
much, but the excitement and exposure of the
shipwreck was more than the system could bear.
We must remember, too, that all shocks to the
mind or body, or to both, act directly on the
stomach. Who can eat when under the influence
of a great worry or a great sorrow ? And if this
worry or sorrow continues long, it always upsets
the digestion, permanently impairs or destroys
the appetite, and thus brings on an apparently
incurable dyspepsia and nervous debility. Why,
gracious goodness ! there are millions of women,
and lots of men, crawling about under this thing
in England every day in the year. And it is to
them we talking. They are half dead, and
we want tie m (■> come to life, and get some good
out of their existence. Whatever the ailment is
called, you may be sure indigestion and dyspepsia
is at the bottom of it, as in Mr. Hill’s case, and
what cured him will cure you.

ment Co.. Limited, 30!) Pitt street, Sydney.
Superintendent Toohey, about to be super-
annuated from the Victorian fooroe, made a
mysterious ass of himself on more than one occa-
sioh; The last time he Came prominently before
the public was .tome fitc of silt years ago* inline*
tlifttely after Ids appointment to suprerrle coni-
mand in the Geelong district. There had been
the murder of a farmer named Beach, at Pettavel,
for which crime Mrs. Beach and a hired man,
sleeping on the premises, were in turn arrested on
suspicion, and discharged for want of any evidence
to connect them with the tragedy. Toohey no
sooner took over his new billet than he cudgelled
his odd-shaped head to find fresh grounds for
suspicion against file feamC. pedplfi: After Working
up a caricature of a “case,” the irrepressible Super-
intendent procured a warrant and proceeded to put
it into execution. Widow Beach and her husband’s
former employe (who had married a wife in the
meantime) were always available for capture,
because they both lived at Pettavel as before, and
Toohey might have whistled them up to him any
time o’ day. Ho preferred, however, to set forth
in the dead of night, armed with a lantern, a pair
of handcuffs and a sinister smile, and swoop down
upon the male Siispect fig 1)0 lay asleep. The poor
devil had to dress himself and be faken off id
chokee by the light of the lantern and moon.
When Toohey’s alleged “case” turned out to be
the merest bosh and was dismissed off hand by
the magistrates the victim brought an action
against his eapturer for playing the fool, but got
no further satisfaction than the payment or his
own costs. The only real fresh evidence against
him, by the way, was that his hair had begun to
turn white since the murder. They might as
well have arrested Toohey, whose hair had been
white before.

The Pall Mall Gazette gives an instance
of a lady contracting cancer in the lip through
partaking of communion from a chalice used by
another communicant suffering from that disease
in the tongue; and a doctor follows up with ex-
amples of consumption, diphtheria, and other un-
clean disorders fiohtracted in the same way. Still
there is no need for departing from the old-
established custom. A special prayer could bo
devised by the Archbishop of Canterbury to
counteract the danger to health (we are here re-
minded that the Tasmanian Pastor Soltan’s
prayer for earthquakes has been answered); and it
would be a pity to abolish a rite associated with
so many touching lessons of humility and brother-
hood. The most hardened infidel, glancing at the
fcontefits bf even a glass of water just sipped by a
score or so of people vtliO had been 'chewing dry
bread, would be forced to admit that there was
something in the ceremony.

The Maori is a ’cute land-dealer. His
customary form of conveyance doesn’t say that he
transfers the land, but, that he has “ wept over
and bid farewell to this place of his ancestors,
with its rivers, branches, trees, hetbftge,- its
inaccessible places, and its available place®, afia
its inferior places, and all things whether on the
surface of the earth or underneath.” This looks
pretty comprehensive, but then the right to weep
over and bid farewell to a place belongs practically
to any member of the tribe who feels an attach-
ment to the spot, and after the actual and visible
proprietor has been bought out forty or fifty more
Weepers are liable to come along, and they must
all be Solaced With hard cash, else they will
lament till further orders. Thefi, if a Iftgoofi runs
dry the original Maori will send ih a fresh hill for
the ground it occupied, because he sold the 'vvatfet'
but then the water is gone, and he didn’t sell the
dry land which lias come instead of it. Very often
a Maori will sell land according to the above
formula, and then keep on roosting there just the
same, on the ground that there was nothing in the
deed about his not continuing to reside on the
property ; and if he is induced to shift by a cash
consideration or otherwise he will come back and
habitually walk over the ground, because there is
nothing about walking in the deed, and this means
more cash—orelsealawsuic. Arid When the Walking
privilege is settled he will come bafcli sotofe Inofe
and fish for cels in the lagoon, on the ground that
eels weren’t specified in the conveyance ; and alter
this last point has been squared up. and the rights
of living on the property, and walking over it, and
fishing on it are all separately bought and paid
for, he will want to make a fresh sale of his rights
to lean Up against the outside of the fence. I here
is no lawyer who can compare with the old,
veteran Maori for picking holes ill a eOfiVeyahce.
Nobody-ever seems to get altogether rid of him.
Even when his right to lean up against the fence
has been duly bought off him the chances are that
he will send in a bill for the sunshine and the
moonshine, on the ground that they weren’t
mentioned in the conveyance and that it was an
entirely dark property which he sold.
